Date: Thu, 01 Jul 93 10:29:24 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: More Splitfire complaining

*** Resending note of 07/01/93 10:23
FROM: Scott Jessen
To: SMTP    --HOVMB

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
SUBJECT: More Splitfire complaining

===> This is a copy of an article I posted on rec.autos.tech... <===

Okay, so I didn't get it all off my chest last time (re: 6/29 posting)...

Something I forgot to mention was the difficulty in replacing the Splitfires
because of their POOR QUALITY sealing washers.  You see, Splitfire
gives you washers that are too big, versus other manufacturers that the
crush washer fits snugly once it's been compressed.  EVERY one of the
Splitfire washers came off when I was removing the plug.  If you have a
motor that the plug hole is easily accessed -- this is a minor hassle.

Mine is an old Porsche 911, where the plug hole is 6 inches in and
impossible to see or feel.  Compounding my problem, I didn't notice
the washers weren't on the plugs until I was on the fifth one I was
changing!  The washers don't just fall away either, some stayed in place
(new plug ends up slightly recessed in the head), one wedged itself partway
under the new plug's washer (ruining it). 3 1/2 hours and lots of creative work
with magnets later....  The car with NGKs is happy again.  The spark plug
moral: if it aint broke, don't screw with it!

A hard lesson to learn a few days before a race!

Another note:  on close inspection, the Splitfires don't appear to have
all had spark going to both sides of their split ground prong.  I doubt
that the pretty perfect picture they show of their plug firing in their ads
is what actually occurs in your engine (or at least my engine).

'Nuff said...


As usual: these opinions are my own............

'''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''''

Date: Thu, 1 Jul 93 15:19:31 EDT
From: lupienj@wal.hp.com (John Lupien)
Subject: Re: More Splitfire complaining

> Another note:  on close inspection, the Splitfires don't appear to have
> all had spark going to both sides of their split ground prong.  I doubt
> that the pretty perfect picture they show of their plug firing in their ads
> is what actually occurs in your engine (or at least my engine).

Any given spark is only going to go to one prong, anyway.
Once there's a plasma path from the center electrode to one
of the prongs, that is the lowest resistance path for further
current to flow: it won't go anywhere else.


- --

John R. Lupien
lupienj@wal.hp.com

Date: Thu, 1 Jul 93 13:54:53 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Splitfires

Splitfires.

Hey Scott, how you guys doin' in Texas? I miss seeing you and Sally at 
the Zone 7 events.

I've been using them in my 944 for over a year with good results, the 
gasket issue extends to other japanese brands also. I replaced mine with
some Bosch gaskets, my results are about a 4 hp improvement and a 
similar improvement in torque. One thing, make sure the plugs are 
torqued to at least 25 ft-lbs with NO anti-sieze compound on the threads
to prevent backing out. if the threads on your Porsche sparkplug holes 
seem tight, use a thread chaser to clean them before installing the next
 set of plugs.

Snap-on, Craftsman and others sell this tool. 

By the way, recheck all gaps for the Splitfires (and all new plugs) 
prior to installation.

My own feeling is that the Splitfires will work better in a wedge-type
head (944, 924, 928, 914 and 912E 2 valve only) because of the partially
 obscured area of a conventional plug. A herron type head, 911 type, 
sees less improvement unless you have emissions-type pistons. I have 
dyno results for the 944 on hand.

Jim Pasha

pasha@hal.com

Diablo Region PCA

July 1993

Monthly Notices for the Porschephiles mailing list. 
PLEASE READ THIS WHOLE MESSAGE!!

The big news this month: Porschephiles is famous! In addition to 
write-ups in several PCA newsletters, we are also featured in this
months edition of Excellence magazine, in an article called "I Drive
the Porsche Electric". So, go out and impress your friends!

A big welcome to the new members from Golden Gate, who have responded
to the writeup in their newsletter. And could someone out there talk
to your editor and get him to mail me a copy?

A FAQ is supposedly under preparation. 

Archives are available via FTP, compressed, since 4/90. There are no
older archives available, which is perhaps unfortunate. Oh well.

Remember, while it seems like a good idea when you're first starting out
on the list to know what has gone on before, there are OVER 7 MEG of 
archives. DO NOT TRY TO FTP THEM ALL AT ONE TIME. Period. And PLEASE DO 
NOT FTP between 7:00 AM and 7:00 PM CST, or I'll have to take access away. 
We do have to get *SOME* work done around here, you know...  Also DO NOT 
ask me to mail you archives, since it's just too much work. Thank you.

NOTE: To FTP the compressed archives, you will have to use BINARY mode in FTP. 
Then on receipt, you need to use the UNIX "uncompress" program, or equivalent,
to restore to textual format.

SECOND NOTE: if you really, really need to get a full set of archives, 
we have a business service where we could write you a tape for a fee.
Let me know if you really, really need that.

I still edit the newsletter for the Lone Star Region of the PCA, and
still plan to put some of the articles on-line for FTP in 
/pub/pca/newsletters/lone-star. If you edit a newsletter or would like to just i
type in articles from your newsletter, you can upload them into the directory
/pub/pca/newsletters/UPLOAD.

+++++++++++

this message!

Have your Visa/MC number *AND* your VIN number(s) handy, if you own a car.
Or they'll send you an application which you can mail in with your check.

This list is still being run as a mail reflector, which sends incoming
mail to the whole list at once. The participants include people with a 
variety of backgrounds, some technical, some race/autocross/performance 
drivers, some owners, some just admirers.  

To send a message to the whole list, mail to

		porschephiles@tta.com

To send mail regarding distribution problems, or other administrative issues,
send mail to

		porschephiles-request@tta.com

If you have a friend who would like to get on this list, or if you feel
moved to tell other people about it, ONLY GIVE OUT THE "-request" address.

The list is also available as a digest, where you get a compilataion of
the day's messages once per day, rather than getting all of the individual
messages. This seems to be a win for people with slow mail links or who
are just too busy to stay current. If you want to convert to (or from)
the digest, send mail to the porschephiles-request address above.

PLEASE NOTE A FEW ITEMS:

1) There are a lot of  members on the list. It's small enough to be effective,
   and large enough to have a number of diverse opinions and sets of
   experience. Remember that before you decide to not ask a question, or
   if you get flamed for asking an "obvious" question. Diversity -- it's
   a *feature*....

2) The list is maintained irregularly. If I'm in town or can reach the
   host system from wherever I am and I have the time to deal with it,
   I *try* to handle changes on a real-time basis. However, as a consultant
   I travel extensively and from time to time, changes will be batched
   on a weekly basis.

3) ONLY send administrative mail to the "porschephiles-request"
   address, or to me directly. And, you can also reach me by phone 
   at (800) 582-6892.

4) In the same vein, if you use a "pure" System V mail system, please pay
   attention to your replies. The default will send the reply to 
   the "porschephiles-request" address, as it is the UUCP "From "
   address (first actual line of the mail header). 

   PLEASE BE AWARE THAT I TRASH SUCH MIS-ROUTED MAIL!!!

5) If your mailer starts screwing up *you will be dropped from the list*
   Should you notice that you aren't getting the list any more, send me
   mail and I'll figure out why not...

Enjoy the list! If I can be of assistance, please don't hesitate to drop
me a note.

Regards From Your Moderator,

Stanley P. Hanks
Principal Scientist,                            Technology Transfer Associates

Date: Fri, 2 Jul 93 14:24:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: Spinning Porsches.....it can be used as an advantage!!!

TO: autox@autox.team.net,
    INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48

.......................................................................

In reference to the question about spinning P Cars..

Having driven Porsches for over 15 years, I have (many times!) experienced what
you described!  Early 911's (especially pre-'69 SWB cars) exhibit what is
called 'trailing throttle oversteer (spinning when you lift the throttle as
you enter a turn!!!). This a very characteristic of 911's that are:

1. Not set up for autocrossing (adjustable shocks, large front bar, etc)
2. Not SMOOTHLY transitioned into a corner under braking.
3. Going WAY TO FAST into a Corner (not hope unless you have a new C2 with
   ABS!!!).

Generally, this is a problem with 911's .  It just takes lots of practice
setting the car up properly before (!) you enter a corner.  I typically
try to brake in a straight line before the corner to evenly load the suspension
and transition into the corner underpower to control the attitude of the
car.

BUT.... this can be REALLY USEFUL!!!  In an autocross course, rotation around a
corner can be the ticket to VERY QUICK times, especially if your car
has LSD!!!  The ability of a rear engined car to 'rotate' easily keep us in
the running with more sophisticated machinery (Mazda RX7 TT's for instance).
We can enter a corner at speed, set the car up, rotate (not spin) at the apex,
ans accelerate like H*LL out of the corner.  This, however take LOTS of
experience (which I still have not mastered !) to pull off, but when you do
the other cars in your class better watch out (IMHO-not flame intended
to all the other neat cars in AS/ASP!!)  I must admit that you need to squeeze
the brakes as you set the car up for a corner- DO NOT SLAM (!!!) on the brakes,
because the instantanious weight transfer to the front REALLY un-balances
a 911 and cause you to spin- before you can say 'Oh Sh*t...'!!

IF you need additional info, especially on car setup/shock settings and the like

please feel free to call me (208-396-5047 W) so I can be of help to
a fellow P Driver (going extinct due to the 'new trick' cars in AS ! HA)

G'day,


MR Wizard

Date: Sat, 3 Jul 93 03:22 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Front end noise in '86 Carrera: Help!

Jim -

Check the front struts for play.  With the wheel off on a given side,
push and pull the strut and see whether the shock piston cocks slightly
from side to side.  I've seen this in 911's and 944's, and it can be
misdiagnosed as a loose wheel bearing, and can lead to a tooting or
"beer bottle" noise.

You don't mention whether your car is dead stock or not.  If not, what
changes?  Any chance of a brake duct hose rubbing against the wheel or
tire?

Kevin

Porschephiles #440                         Sun Jul 4 23:09:31 CDT 1993
Date: Sun, 4 Jul 93 03:30 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Worn 911 clutch?

Ben Evans asks...

>How does one tell if the clutch on a 911 ( `88 Carrera, 43k miles)
>is wearing out? I don't have a workshop so I have no facility
>for getting under the car or working on it :-( All I can do
>is look for symptoms.

>My clutch appears to be fully functional, even under extreme
>conditions I can't make it slip while engaged, but my dealer 
>says it is `lifting high' and requires replacement soon. 

>Do I have the clutch replaced now or wait until a problem occurs?

Sorry for not replying sooner.  Unfortunately, even th 87-forward 911
hyrdraulic clutches lack the nice inspection port that 944's have.  Said
port lets you check both release fork travel (indicating proper function),
and friction disk wear.

Not clear to me whether you feel you have a problem.  You should be able
to slip the clutch as a matter of normal use.  If it's engaging "high",
make sure the free play at the pedal is properly adjusted.  If not, and
the rod is too far extended even when trhe pedal is released, you will
never be fully off the clutch, and it will be as though you were always
riding the clutch.

The free play adjustment is quite easy, and I would assume your dealer can
or has already done it for you.

Can't say whether to replace it now.  You need to figure out whether there
is a problem.

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Sun, 4 Jul 93 03:22 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Porsche Dealer Service

>I just had the dealer (The Sewickley Car Store, Pittsburgh, PA) change the
>clutch on my '87 951. Total cost of about $1,900 (!!!!). Looking at the
>bill there is a $110 charge for "drilling out broken bolts in wastegate
>unit". The mechanic told me that two bolts broke off when he was trying to
>remove the wastegate as part of the clutch change. My question is, do I
>have to pay for what they brake ?

Well, this really boils down to a few subquestions.  First would be whether
it is reasonable to expect exhaust bolts/studs to freeze and break during
r&r.  If the answer is no, then the mechanic messed up and it might not be
unreasonable to expect them to eat the cost.

If the answer is yes, then you have another question.  If exhaust bolts
sometime break during work, should you build the cost of their repair
into a flat rate bill, or should you charge only customers who are
unlucky enough to have this happen to them?

My opinion and experience is that exhaust bolts and studs do from time to
time seize and break, through no fault of the mechanic.  I suspect that
a stressed piece like a wastegate might be more prone to this sort of
problem, but I don't drive or work on turbos.  You should also know that
the book time for a 951 clutch is huge, like 20 hours or so.  A good
mechanic can do it in less time and charge accordingly.  I personally would
much rather pay for what time and materials it takes to do a job, rather
than book.  If I get unlucky, hey, shit happens.

Given the dealer cost of a clutch and expected labor time, I think that
$1900 is not an unfair price.  It's a bitch of a job, but that's not
Sewickley's fault.

Kevin

Date: Sat, 3 Jul 1993 23:37:33 -0400 (EDT)
From: Tony Matt <tonym@world.std.com>
Subject: Wheels/Bearings/Beer Bottles


I'd like some assistance with the following three items:

1. Wheels

I've been researching my options for a set of autox wheels to use
on my 924S.  Apparently the 7x15 phone dials from the '87 944
were an option on the 924 (rears only).  This leads me to wonder 
whether other 7x15 wheels (Fuchs, cookie cutters) would fit too.
What is the offset on those phone dials, and are there any other
styles with the same (or nearly the same) offset?

2. Bearings

At my recent major service I had the front wheel bearings checked.
I was told that they were passable, but should be replaced in the not
too distant future (I figured I'd do it before the end of the season).
I looked over the procedure in the 924 and 931 manuals, and have the
following questions:

Are inner and outer bearings replaced at the same time (i.e. they
wear at the same rate)?

Are the races replaced with the bearings, or just inspected for
proper wear limits? If the latter, what are the limits?

Does the 931 race replacement procedure (involving heating the hub
and using the special tools VW 511, 9154, and P 263) apply to the
944/924S hub? Are there shadetree alternatives?

(Massachusetts readers) Can you recommend any machine shops in the
Boston area to replace the races?

3. Beer bottles

On Sat, 3 Jul 1993, Kevin C. Gross wrote:
> Jim -
> Check the front struts for play.  With the wheel off on a given side,
> push and pull the strut and see whether the shock piston cocks slightly
> from side to side.  I've seen this in 911's and 944's, and it can be
> misdiagnosed as a loose wheel bearing, and can lead to a tooting or
> "beer bottle" noise.

You mean like blowing in a beer bottle? I have that noise at (I believe)
the right rear corner of the car.  It's very intermittent now (once or 
twice for a few seconds per month), but happened more frequently in 
40-50 F weather, and always at slow speeds (10-15 MPH, when decellerating
or turning).  Since it had basically gone away, I didn't mention it when 
I took it in for service, but I'd like to nail it down if I can.

Thanks,
Tony Matt  tonym@world.std.com

Date: Sun, 4 Jul 1993 8:43:38 GMT    
From: uni!qm_server!Larry_Rebarchik@uunet.UU.NET (Larry Rebarchik)
Subject: 914 rear glass 

Date	7/4/93
Subject	914 rear glass
From	Larry Rebarchik
To	Stan Hanks, porshephiles

        Reply to:   914 rear glass
Stan,
Along the same line of 914 glass.
What collective knowledge do we have on ways to fix a rattling rear window?

It appears to be loose on the bottom and sides.

Larry Rebarchik - 

Update on repairs to 914-6, got the trans rebuilt with new 1st and 2nd
syncros, and dogteeth, it's ready to go in.  We finished with the carbs and
they are ready.  In the process of cleaning the exaust manifold and heater
boxes, we noticed an excessive amount of carbon buildup on some of the
exaust valves.  This warranted a check of the heads.  After pulling the
heads off, we found a couple a flattened lobes on one of the cam shafts,  it
turned out to be a clogged oil splash tube that caused this.  So, a couple
of valves later and a new cam, the engine is coming back together, waiting
on a new clutch and flywheel now.

Any folks have a comment on how the oil splash tubes might have clogged, or
any way to catch this problem before it wears the cam down too far?

- --------------------------------------
Date: 6/29/93 1:05 PM
To: Larry Rebarchik
From: Stan Hanks

>Also found the problem with the California rain coming inside
>the car.  My windshield is falling out!

Rain? California? Surely you jest... 8{)

>Anybody got suggestions for 914 with loose windshield and 944
>with hatch glass very loose (could be a new breed here).  Half
>H2O and half N2+O2 (Stan, you listening?)

The 914 is one of those "it depends" deals. If the windshield is loose
because of the seal being shot, then you should go to a glass shop and
have them re-install the windshield. Should be cheap, $50 or so. If it's
loose because it's been loose before and the metal has rusted away, well,
that's not so good, nor cheap to fix. Involves fabricating new metal from 
flat stock, welding it in, etc. Unfortunately, you won't know until the 
glass is out...

>Help me, I've fallen and I can't get out of California....

Sounds like a good bumper sticker to me...

Stan

>Ben Evans asks...
>>How does one tell if the clutch on a 911 ( `88 Carrera, 43k miles)
>>is wearing out? 
>
>Sorry for not replying sooner.  Unfortunately, even th 87-forward 911
>hyrdraulic clutches lack the nice inspection port that 944's have.  Said
>port lets you check both release fork travel (indicating proper function),
>and friction disk wear.

Which is why I developed the following seat-of-the-pants test: find a long
level stretch of road. From a dead stop, wind the car all the way past
red line in first, then speedshift into *fourth*. If you hear a juddering
noise, or smell burnt clutch lining, you have a problem.

Stan

Date: Sun, 4 Jul 1993 03:43 EST
From: uni!qm#h#h#h#u#h#h#h#server!Larry#h#h#h#u#h#h#h#Rebarchik@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: 914 rear glass

Date	7/4/93
Subject	914 rear glass
From	Larry Rebarchik
To	Stan Hanks, porshephiles

        Reply to:   914 rear glass
Stan,
Along the same line of 914 glass.
What collective knowledge do we have on ways to fix a rattling rear window?

It appears to be loose on the bottom and sides.

Larry Rebarchik - 

Update on repairs to 914-6, got the trans rebuilt with new 1st and 2nd
syncros, and dogteeth, it's ready to go in.  We finished with the carbs and
they are ready.  In the process of cleaning the exaust manifold and heater
boxes, we noticed an excessive amount of carbon buildup on some of the
exaust valves.  This warranted a check of the heads.  After pulling the
heads off, we found a couple a flattened lobes on one of the cam shafts,  it
turned out to be a clogged oil splash tube that caused this.  So, a couple
of valves later and a new cam, the engine is coming back together, waiting
on a new clutch and flywheel now.

Any folks have a comment on how the oil splash tubes might have clogged, or
any way to catch this problem before it wears the cam down too far?

- --------------------------------------
Date: 6/29/93 1:05 PM
To: Larry Rebarchik
From: Stan Hanks

>Also found the problem with the California rain coming inside
>the car.  My windshield is falling out!

Rain? California? Surely you jest... 8{)

>Anybody got suggestions for 914 with loose windshield and 944
>with hatch glass very loose (could be a new breed here).  Half
>H2O and half N2+O2 (Stan, you listening?)

The 914 is one of those "it depends" deals. If the windshield is loose
because of the seal being shot, then you should go to a glass shop and
have them re-install the windshield. Should be cheap, $50 or so. If it's
loose because it's been loose before and the metal has rusted away, well,
that's not so good, nor cheap to fix. Involves fabricating new metal from 
flat stock, welding it in, etc. Unfortunately, you won't know until the 
glass is out...

>Help me, I've fallen and I can't get out of California....

Sounds like a good bumper sticker to me...

Stan

------------------------------

Date: Sun, 4 Jul 93 20:40:04 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 rear glass

>Along the same line of 914 glass.
>What collective knowledge do we have on ways to fix a rattling rear window?

Hmmm. That's hard. To the best of my knowledge, the only *REAL* fix is to
remove and reinstall it. There's a Sigla or PPG kit for that, and it's a
real pain to do. I have (with some success) been able to press forward on the
glass (from outside) and insert RTV in the gap between the glass and the groove
it slips into, then after it sets up, repeat from the other side. There's also
a little rubber strip at the base of the window on the outside which helps to 
hold the glass in place. You might try replacing it.

>Any folks have a comment on how the oil splash tubes might have clogged, or
>any way to catch this problem before it wears the cam down too far?

"oil splash tubes"??? explain, please.... do you mean the oil return tubes
from the heads to the case, or the cam oiler galley, or the hose that goes
from the main case to the heads???

In general, any "plugged up" oil passage is either an indication that
the oil wasn't properly maintained, or of some other catastrophic event
in the life of the engine.

Stan

From: DENYS@A1.ISD.UPMC.EDU

> I've been researching my options for a set of autox wheels to use
> on my 924S.  Apparently the 7x15 phone dials from the '87 944
> were an option on the 924 (rears only).  This leads me to wonder 
> whether other 7x15 wheels (Fuchs, cookie cutters) would fit too.
> What is the offset on those phone dials, and are there any other

The design 90 style as well as currnet wheels, and any 928 wheels (phones) have the correct offset.

want to go this route, but given my problem, it may be necessary. 

Thanks for any input/help in advance.

Date: Tue, 6 Jul 93 13:49:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: Goofyeard GS/GS-C's

TO: autox@autox.team.net,
    INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48

.......................................................................

Item Subject: Goodyear GS/GS-C's
The Goodyear GS and GS-C's are ok for stock, since these are the
street tire version of the GS-CS's (the race tire).

They work OK but at not up to the 'stickiness' for the other
AX tires (even the old 008RTU are stickier). Besides, they take to long to
heat up (they are RR compounded tires- takes a lap to heat them up...)).

HOWEVER, having driven in the RAIN on the GS-C's, they are FANTASTIC!!!
Lots of grip and VERY RESPONSIVE!!!

If they release a AX compound next year, they could be quick!

As far as the current RR tire (GS-CS), they wear really quick and are only
good for 3-4 Heat cycles- then they get real greasy!!! (info from a friend
who runs a C2 in the Porsche Club Race Series).  The BFG's last much longer
and lap times are only .5 seconds off of the GS-CS's (but in a 20 minute
race, that can add up, so if you want to be the fastest, you pay......)


G'day,

Mr Wizard

Date: Tue,  6 Jul 93 10:25:06 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Wheels/Bearings/Beer Bottles

| From: Tony Matt  <netmail!tonym@world.std.com>
| To:  <porschephiles@tta.com>
| Subject: Wheels/Bearings/Beer Bottles
| Date: Saturday, July 3, 1993 11:37PM
[stuff deleted]
| 2. Bearings
|
| At my recent major service I had the front wheel bearings checked.
| I was told that they were passable, but should be replaced in the not
| too distant future (I figured I'd do it before the end of the season).
| I looked over the procedure in the 924 and 931 manuals, and have the
| following questions:
|
| Are inner and outer bearings replaced at the same time (i.e. they
| wear at the same rate)?
I'd recommend replacing both - they're cheap and you'll have both of 
them in your hand simultaneously [front], so why not?  There's no sense 
in taking all the time (or $, if done by a shop) to only replace one.

| Are the races replaced with the bearings, or just inspected for
| proper wear limits? If the latter, what are the limits?
The ball bearing races are unservicable and are replaced as a unit.  
The stub axle that they spin on should be inspected and replaced if 
necessary.  Normally the axle is fine, unless the bearing failed and 
chewed it up.

| Does the 931 race replacement procedure (involving heating the hub
| and using the special tools VW 511, 9154, and P 263) apply to the
| 944/924S hub? Are there shadetree alternatives?
The front bearings and seal are easily done by a shadetree mechanic.  
Have some nice synthetic grease available for the repacking job.
The rear bearings must be done similar to how you describe above.  
Cannot be done under a tree; needs a serious mechanic with proper experience.

| (Massachusetts readers) Can you recommend any machine shops in the
| Boston area to replace the races?
Tony, I'd go to Jerry's (European Performance Engineering) - he has 
lots of bearing experience on 951's thanks to me...

| Thanks,
| Tony Matt  tonym@world.std.com

------------------------------

Date: Tue,  6 Jul 93 10:36:47 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: Re:  fuel fumes in my '77 924

| From: Andrew P. Anselmo  <netmail!anselmo@thermsa.eng.sunysb.edu>
| To:  <porschephiles@tta.com>
| Subject: Re:  fuel fumes in my '77 924
| Date: Tuesday, July 6, 1993 9:33AM
|
| Hi All-
|
| I'm a bit dizzy after my drive up from New York to Boston yesterday.  
It seems
| I have developed a bit of a fuel tank leak, or some sort of fuel related
| problem.  After filling up, the smell is the worst, and even with the
| windows down at 70 MPH and the sunroof opened up (not off; experiment shows
| this worsens the smell), it still is apparent.  When the tank gets emptied,
| the smell is lessened, but still strong.  Either that, or I'm getting used
| to it.  I don't think this has anything to do with the charcoal canister
| in the engine compartment (as was mentioned earlier in this group), but
| may be related to the filling area, since the smell is strongest there.  So:
|
| a) Anybody ever have this problem?
Yes.

| b) Any ideas on what it might be?
Fuel filler area, inside rear cargo hold.

| c) Pulling back the cargo-area rug,  I notice that there is a plastic cover
| over the soft fuel hoses.  I once sealed this area with silicone, when
| I thought I smelled fuel back there a long while back.  This may have
| hidden the problem until now!  Should this plastic cover be sealed against
| the interior of the car, or is it just a cover to prevent things from
| crushing the fuel hoses?
My problem was general lack of vapor-seal on the hoses in the filler 
neck area.  It was quite a persistent problem, even though all hoses 
were fine and tight.  This was on a new '83 944.

The neck cover (inside the car) isn't a vapor seal, it's just a 
protective covering.  The point is, your hoses and fuel tank shouldn't 
leak in the first place - thus no need for a seal like that.

If the tank or lines to/from it leaked, the odor would be outside the 
car if your hatch is shut as you drive.  Since this doesn't seem to be 
your problem, I'd say you're in the right area on your car (filler neck).

| d) Do the soft fuel hoses that connect the tank to the the filler neck go
| bad?
Dunno - I suppose they have the same problem any hose has - they get 
brittle with time (ozone), and can be physically damaged.  I've also 
seen many a hose "lose" its seal; the hose loses strength / form around 
the sealing, relaxing slightly and allowing a slight gap to appear.  
This is a *really* common problem on power steering pump resevoir, 
which has virtually no pressure but loves to leak at the hose mounting 
point.  Solution - snip end of hose and move a new section onto the 
part (is possible).

| e) Now for the painful part- How difficult is it to pull the tank?  I 
really don't
| want to go this route, but given my problem, it may be necessary.
I haven't done this, but it doesn't look impossible nor simple.  You 
must drain the tank and be very careful regarding fire/explosion.  
Gasoline doesn't burn, but its vapors do (guess what an "empty" tank 
has in it?).  Disconnect the battery before doing anything!  Prepare 
for downtime, since this job may take more than a day and a view 
special parts (hoses).

| Thanks for any input/help in advance.
|
| -Andrew

Date: Tue, 6 Jul 93 09:26:33 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Rumble from Front end

Jim,

Depending on what kind of camber, toe, caster and tires, you may have
some cupping and wear that give an audible noise and feel at low speeds,
this is particularly true of Yokohama A008s and AVS tires that are worn
on a car set with negative camber all around. Imperfections from hard 
braking and wear are very noticeable.

Borrow a SET of wheels and tires and try them to see if it goes away.

Jim Pasha

pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

The guy could not sell it for the next year and a half.
The last time I saw him advertize it, he was
asking 18000$Cdn = 14000$US.

So, if the market in Montreal (CANADA) is 
anything like the U.S., tell your friend that he 
is asking for approximately double the ongoing 
market price. 

Given that I now own a Porsche (944,'86, 64000 miles)
and knowing what the repair bills look like
around this mileage, I think I was stupid to have 
even offered more than 13000$Cdn = 10000$US. 
I am lucky that the guy did not accept my offer. 
THANK YOU GOD!!!

some money. (Gee, like they need more profit, right ?)

Date: Tue, 6 Jul 93 10:21:53 EDT
From: Mark Sirota <mark@greenwich.com>
Subject: Re: 

> Along the same line of 914 glass.
> What collective knowledge do we have on ways to fix a rattling rear window?

Mine was rattling so bad like this I was sure it was just going to break
while I was driving.  Loose especially at the lower right-hand corner, but
also fairly loose at the lower left.

I ended up putting in some strips of 1/4" thick rubber at the top inside,
between the window and the underside covering of the targa bar.  Three
little pieces, two on the outside edges and one in the center.  Worked great
for the noise, but it still leaks like a sieve...

are necessary.

Date: Tue, 6 Jul 93 17:03:06 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Tires


Andre,

Try the new Toyo I 600-F1s. They are much cheaper than the Yokohama 
AVS, almost as sticky, ride better and perform well in snow and other 
moist goo. They are V-rated and run about $114 (US) each from Roger
Kraus Tires (510) 886-4636. Ask for Roger, Joe or Bert.

You can put wider tires, 225/50VR 15 work well, but you should consider 
a BETTER tire first. The guys you've been using aren't the hot ticket
for a 944. My preference for the street is like this:

Yokohama AVS
Goodyear GS-C
Toyo I600-F1
Yokohama A008
Dunlop
Yokohama 509
Firestone Firehawk

I view the Toyos as the best, price/performance considered, use your 
discresion.

Autocrossing, I use A008RTU, they are still awesome for what they give.

If you have only 7" rims, you can gain a lot by going to 8" wheels at 
the rear, I run tire pressures of 34 front and 36 rear also to improve 
the steering. 

The major problem with the Michelins is that the manufacturer uses a 
large amount of creosote as a filler in the tread compound to extend 
tire mileage. This compound acts like grease in wet weather when you 
try turning on a surface that isn't a true asphalt surface. Michelin 
and the cheaper tires available use a lot of creosote to extend tire 
life. More than one Porsche owner has been lured into `less expensive'
tires with grim results.

Best Regards,

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Tue, 6 Jul 93 22:49:00 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 Engine Layout

>    So until I can find one of those, I have two questions I hope you can
>answer:

Well, there was something in Pano but I don't remember when... This would
be a lot easier if I hadn't sold my 1.8 a while back...

>1)  I'm trying to locate the Decel Regualtor on my '74 914/1.8L.  Is it the
>wide silver-ish cylinder with a conical end and several hoses coming out (one
>of them connects to just past the Air Flow Meter box) that is nestled right
>next to my intake air distributor?

The decel regulator is the device over near the battery with three vacuum
hoses going to it -- one in each end, one on the side. 

>2)  What about the Auxiliary Air Regulator?  Is it the (slightly rusted)
>narrower cylinder down below the battery?

The aux air regualtor is sort of a disk-looking thing, with two > 1/2"
hoses going to it, one on each side. There's also an electrical connector,
and usually on the 1.8, a protruding shaft and black knob you can use
to turn the insides. Usually pretty close to the air box (the lines connect
there, one to the air filter, one to the main air body past the flapper
valve).

Good luck.

Stan

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 08:53:18 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Bearings


Tony,

The bearings in a 4-bolt hub for a 924 or 931 (924 Turbo) are basically
Volkswagen sizes.  The outer races should be replaced at the same time
the inners are done on any wheel bearing replacement.

The late 924S uses the same bearings as the 911 up to 1989, this is the 
same for the 924, 931 w/4 wheel disc brakes and non-turbo 944s to 1986.

All outer races may be either pressed out or driven with a long, fine
tipped punch and hammer. There is a relief in the hub for doing just 
that on all the hubs and one-piece rotors. Always replace the bearing 
seals when doing the inners.

Both inner and outer should be replaced as a set, adjustment should be 
tight enough so that there is no play when wiggling the wheel when it 
is installed.

The outer bearing on all 924/944/968 variants will wear first because
of the load added by the "negative scrub radius" feature of the 
suspension design.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

------------------------------

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 11:02:09 -0500
From: stan@tta.com (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Problem replacing 914 rear pads

>	I need to replace the rear pads on my '74 1.8L 914.  I was
>following the Hayes manual when I tok out the retaining pins,
>and the speader spring.  That all worked really well for a car that
>was probably still on the OEM pads from '74.  The problem arose
>when I tried to get the piston to retract so that I could insert
>the new pad(the outside piston).  The manual said to use a plastic
>or wood bar and pry the piston back (being careful off the rotor)
>while rotating the hex bolt.  When I tried this, after beaking 
>the nut holding the hex screw,rotating the hex screw clockwise
>seemed to retract the piston a little bit, while counter clockwise
>seemed to force it in more.  But I couldn't get it to retract
>enough ofor the new pads :-(.  Does anyone have any suggestions on
>how to get this to work????

The problem you're seeing is probably the parking brake adjuster. On the
914 rear calipers, there is this screw gizmo on the back of the caliper
(i.e. towards the transmission) that regulates the pad travel for
the cable operated portion of the unit. You have to let that all the
way out to change the pads.  This is talked about in fairly oblique terms
in Section 8.9.12.

And, once you're done, you have to adjust it back to the right place, too.
That operation is described in the 8.10 "Venting Clearance". Nice picture 
(fig. 8.17) showing the adjustment being made. 

Oh, and this is a two-part process: there's a cap over the actual adjustment
screw. It's an Allen screw cap, and frequently strips. One of the more popular
accessory type items for the 914 is new caps, made from stainless, with 12mm
bolt heads. About $15 a set from most vendors (Automotion, Perf. Prods., 
Tweaks, etc.)

You could also have a frozen piston. On cars where the pads have been worn
way down for a long time, I've had them build up corrossion from the 
hydgroscopic nature of brake fluid between the bottom of the piston and the 
bottom of the bore, and require bead blasting and rebuilding.

Aren't 20 year old cars fun?

Stan

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 09:34:33 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924 Performance


Steve,

I've worked with the water cooled Porsches for sometime, I'll try to 
point you in the direction I think will work for you in the UK.

All 924 and 924 turbo parts suspension interchange EXCEPTING those on 
Nov 1981 and later (same as 944). Of all the stock swaybars, the 23mm
M471 option front bar works the best. There were 21mm and 22mm front 
bars also. Rear bars interchange on all 924/944 variants, make sure the 
car you purchase has a rear bar or the mounts will NOT be on the torsion
bar tube. A kit is available from Automotion in the US to provide for a 
mounting point. The stock rear bar for the 924 is 14mm( the same for 
the 944). 
Automotion also offers aftermarket swaybars, 28mm front, 19mm and 25mm 
for the rear. These are race quality, very well suited to street/track 
use.

Some advice in selecting a car, Buy a March 1980 or later car, there
were significant changes in the body and quality of assembly. You have 
the 125 HP engine, so the only thing you might want to do is add a 
header. Retain the stock exhaust, as it is much better than any cheap
replacement (more HP). Flowing the head helps, but adding a 2300CC 
stroker kit works the best, you'll have about 145HP and weigh much less than a 944.
If you can find one, some of the cars with sport suspension had 4 wheel
disc brakes, very desireable (I have one). 
I  would find one with 15 inch wheels in any case.

Automotion is at 193 Commercial Street, Sunnyvale, Ca, USA, 94086, phone
(408) 736-9020
Their UK distributor is G-FORCE
                         1A Bessmer Crescent
                         Rabins Lane
                         Aylesbury, Bucks HP1 93TF
                          683-3930

Best Regards
Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

are stiffer but Konis are adjustable.

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 14:08:20 EDT
From: ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu (Nathan Ulrich)
Subject: Sticky tires (no subjective comments included)

Based on testing that I have done on my race car (944 prepared to  
SCCA ITS rules, which means 2535 lbs minimum weight, about 175 hp,  
and tire size of 225/50R15), and based on the results of testing that  
others that I trust have done in similar 944s and RX-7s:

Fastest:
	Goodyear GS-CS
	New BFG Comp T/A

Next fastest (about 3/4 sec per lap slower at 1.5 mile tracks like  
Lime Rock and NHIS):
	Old BFG Comp T/A
	Yoko A008RS

Slowest: (between 1-2 seconds slower)
	Hoosier Street TD
	Bridgestone RE71

The Goodyears and the new BFGs are basically indistinguishable in lap  
times, although if you look at the data cross-eyed, the Goodyears  
might have a slight (.1 sec) edge.  The Goodyears go away after 4  
heat cycles, and by the fifth or sixth heat cycle are as much as half  
a second slower.  I don't know how quickly the BFGs go away, though  
they are rumoured to last longer.

I run the Goodyears because I prefer their "feel" and because their  
diameter is slightly smaller, which suits my gearing better.   
Contrary to what was said in an earlier post, they are legal for PCA  
club racing.  The rules now require only that there be  
distinguishable tread left after the race.  This isn't really a  
problem, because by the time the tread is gone, the tires are no  
longer sticky anyway.

I have no objective data on other tires, though several people I know  
are happy with the Toyo and the new compound (A008RS II) Yokos.  We  
didn't test the new Yokos because the manufacturer said that they  
wouldn't last on our cars under race conditions.

The results for cars with other characteristics will almost certainly  
be somewhat different.  A lot of time and money was invested to yield  
the information above, but your mileage may vary.

Nathan
ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 14:14:23 EDT
From: Michael C. Seibert <seibert@xn.ll.mit.edu>
Subject: 944 shocks

Rear shocks are very easy to replace on the 944.  You just remove
a wheel, unbolt the 2 bolts holding the old shock in, and bolt in
the new one.  No alignments, nothing else to remove, no fuss.
It'll take you about 15 minutes per side with manual handtools.
I've had Bilsteins all around for 5 years now (guaranteed
forever) and am happy so far.  _MS

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 10:58:50 PDT
From: Mike Weihman <mikew@hpsadpe.sad.hp.com>
Subject: Re: fuel vapors in the cabin


I had a problem with gasoline vapors in the cabin of my 1980 931 as
well.  Although I suspected the soft filler hoses for a while, the
culprit turned out to be a pinhole rust-through near the bottom of the
fuel tank itself.  For the first couple weeks, the leak was small
enough that no fuel dripped on the ground, but it shed plenty of vapor
into the air.  The 931 seems to suck air in to the rear hatch area,
and so the normal holes and leaks in the rear of the car were enough
to let in a lot of vapor, whether the widows were up or down.

Removing my fuel tank was not too terrible a job.  I did drop the rear
exhaust to make it easier.

 -mike

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 12:36:31 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re: Problem replacing 914 rear pads

 He could also have too much fluid in his hydraulic system. If you
 have had worn pads for a while and have topped up the fluid level
 then when you insert new pads you will be attempting to increase
 the pressure in the system by hand. Not going to happen. Remove
 the reservoir cap and check the fluid level. If it is topped up
 remove some fluid (an old battery tester is ideal for this - 
 neutralize with baking soda and water and clean before use). Lower
 the fluid level and try prying the pads back again. A good friend
 showed me the nifty trick of just turning the pads around and 
 installing them with metal plates inward (toward one another).
 Then you insert your prying bar (whatever the material) between
 the two pads without damaging the pad surface.

 Note that to get at the venting adjustment screw you may need some
 special tools (like a wobble drive extension). I've found it helps
 to jack up the rear A-arm slightly sometimes to help with the 
 clearance. Of course, the usual disclaimers apply - support the
 car with proper jackstands, etc, etc.

  Stef

Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 17:15:18 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 914, 914/6 proportioning valve


Hi guys,

I've been reading about the 914 rear pad issue, I want to remind you 
about a seldom appreciated feature on the 914 series, the proportioning
valve that resides on the lower firewall on the driver's side (passenger
side for you Brits) of the car.

When bleeding the brakes or searching for that spongy pedal source, 
bleed all corners as you usually would (rr 1st, lr 2nd, rf 3rd, lf last)
then go to the valve. Have someone pump the brake a few times slowly, 
then unfasten the line on the output side (lines going to rear of car)
until fluid seeps past. Repeat for the input side. This should remove 
any air bubbles in the machined cavity.

To adjust, loosen the nut around the center shaft. Turn clockwise to
bias to the front, counter-clockwise to bias to the rear. This 
adjustment should only be done with all other parts of the system in
correct adjustment and operation. 

One other source of a spongy pedal is the master cylinder, pull the 
carpet back, then remove the floorboard under the pedals. examine for 
an oily residue that feels like brake fluid. The failure mode for the
brake master is usually the seal to the shaft. 

Hope it helps.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

------------------------------


Porschephiles #444                         Thu Jul 8 23:02:38 CDT 1993
Date: Wed, 7 Jul 93 16:48:37 EDT
From: F.W.Kerfoot__Iii@att.com
Subject: Re:  Spinning Porsches

> It _is_ possible, contrary to popular belief, to spin a 914 as well.
> I've been lucky enough to do it twice (and not hit anything either
> time). Both events came from driving too fast on wet roads (believed
> to be dry). I must say that it is the fastest spin I've ever been
> in - little or no warning. 
>
> But then, this is all anecdotal (or it will be after you read it),
> and you should go back to believing that 914s don't spin. Because
> after all, you haven't seen one spin, have you?

I must have missed the original post on this subject, but I am not
aware of any popular belief (is this similar to the much referenced
"conventional wisdom"?) that the 914 can't spin. In fact, the 914 is
among the easiest of Porsches to spin unless you have quick reflexes,
due to the low polar moment of inertia. The latter is due to the
mid-engine design, and is one of the dominant reasons that most
race cars are mid-engined where the rules allow it. The low polar
moment of inertia makes it very easy to rotate the car around a
vertical axis through the center of gravity. This means that the
car spins very easily, and very quickly. It also means that small
steering inputs, applied quickly and properly, will stop the spin.
In the hands of an expert, this makes the car very capable. In the
hands of an average driver on slippery pavement, it means a lot
of spins. I have heard it said that this tendency to spin easily
when driven by average drivers, especially in slippery conditions,
is one of the reasons Porsche has stayed away (so far) from another
mid-engined design.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 09:42:06 EDT
From: lmcsugi@LMC.ericsson.se (David Sugirtharaj)
Subject: Re: 911 feasable in winter?


.I live in New England and can't afford a separate "winter car".  Is it
.feasable to drive a 911 in New England winters with snow, or am I just
.asking to go through a guardrail backwards?


For me, the main reason for owning a Porsche is enjoyment. You simply
do not enjoy driving the car in the winter. If you plan to keep the car
for a long time, you probably would suffer from higher maintenance cost and
insurance as well as depreciation. Not to mention potholes, low clearance
and salt. No one would want to buy your car knowing that it has been 
winter driven.

Instead of spending about $1500 dollars on winter tires and wheels you 
could pick up a mid-80s VW Golf and enjoy your winter and save your 911s'
heater boxes.

Sugi
Montreal,
Ericsson Communications

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 1993 09:44:45 -0400 (EDT)

It sounds like the Adopt-A-Highway project that the Golden Gate PCA
region is involved with.  A group of people from a business or
institution work litter patrol on a stretch of road a number of
times per year.  In return, the state erects a sign to acknowledge
the group's 'adoption'.  This was written up in Pano a year or so
ago, and briefly mentioned in this month's 'From the Regions' column.
Now if I could only get the NER to 'adopt' my house ;-)

Subject: Dunlops


Lee,

Well, that's a good question. 8's will work in the front, the big 
question is with the late wheel offset is whether you have enough
clearance between the strut and the tire. I would mount one and install 
it on the car, putting it back on the ground and checking for both strut
and fender clearance. I would suspect that 225s would be a better 
choice. 

Basically, the Dunlops are a very good tire, check the country of origin
as the German or Japanese ones seem to be better constructed. Since
Sumitomo purchased Dunlop the quality has improved vastly. These are 
first line tires in most situations, just remember that when they wear,
they will have the SAME tendancy that all wide performance tires do in
wet or snowy conditions, the lose some grip. Just adjust the aggresive-
 ness of your driving.

Again, I think 225's will fit, the 245's need to be looked at, I also 
think you would find that the sterring effort and handling would be 
better with the 225s on the front.

Best regards,
Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Thu,  8 Jul 93 13:55:55 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: New Owner Question.

| From: Afsar Husain  <netmail!ahusain@nis.naitc.com>
| To:  <porschephiles@tta.com>
| Subject: New Owner Question.
| Date: Thursday, July 8, 1993 12:10PM
[stuff deleted]
|   1) Where is the mileage trip reset switch located?
Is this is an 85/2 car with the new interior, or the 85/1 which is the 
original style?  If it's an 85/2, the reset button is in the vent grill 
immediately right of the instrument cluster.  It's disguised as the 
center vent louver - just push it in with the ignition on and a 
solenoid resets the trip odometer.

|   2) Is it worth buying the Porsche Workshop manual($150),  or are
| 	 there some recommended publications that I can buy to help me
| 	 better understand this car. I plan to work and fix the car
| 	 myself so it must have Photos and should be good technical?
If Bentley makes a book yet, that would be a good one to get.  I don't 
think they have one though.  I've heard that the Haynes book isn't too 
good, although it covers the basics (I have a VW Haynes book that I 
don't even use any more - that's a statement about its quality!).  The 
factory books are great if you don't mind spending the $.  Try a better 
quality bookstore for Bentley, or call the publisher in Cambridge, MA.

|   3) Why is my front brake light On. Is this a warning and if so
| 	 how much time do I have to replace it.
Dashboard lamp?  Probably because your brake pad wear sensor has been 
cut, indicating a very low pad.  Check the pads (all 8 - requires wheel 
removal) and wear sensors that clip into the pads.  If any sensor wire 
is cut through, you'll have to buy a new one (~ $18 for your car).

|   4) How can I get an Owner manual for this car.
Contact a dealer, or possibly some aftermarket shops that specialize in 
manuals.  This should be easy to get.

|   5) What is the difference between an 85 and an 85-1/2 944. How
| 	 do one distinguishes this?
The 85/2 is the new interior, HVAC (heating, AC), seats, suspension, 
etc.  85/1 is like the 924 & original 944.

|   6) Last one, is how to deal with this standard transmission. I
| 	 have been driving an non-automatic car for over 8 years
| 	 and after 200 miles on my Porsche I am still very rough when
| 	 the clutch is release and the gear are engaged (You can hear
| 	 the gear engaging with a jerky noise). How much practice and
| 	 time do I need to perfect it. Are there any tips.
Best thing would be to have someone who *really* knows how to shift, 
show you.  Are you in Porsche Club of America yet?  PCA is a source of 
exceptional talent and ability when it comes to anything Porsche.  I 
can send you info if you'd like.

Basically, the Porsche is a tighter machine that doesn't hide the 
driver's mistakes or roughness when shifting.  I can't diagnose much 
via email, so more seat time and some experimentation on your part will help.

Good luck, and welcome to the Porsche world!

		JBS

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 12:06:30 -0700
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: exercising brake calipers


>Does nayone have suggestions on how to exercise the pistons on the calipers
>now that I have them off the car?

>      Alex


Re: exercising the pistons in a caliper.

You might want to rebuild the caliper, if so, this procedure was used
successfully.

After trying the recommended compressed air + wood stop block removal of
caliper pistons, I didn't like the lack of control and the loud pop when 
the piston comes out.

When I rebuilt the brakes on the the 72 911T, each caliper was unbolted,
in turn, but left connected to the brake line.  A large C clamp was used to 
retract and hold one piston.

The master cylinder was used to force out the unclamped piston.  

The piston/cylinder were cleaned up, a new seal was installed and the piston
was re-installed at the correct angle.

The step on the piston MUST be installed at the correct angle for the
pads to wear evenly.  

The process was then repeated on the other piston in the caliper.

I found using the master cylinder to push out the piston was far more
controlled than the compressed air method.

It's wasteful of brake fluid, but it gets the job done, and with
your parts car, you don't even have to take the running car out of
operation.

John Wright

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 15:40:18 CDT
From: cork@cs.rice.edu (Corky Cartwright)
Subject: Cross-cultural Dunlop SP 8000's

The recent discussion on list about Dunlop tires reminded me of the
following anomaly.  I recently purchased SP 8000's for my 951S from Tire
Rack.  Since the SP 8000 is available in size 225/45-16, I decided to
order 225/45-16's for the front instead of OE size (225/50-16's)
and 245/45-16 (OE size) tires for the rear.  The rear tires were
manufactured in Japan (which I expected), but the front tires were
manufactured in Germany (which I did not expect).

I like the subjective feel of the new tires much better than the old ones
(Dunlop D40/M2's in OE sizes), but I haven't tried them yet at the track.
I haven't detected any difference in construction or performance beween 
the Japanese and German versions of the SP 8000.  Nevertheless, I still think
it is strange to drive a car with matching front and rear tires made in
different countries.

- -- Corky Cartwright
   Rice University

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 14:27:10 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re:  914 brake caliber rebuild?

 I've found it really, really helps to have (1) a compressor with
 air nozzle attachment and (2) a variety of different thickness
 aluminum blocks (more necessary for >2 piston calipers).

 As a rule it is undesirable to actually split the caliper halves.
 I would clean them externally in a solvent bath before attempting
 piston removal. 

 Then place different blocks into the aperture between the pistons
 Then insert the compressor nozzle into the brakeline aperture (surround
 it with a rag to get a halfway decent air seal) and pull the trigger.
 Depending on how good a seal you get you can force the pistons out
 a little bit at a time. Because of space constraints you'll get out
 one piston before the other. Use a block to shut off the cavity and
 try to force out the other piston with air pressure. 

 Time and a variety of small nylon bristle brushes will allow you to
 get most of the crud out of the caliper. Try and use a good solvent
 (SafetyKleen) to make sure that you leave as little deposit in the
 calipers as possible.

 You might want to use a honing stone to reduce any surface pitting
 on the outside of the piston. While you can't reduce blowby from the
 pitting at least you won't slice up your new seals.

 I use teflon sealing tape on the bleed screws as well.

 Stef

Panorama is the official publication of Porsche Club of America, and is 
available only to PCA members.  You can get a sample copy from your 
local PCA membership chair (Danielle Calder, 508-443-8432).

magazine.  I don't know if it's available at well-stocked newsstands or 
not.  You may have to go to the publisher directly.  Porschephiler's: 
Anyone have the address on this?  You might also look for Excellence 
advertisements in other car mag's, including Pano.

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 14:37:36 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re:  Spinning Porsches

 Hmmm.. I should have added a smiley.

 However, I disagree with your assertion that 914s are easy to spin.
 I think they are very difficult to spin. A low polar moment of inertia
 should contribute to a higher degree of traction at both ends of 
 the car. A high polar moment leads to 911 like handling (see definition
 of trailing throttle oversteer). 

 The car does indeed spin very quickly but I think that is a result
 of how many lateral g's it'll take before it does spin. I read somewhere
 that you can get 1.1 lateral g's out of a 914 with race rubber (and
 no other changes). Pretty damn amazing. 

 I attribute my spins to driving hard on wet pavement with poor rain
 tires (somewhat worn A403s - old style).

 The engineer who designed the car said of it that it was a 30% better
 handling car than the 911 (how he came to this figure I have no idea).

 S.

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 17:01:23 -0500
From: Lawrence Smith <lawrence@uhura.cs.wisc.edu>


||   3) Why is my front brake light On. Is this a warning and if so
|| 	 how much time do I have to replace it.
|Dashboard lamp?  Probably because your brake pad wear sensor has been 
|cut, indicating a very low pad.  Check the pads (all 8 - requires wheel 
|removal) and wear sensors that clip into the pads.  If any sensor wire 
|is cut through, you'll have to buy a new one (~ $18 for your car).

	The light also comes on if your brake reservoir is low, so check to
make sure this is topped off, there should be a barely visible line on the
outside of the reservoir.

||   5) What is the difference between an 85 and an 85-1/2 944. How
|| 	 do one distinguishes this?
|The 85/2 is the new interior, HVAC (heating, AC), seats, suspension, 
|etc.  85/1 is like the 924 & original 944.

	There were a great deal of mechanical and electrical changes made
to the 85-1/2 model year, so you will *definitly* want to know which model
you have.  The 85-1/2 has a large oval vent setup that stretches across the
dash, the early 85 models have a squarish dash rising up from the driveshaft
tunnel, with a few guages in it. (i think; i have an 85-1/2).

|   6) Last one, is how to deal with this standard transmission. I
| 	 have been driving an non-automatic car for over 8 years
| 	 and after 200 miles on my Porsche I am still very rough when
| 	 the clutch is release and the gear are engaged (You can hear
| 	 the gear engaging with a jerky noise). How much practice and
| 	 time do I need to perfect it. Are there any tips.

	My gears make an audible and tangible "thunk" sound when they engage,
i think this is fairly normal, all the 944's i drove were like this. Just
about everyting about these cars is solid and heavy, that's the way they
are.  Roughness with the clutch may be a problem with the clutch.  "chatter"
when engaging is an example.  Have someone knowledgeable drive the car or
take it to a service dept that you can trust.

Date: Thu,  8 Jul 93 18:01:18 TZ

> As a rule it is undesirable to actually split the caliper halves.
My factory manual states explicitly, not to separate the halves of 
aluminum 4-pot calipers because the bolts securing the halves "are 
tightened to the yielding point".  They go on to say that only 
specially equipped shops can install and torque the bolts.

Of course these calipers are different than the 914 calipers under 
discussion, but I think it's a point worth mentioning!

		JBS

Date: Thu,  8 Jul 93 15:17:50 PDT
From: Mike Conte <mikecon@microsoft.com>

|   6) Last one, is how to deal with this standard transmission. I
| 	 have been driving an non-automatic car for over 8 years

| 	 the clutch is release and the gear are engaged (You can hear
| 	 the gear engaging with a jerky noise). How much practice and

This might be you, or it might be a damaged clutch. If the rubber 
center on your clutch has been damaged (or is on it's way out), you may 
hear unpleasant noises when shifting, and during transitions to power 
and overrun. I'd have an experienced 944 owner and mechanic drive it.

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 22:20:04 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 911 Shifter question

>Sometimes when I shift into 2nd
>the shifter binds up, more likely on a down shift than upshift.

You have 915 Disease.  The only known cure is a transmission rebuild. If
it's really binding, then you're already hosed and there's no point in 
taking it apart until it stops working. Then be sure and exchange your
transmission for a high-quality rebuilt unit with a warranty. The cost to
rebuild yours will *FAR* exceed the cost of the exchange.

>Anyone know a good place to get 74 style heat exchangers?  All I ever see listed
>are SSI's and I don't really need stainless, and certainly not at the cost.

If you don't like the SSI prices, you *REALLY* won't like prices for new OE
'74 heat exchangers. The SSIs are about $390 a side, wholesale, but you
typically buy them as a "kit" which has all the new oil hoses, sheet metal,
etc. that you need for the conversion, and a new high performance muffler
setup as well, for about $1800 retail. It's well worth it. If you want to
try used, well, there are a zillion Porsche dismantlers, start calling.

Stan

Date: Thu, 8 Jul 93 17:13:59 CDT
From: tonye@weissach.eng.hou.compaq.com (Tony Eggers)
Subject: New Owner Question. (fwd)

Affie writes:
>   1) Where is the mileage trip reset switch located?
On 85.5 and newer...
The tripmeter reset switch is located in the left center A/C vent.
It is grooved, so it looks a little different that the vent louvres, but
it is hidden well. I'm not sure about the '85.
> 
>   2) Is it worth buying the Porsche Workshop manual($150),  or are
> 	 there some recommended publications that I can buy to help me
> 	 better understand this car. I plan to work and fix the car
> 	 myself so it must have Photos and should be good technical? 
> 
If you have a microfiche viewer, TMC Publications has shop manuals for $32.
 Call Jeff @ (412)367-4490

>   3) Why is my front brake light On. Is this a warning and if so
> 	 how much time do I have to replace it.
> 
There is a small polymer/plastic disc brake pad wear sensor on each wheel.
When the pad wears too thin, this sensors wears. When this sensors gets worn
out, a circuit is opened causing your brake light to come on. THIS SHOULD BE
CHECKED IMMEDIATELY. If your pads are this thin, then you are about to go
metal to metal contact on your rotors. It's possible the pads have been
replaced and they're ok, but the sensor is fairly cheap and should be replaced.

>   4) How can I get an Owner manual for this car.
> 

Any suggestions anyone?
> 
>   5) What is the difference between an 85 and an 85-1/2 944. How
> 	 do one distinguishes this?
> 
The dash is the easiest way. If your instrument cluster sits under a
sort-of square dash, then it's an 85. The 85.5 and newer dash is more of
an elongated oval with A/C vents included in the oval.

>   6) Last one, is how to deal with this standard transmission. I
> 	 have been driving an non-automatic car for over 8 years
> 	 and after 200 miles on my Porsche I am still very rough when
> 	 the clutch is release and the gear are engaged (You can hear
> 	 the gear engaging with a jerky noise). How much practice and
> 	 time do I need to perfect it. Are there any tips.
>
Well..., Not sure how to answer this one. Anyone else? It may be that your
clutch and tranny are about finished. Try to find some one at a local autocross
event that drives a 944 and let them take your car for a spin.

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 08:46:21 EDT
From: ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu (Nathan Ulrich)
Subject: Re: Spinning Porsches

sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs) writes:
> However, I disagree with your assertion that 914s are easy to spin.
> I think they are very difficult to spin. A low polar moment of  
inertia
> should contribute to a higher degree of traction at both ends of 

> the car. A high polar moment leads to 911 like handling (see  
definition
> of trailing throttle oversteer). 


I'm sorry, but you're wrong.  The polar moment of inertia is only  
important dynamically, and has no influence on the steady-state  
cornering ability of a car.  It is a measure of the car's inertial  
resistance to torques about the vertical axis through the center of  
mass of the car.  A moment of inertia is for torque what mass is for  
force (tau = I omega dot vs. F = m a).  A car with a low moment of  
inertia will turn in easier and respond more quickly to steering  
forces.  It will, however, also theoretically be quicker (and easier)  
to spin.  Having raced (and spun) 914s, 911s, and 944s, I can tell  
you unequivocably that the 914 is quicker to spin.  This  
characteristic is independent of the "degree of traction" at each end  
of the car.

The trailing throttle oversteer characteristic of the 911s is not a  
result of a high polar moment of inertia, but instead a result of the  
high amount of rear overhung mass.  Any car set up to be quick on the  
track will have this characteristic to some degree, however.

> The car does indeed spin very quickly but I think that is a result
> of how many lateral g's it'll take before it does spin. I read  
somewhere
> that you can get 1.1 lateral g's out of a 914 with race rubber (and
> no other changes). Pretty damn amazing. 

> The engineer who designed the car said of it that it was a 30%  
better
> handling car than the 911 (how he came to this figure I have no  
idea).

Why should the lateral acceleration have anything to do with how  
quickly it spins?  The word from the design engineers at Porsche has  
always been that the 914 handles better than the 911, the 924 handles  
better than the 914, and the 944 handles better than the 924.   
Prepared to equivalent standards, the 944/968 is clearly the  
best-handling Porsche to date.  Porsche obviously realizes this, and  
will introduce a revised 911 suspension sometime soon (anyone know  
exactly when?).

Nathan
ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu

All I remember about my old 914 1.7l was that it was the type of 
car that I could easily perform four wheel drifts, with
very little steering movement.  The slightest change in the steering
wheel was transmitted directly to the chassis.  Very little fat!

to brake coming into the turns and how I could attack the curves much more 
agressively.  Unfortunately once the road opened up the little 1.7
was no match...:-(.

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 11:46:12 EDT
From: sreit@ctp.com (Stefan Reitshamer)
Subject: Re: Spinning Porsches

> The trailing throttle oversteer characteristic of the 911s is not a  
> result of a high polar moment of inertia, but instead a result of the  
> high amount of rear overhung mass.  Any car set up to be quick on the  
> track will have this characteristic to some degree, however.

I believe the suspension geometry increases the rear wheel's toe out
as the wheel moves down relative to the body (weight
is lifted from the rear suspension when throttle is decreased); oversteer
is not due only to front/rear weight distribution.

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 09:26:53 EDT
From: F.W.Kerfoot__Iii@att.com
Subject: Re:  Spinning Porsches

> However, I disagree with your assertion that 914s are easy to spin.
> I think they are very difficult to spin. A low polar moment of inertia
> should contribute to a higher degree of traction at both ends of 
> the car. A high polar moment leads to 911 like handling (see definition
> of trailing throttle oversteer). 

The low polar moment of inertia is, to first order, independent of
the relative (or absolute) degree of traction at the ends of the car.
The degree of traction (more specifically the relative slip angles)
are functions of the vertical load on each tire (including any
dynamic load from weight transfer), the angle of the tire to the road,
and the tire characteristics, basically the lateral force versus
slip angle as a function of normal force. If the car has already started
to rotate there is a small contribution to lateral acceleration, but
I believe this is a second order effect. The big effect from polar
moment of inertia comes when the front and rear lateral forces are
unbalanced, and the polar moment of inertia then determines how
quickly the car rotates, either until the appropriate slip angles
are achieved to balance the lateral forces, or until the car spins.

By the way, a better example of a high polar moment of inertia Porsche,
without the large weight unbalance of the 911, is the 944. In the 944,
the engine and the transmission form a "dumbell", which has a high
polar moment of inertia.
>
> The car does indeed spin very quickly but I think that is a result
> of how many lateral g's it'll take before it does spin. I read somewhere
> that you can get 1.1 lateral g's out of a 914 with race rubber (and
> no other changes). Pretty damn amazing. 

I don't think 914's achieve substantially higher degrees of lateral g's
than other Porsches in similar degrees of preparation (stiffness,
tire sizes, etc.). The benifit of the 914 chassis comes from being
able to manage slip angles more effectively very near the limit.

>
> I attribute my spins to driving hard on wet pavement with poor rain
> tires (somewhat worn A403s - old style).

I know a number of other 914 dirvers who have similar war stories of
fast, unexpected spins in the rain, not necessarily with bad tires.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

had changed color from yellow to red. I mean every panel was

and difficult to paint.

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 14:13:04 EDT
From: F.W.Kerfoot__Iii@att.com
Subject: Re: Membership

>Bad news for the heathens(re:Rod Sprattling). You MUST be the registered
>owner (or on the regisitration in some way) of a Porsche and have a 
>valid  drivers license. It's all there in the PCA bylaws. 

To be very specific, the membership application has a place to enter
the chassis ID number of your Porsche, as well as a description (model,
year, etc). I do not believe that any check is done other than to
see if the form of the ID number is appropriate for the car described.
I'm not proposing that anyone circumvent the bylaws, of course.

Another possibility is to attend meetings and events as a non-member
until you purchase a Porsche. You won't get Panorama, of course, but
the region president gets extra copies of Pano (or used to, anyway),
and you may be able to talk him or her into a copy for a few months
while you pick out your car. Individual regions may have policies on
this sort of thing, but I know the Jersey Shore region (and I suspect
many others) would be amenable to this sort of thing.

>
>Once you've owned a Porsche and been a member, you may remain as an 
>inactive member as long as you pay your annual dues. 

Unless you go out of your way to tell them, I believe things just
coast along, and you can stay as a full member without a Porsche
(whatever the bylaws say). Of course it is harder to be active in
some of the activities without a Porsche.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 13:43:33 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Membership

>Bad news for the heathens(re:Rod Sprattling). You MUST be the registered
>owner (or on the regisitration in some way) of a Porsche and have a 
>valid  drivers license. It's all there in the PCA bylaws. 

OK, I decided to check this out. Here in the Manual of Proceedures (your
friendly neighborhood region officers should have one):

ARTICLE IV: MEMBERSHIP DUES AND FEES

Section 1: Membership

Membership shall be restricted to owners, lessees, or *co-owners* of Porsches
who are 18 years of age or older, and to *such other persons interested in
the Club and its objectives as provided in Section 2 (B), (C), and (D).*
(emphasis mine)

OK, down to the relevant Sections:

2(B) ASSOCIATE - Any active member who ceases to own, lease, or co-own a
Porsche while in good standing, or any person, employed by a Porsche-oriented
business, interested in the Club and its objectives having paid Club dues an
fees as required. 

2(C) HONORARY - ok, this isn't likely...

2(D) LIFE - ok, even less likely (both are given by the PCA Board of Directors)

So: it depends on how you read this. Does "interested in the Club and its 
objectives" modify "employed by a Porsche-oriented business" or does it
modify "any person"? Second item: "co-owner". I sell a guy the seatbelts
in one of my cars for $10. He's now a co-owner, right? Something to think
about...

Stan

Date: Fri, 09 Jul 93 14:07:00 PDT
From: "Goodman, John" <Goodmjo@hvsmtp1.mdc.com>
Subject: 911 hates the rain


I've been experiencing a problem with my '85 911 ever since I got it (about 
a year and a half ago).  In most any wet environment (light/heavy rain, 
post-rain period,etc) the car bucks, misses,coughs, and generally acts up 
about 80-90% of the time.  This situation only exists under load (idle, no 
load, etc.  is fine) up to about 4000rpm, where it wakes up and pulls 
smoothly.  I've replaced the plugs, cap and rotor, adjusted the valves and 
generally have it running really well (in the dry!).  Before I purchased the 
car, I was looking through the records and noticed one for a wet running 
problem.  I believe they either replaced or torqued down the cyl. head temp. 
sensor.  Unfortunately, I bought the car a few months later and this receipt 
was not in the folder of records.  The ex-owner claims that I have all the 
records and that the car didn't want to run at all before the shop fixed it.
Anyway, I checked the torque on the sensor and also the resistance across 
the sensor (hot and cold).  Everything checked out, even when I sprayed the 
sensor with water.  I did the same for the air intake sensor.  The spark 
plug wires don't appear to be arcing.  I've checked at night in humid 
conditions and neither see a discharge, hear a click, nor go into 
fibrillation when I grab hold of one!  I thought it was the O2 sensor since 
 this is taken out of the loop at full throttle (appr 4000rpm) and I had an 
indecisive idle speed.  Replacing this has given me a nice idle but no end 
to my rainy day blues.
The car has 106,000 miles on it, gets about 27-28 mpg, and has easily passed 
two emissions tests.    Obviously, I've learned to cope with this problem, 
averaging 20K miles a year, but the embarassment is starting to bother me a 
bit.  I'm going crazy and running up water bills trying to recreate this 
problem with a hosepipe.  HELP !!! I don't think sunny Southern California 
can support another engineer, so relocationg is not an option.  Any 
suggestions?

Please - no suggestions that I take it to a shop.

John O. Goodman
goodmjo@hvsmtp1.mdc.com

P.S. Does anyone know if a BMW- or VW-only digest exists and what its 
address is if it does?

Date: Fri,  9 Jul 93 13:53:34 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: Re: Spinning Porsches

[stuff deleted]

| I'm sorry, but you're wrong.  The polar moment of inertia is only
| important dynamically, and has no influence on the steady-state
| cornering ability of a car.  It is a measure of the car's inertial
| resistance to torques about the vertical axis through the center of
| mass of the car.  A moment of inertia is for torque what mass is for
| force (tau = I omega dot vs. F = m a).  A car with a low moment of
| inertia will turn in easier and respond more quickly to steering
| forces.  It will, however, also theoretically be quicker (and easier)
| to spin.  Having raced (and spun) 914s, 911s, and 944s, I can tell
| you unequivocably that the 914 is quicker to spin.  This
| characteristic is independent of the "degree of traction" at each end
| of the car.
|
[stuff deleted]
| Why should the lateral acceleration have anything to do with how
| quickly it spins?  The word from the design engineers at Porsche has
| always been that the 914 handles better than the 911, the 924 handles
| better than the 914, and the 944 handles better than the 924.
| Prepared to equivalent standards, the 944/968 is clearly the
| best-handling Porsche to date.

There are multiple topics here, under the guise of "handling".  All 
voices here have validity in different ways.

From the view of physics and mass acceleration (et. al.), the above 
states the facts well.  From the seat-of-the-pants view about the real 
world, complete with bumps, flexible suspensions, and imperfect 
drivers, how "easy" or "difficult" it is to spin a car can be another 
matter.  The two items are closely related but there's some flexibility 
in the 2nd view, since it is after all, an opinion.

I always have to chuckle at phrases like "handles better", because it's 
so subjective.  My 70+ year old dad likes his large sedan.  While I 
understand & appreciate his point of view, I have a view more suited to 
my interests, and anyone on this list can probably guess what it is.  
So, saying a 944 handles better than a 911 is an opinion, and isn't 
right or wrong.  It reflects the interests of the speaker.

Just 2 cents worth...

		JBS
