Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 14:15:29 -0700
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: struts/springs

Subject: RE: New Owner Question.

> # Return-Receipt-To:ahusain@nis.naitc.com
>   - Is their a way to rebuild the two supports ( it is the little 
> 	shock absorber/hatch support) for the front hood.

>Not really. Those gas struts are sealed units (idiot designers...).
>Aftermarket replacement parts can be had for about $60 a pair.
>
>Now if I was designing it, I'd have a tire valve on the side. Any time
>it stops holding the hood up, just pull up to an air station, apply the
>air hose, and off you go...

*********************************************************************

I went to the trouble of measuring the force of a 911 front hood
strut, as I remember, it was about 60 lbs (used), 
about 80lbs (new).  I used a bathroom scale. 

The way the struts are designed, the strut's effective piston area is the 
cross-sectional area of the rod that extends from the cylinder, I would guess
it is about 3/8" in diameter.

so the effective piston area is about

   3.1416* (.375"/2) = .110 in^2

To do 60 lbs means the operating pressure of this worn strut must be about
60/.110 inches or 545 psi.

And a new strut has to be filled to about 727psi.

This is difficult to get out of an ordinary compressor at a gas station. 
They probably max out at 150psi, so you'd have to go to a dive shop.

To use a conventional air compressor a radically different design would be
needed since the current design would need to be considerably larger to use 
normal air pressure.  With the same design, at 80psi, the piston would need 
to be about 1.12" in diameter to achieve necessary 1 in^2 for the 80lbs force.

The cost of the pump up strut would also go up because a manufacturer
providing a pump up feature would probably be required by liability insurers
to put in a over-pressure relief value to prevent someone from hod rodding
the strut.

I have replaced my share of gas-springs, and am currently using the double-up
method on one side of the hood.  

It failed after a year, so I'll try two on the other side.

However, I still think the gas springs are a clever design.

Date: Fri, 9 Jul 93 19:11:56 EDT
From: boubez@tastesgreat.rutgers.edu (Toufic Boubez)
Subject: Re: 914 rear glass


> Along the same line of 914 glass.
> What collective knowledge do we have on ways to fix a rattling rear window?

The best thing to do is to remove it an install it again using a strip of
good rubber sealant that you can find at your friendly auto parts dealer.
The procedure is very straight forward. After you remove the glass, apply a
rope of rubber sealant all around the contour of the glass and, standing
inside the car (remember to remove the targa top!) position the glass and
apply pressure until it holds. It didn't take me long to do that a couple
of years ago, and it's still holding without any rattles or leaks. Good
luck.

            R 2 4      Toufic Boubez
            |_|_|      boubez@cesl.rutgers.edu
            | | |      Computational Engineering Systems Lab
            1 3 5      CAIP Center, Rutgers University, NJ

All the commentary I've read agrees with the above analysis.
My feeling is that the 928 is just too big and heavy to be in
the same league as the 944/968.  That is not to say it does
not handle well, or its roadholding is poor, which is clearly not
the case.  The 911 is a superior handling car too, but only
if you know what you are doing.  It is entirely predictable,
which is important, but it is not neutral or foolproof, which
must be taken into acccount when discussing "great handling"
cars.

Date: Sun, 11 Jul 1993 01:13:53 -0400
From: johnc@cs.rpi.edu
Subject: Spinning Porsches


   The trailing throttle oversteer characteristic of the 911s is not a  
   result of a high polar moment of inertia, but instead a result of the  
   high amount of rear overhung mass.  Any car set up to be quick on the  
   track will have this characteristic to some degree, however.

I just love armchair physics :) Actually the "spinning" tendancy that is
always atributed to the 911's rear overhanging engine is, well not that
simple. It has alot more to do with suspension geometry than weight
distribution. According to Chaparall-builder and vehicle dynamics expert
Jim Hall:
	"In short it is extremely important to realize that a rear
	engine car is not inherently oversteering, that it is, in
	fact, capable of superior handling. Unfortunately, many people
	intuitively, and by hearsay, believe that the rearward 
	concentration of mass is a controlling factor in cornering
	behavior. This is true only because of the slip angle 
	characteristics of tires. If there were no slip angles, there
	would be no problem. As long as there is a means of controlling
	the slip angle through tire and vehicle design, the "problem" can
	and has been solved."

Or think of it this way: On a rear engine car the centrifugal force
generated in a corner would be twice as great on the 1000lb wheel
in the rear than on the 500lb wheel in the front. But this has no effect
on the limit of friction because the 1000lb wheel is capable of twice
the side force of the 500lb wheel. 



John Colasante		johnc@gdstech.grumman.com








------------------------------

Date: Sun, 11 Jul 93 12:05:48 EDT
From: ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu (Nathan Ulrich)
Subject: Re: Spinning Porsches

I wrote:
   The trailing throttle oversteer characteristic of the 911s is not a  

   result of a high polar moment of inertia, but instead a result of the  

   high amount of rear overhung mass.  Any car set up to be quick on the  

   track will have this characteristic to some degree, however.

And John Colasante replied:
   I just love armchair physics :) Actually the "spinning" tendancy that is
   always atributed to the 911's rear overhanging engine is, well not that
   simple. It has alot more to do with suspension geometry than weight
   distribution. According to Chaparall-builder and vehicle dynamics expert
   Jim Hall:
	"In short it is extremely important to realize that a rear
	engine car is not inherently oversteering, that it is, in
	fact, capable of superior handling. Unfortunately, many people
	intuitively, and by hearsay, believe that the rearward 

	concentration of mass is a controlling factor in cornering
	behavior. This is true only because of the slip angle 

	characteristics of tires. If there were no slip angles, there
	would be no problem. As long as there is a means of controlling
	the slip angle through tire and vehicle design, the "problem" can
	and has been solved."

   Or think of it this way: On a rear engine car the centrifugal force
   generated in a corner would be twice as great on the 1000lb wheel
   in the rear than on the 500lb wheel in the front. But this has no effect
   on the limit of friction because the 1000lb wheel is capable of twice
   the side force of the 500lb wheel. 


Actually, it isn't.  And you don't seem to understand what Jim Hall was  
talking about.  You might want to check a book like Puhn's "How to Make Your  
Car Handle" or Smith's "Tune to Win" for a simple introduction to race car  
dynamics and handling.  I don't want to go into too much detail here, but  
I'll briefly cover the relevant points (much simplified).

The lateral force (or grip) produced by a tire is roughly proportional to  
the normal force (vertical load) at the contact patch.  Unfortunately, there  
are a whole number of other factors as well, including tire temperature,  
slip angle, camber angle, and contact patch pressure (load/unit area of the  
contact patch).  For a given tire, if you double the load, such as your  
hypothetical "1000 lb wheel" above, the contact patch doesn't double, which  
means there is a higher contact patch pressure, which means the grip does  
not increase proportionally to the load.  In other words, all other factors  
being equal, the "1000 lb wheel" will resist considerably less than twice  
the lateral force of the "500 lb wheel."  And the additional grip available  
gets progressively less as you increase the load.

If you have complete design freedom (as Jim Hall did), then the weight  
distribution becomes less important because you can increase the tire  
diameter and width to compensate.  And if you have a very high power/weight  
ratio, you become traction-limited and would tend towards a rearward weight  
bias, with correspondingly larger rear wheels and tires.  See, for example,  
the weight distribution of a current F1, Indycar, or GTP car.

A production car, or a production-based race car, does not have this design  
freedom and is usually limited in the tire and wheel sizes allowed.   
911-derivative race cars have always have the biggest rear wheels and tires  
that could be stuffed under the bodywork (or modified to bodywork to match)  
exactly to compensate for their rearward weight bias.  Unfortunately, as the  
rear wheels get wider and wider, all kinds of other problems crop up, like a  
rear track which is much wider than the front track (bad news for handling).   
Building a race car based on a production 911 chassis requires all kinds of  
compromises, and development is difficult (Stan, am I wrong?).  The  
911-based race cars have been historically successful primarily because of  
their power advantage, and because early on they were racing against even  
more primitive suspension designs (no longer true). 


As I mentioned in my previous message, any car that is set up to be  
neutral-handling on the race track will exhibit trailing-throttle oversteer,  
simply because of weight transfer off the rear wheels.  This can be  
exacerbated by the position of the center of mass, the suspension geometry,  
or many other factors.  Ideally, you would like some controllable  
trailing-throttle oversteer, because it is one way for the driver to correct  
his line through a corner.

Nathan Ulrich
ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 15:38:55 +0200
From: kevinh@hasler.ascom.ch (kevinh)
Subject: Re: 911 Horn failure

> One of the pair of horns on my 88 UK spec 911 has stopped
> working. Does anyone have any advice on how to trace the
> problem without use of a meter of any kind?
> 
> # Ben Evans

Try connecting a simple 12v bulb to the two wires going to the defective 
horn (spade terminals) - if they don't light when you press the horn button then 
the wiring is faulty between the fuse box and the horn. I would guess the most 
likely place other than the horn itself is the big 12(?) pin connector mounted 
on the inner wing on whatever side the defective horn is. This advice is based
on working on my '73 911 - if yours is very different ignore it :-).

kevinh@hasler.ascom.ch

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 09:28:10 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: "Best handling Porsche, etc."


Dear fellow Porschephiles,

If you know me from my 20+ years of being a Porsche fanatic out here 
with Golden Gate Region and Diablo Region of PCA, you'll know I'm no 
shrinking violet when it comes to getting the facts across. 

Point is, it sounds kind of dumb to talk about spinning Porsches and 
which one handles the best. Seems about ten years ago, Tom Green of 
Automotion fame went to some real effort to get his 944 to break 1.0G
on the existing street tires of the day (pre sticky-stuff). He did it
after some extensive modifications to the car. The joke was that Roger
Kraus and I had done the same thing in my A/S 914/6 on Pirelli P-6s in
1979 preping my car for the SCCA Solo 1 series. We didn't think it was
worth the effort to publicize, winning was the true goal (we did).

The moral of this tale is: What ever Porsche you have at hand can be 
made to handle even better than it does, dependant on time, money and 
skill. Don't worry about whether you have a 356 or 928, just enjoy it 
and your fellow Porsche owners. Ask Vic Skirmantis, Chuck Forge, etc.

By the way, a few years back I competed in the Zone 7 autocross series
with the intent of getting a seperate class in production for the 944s 
so we wouldn't have to compete with the big bad Carreras. I figured 
that my showroom stock 944 would get pushed around. Wrong, I won for 
the year and we still have to run with the Carreras. All Porsches have 
a lot more potential than we'll ever need in normal life, we all need 
to study the cars to realize the ultimate potential.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 15:36:23 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Please help with cold start problem.


   Hello there, my 80-911 recently develops this cold start problem.
   The car can not be driven when the engine is cold. I have to put
   a light pressure on the gas pedal to keep the engine idle above
   1000RPM for about 1-2 minutes before I can drive it. The engine
   dies if I release the gas pedal. I suspect that the automatic
   choke must be faulty. Where is this choke? Many thanks for any 
   comment.

Check out the auxiliary air regulator. It is a weird looking alluminum
casting about 4" long and about 2-3" high, with 2 air hoses connected,
one going in one end and the other exiting opposite. It also will have
a typical Bosche electrical connector in it.

It is located amongst a rat's nest of vacuum hoses in behind the engine
top, close to the firewall. You may find lowering the engine/trans
will give you enough room to inspect it carefully.

It allows air to pass when cold, similar to "putting light pressure on
the gas". It sounds like it's stuck closed all the time. It's
supposed to close gradually as it gets hot with the assistance of
12V going through the electrical connector. 

One way to diagnose is to disconnect the air lines and run them
together so you effectively take the thing out of the loop and see
if the cold idle gets better, or else just look through the hole and
make sure it's fully open when cold. 

A worse situation exists when these suckers stay *open* which makes
no problem to start the car but a massive vacuum leak when warm. I
think this is the more common failure mode.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 17:46:05 PDT
From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: Fine Design / Dealer service

	Each time that I open up an area that the local Dealer has had
	his fingers in I'm reminded why I usually choose to do my own 
	service.
Background:
	Last December, my '87 Carrera was broken into via the Driver Side
	window and vent glass being smashed out. I went to the Dealer to 
	replace the glass because it was; a) winter, b) convenient.
	I did not notice that the window frame had two gouges/dents
	from the perps. screwdriver until washing the car later that week.
	(No mention from the dealer). I proceded to the insurance adjuster
	and he cut me a check for the additional work and parts at "Flat Rate".
	I decided to order the replacement parts and wait 'till "more favorable
	weather" to replace the Frame.
	That happened yesterday.

	Upon dissassembly of the door I found several loose (untourqued)
	fasteners which had caused a lot of rattleing internal to the door,
	and unfounded concern that "they don't make Porsches the way they 
	used to". So the deed is done, new frame installed the fasteners 
	are tight and the car doesn't rattle any more!

Now for the Fine Design Part:
	I've never been inside a late model Porsche door before, and I'm
	pleased to say that I'm not dissapointed in the quality of engineering
	even in so humble a part as the Window Frame. The task of replacement
	was easy, and the adjustability and assembly puts all other like door
	assemblies to shame. 
	It is constructed of three different aluminum extrusions, cut, formed, 
	welded, bolted and bracketed in Porsche's typical economy of weight, 
	and designed with a high degree of strength, and high utillity. I 
	recommend that at first opportunity each of you examine this design 
	for yourselves.;{>
	You don't have to confine yourself to merely laying underneath
	your Porsche watching the engine oil drain, you can marvel at the 
	sophisticated simplictiy of the inner workings of your doors.

	Don't shy away from replacing the door glass yourself, as it can be 
	done by any competant backyard mechanic with more time than money! 
	And usually with better results! "You too can earn $65.00 per hour"
	Right Stan?
	Next, "Fix the Door Light Switch Circuit", a continuing saga. 8{>.

John Tilden
Data I/O Corp.
Redmond Wa.

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 15:40:02 PDT
From: Mike Conte <mikecon@microsoft.com>
Subject: Spongy Brakes in 911

My 74 911 brakes have been getting spongier and spongier, even after
bleeding. A similar thing happened with my first 911, an 88 Carrera,
and it seemed nothing we did could restore that original firm feel. On
that car, we bled the brakes, replaced the lines (with stainless),
tried 3 or 4 sets of pads (DS11, AF, and Textar), and replaced the
master cylinder. It would improve, but never to its prior, stock level.
Finally, I sold it...  I was quite happy with the non-assisted brakes
on the 74 when I first got it last September, but now that they are
going soft, I'm loath to start another expensive wild goose chase.

Some history: the car is a basically stock 1974 911 Carrera. I drive it
everyday and at local track events. It isn't unusual for me to boil the
fluid at the track. So I have gone to Motul (300 deg C), Cool Carbon
pads, Kool Brake ducts, stainless lines, and no backing plates. I have
the brakes flushed and bled after every event, and have had it done by
two quite reputable shops. But each time it seems they are a little
softer. If I stand on the pedal at rest, it does not sink, so the seals
all seem fine. Is it possible that some air has gotten into a part of
the system that can't be bled? Or that hard use is deteriorating some
other parts of the system? Thanks

Mike

Porschephiles #449                         Wed Jul 14 09:03:46 CDT 1993
Date: Tue, 13 Jul 93 04:24 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 944S2 Sparkplugs

>There is a technical bulletin, Group 2 9006, that is about sparkplug usage
>for the 944S2.  I have not had time to go to the dealer to review it yet so
>I would appreciate it if someone could give me a summary of what it is
>about. I am considering changing plugs so I thought I should read this
>first.

The bulletin simply states that the 944S2 can be fitted with either 
WR 7 DC or WR 5 DC plugs.  Cars which are street-driven and generally
for short distances should use the WR 7 DC plugs, since they reach self-
cleaning temperature sooner.

>Also I would like to hear any recommendations of brands in comparsion to
>what I would normally use, Bosch Platinums-new style.  I have heard that
>NGK Gold Paladiums work great in 911's. Are they better than Bosch and, if
>so, would the same apply to my 4-valve/cylinder water cooled motor? There
>has also been some discussion of Splitfires. Someone mentioned Splitfires
>being a benefit in wedge-style combustion chamber 2-valve heads.  What
>about my 4-valve head?

Spark plugs are commodity items, and any good name-brand part from Bosch,
NGK, etc. will do just fine.  My mechanic friend prefers the copper
Bosch plugs over the platinum ones, although he doesn't feel there is
any great difference.  I think his preference is based on the fact that
the copper's electrodes are harder to foul, but I could be mistaken.

The Splitfires are nonsense as best I can tell.  Two-valve heads do not
have wedge-shaped chambers.

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 13 Jul 93 04:24 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Manufacture Site and more

>  - Where are 944 manufactured. My VIN number say "N" but I don't have a
>      clue as to what it stands for?

"N" is for Neckersulm, the location of the Audi factory at which all but
the engines were assembled.  The engines were all built in Zuffenhausen.


>  - While going through the 87'944 owner manual I read that there is a
>      10-year factory warranty against rust/corrosion. I was just wondering
>      when was the first time Porsche started these warranty. Since I
>      have not ordered my 85/2 944 manuals YET :-(  I was hoping that some
>      kind 85/2 owner could look it up for me and let me know if it applies
>      to that model year.

The rust warranty is for rust perforation, not for surface rust.  Go
figure...

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 13 Jul 93 04:24 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Oil Change

>1)  I hate to start this but what is the common wisdom about using 
>       Mobile Synthetic Oil in 944.

I recommend synthetic oil in all 944-series cars which spend time at the
track (driver's ed).  I've seen temperatures in excess of 275 degrees
in my 944S.

(The good news is that I just added an oil radiator.  In thirty-minute
lapping sessions in 100+ degree heat Thursday at Bridgehampton, we think
the oil temp is now down around 235 degrees.)

> 2) If not than what type (Weight and Brand) of oil and Plugs should on
>       Use. Is there a FAQ or a summary of the steps/Checks/adjustments
>       required to perform a complete tune-up on a 944.

Use the oil weight and spark plug heat range recommended in your owner's
manual.  Use name-brand oil and plugs.  If you want to learn how to tune
up your car, pick up a copy of the relevant service manual and workshop
manuals from Porsche.

Kevin

Date: 13 Jul 1993 09:24:01 PDT
From: "Graphics Group" <Graphics_Group@qmgate.arc.nasa.gov>
Subject: Re:  Steering Column

 Re> Steering Column
>From: marva4!yves@ucdavis.edu (Yves Starreveld)

>Porschephiles,
 . . . 
>(2) The big contact finger which
>brushes the slip ring on the steering wheel for the horn to work has worn
>through and makes erratic contact.
 . . .

Hmm...  That doesn't sound like my '74, but I'll still put in my $00.02....
The wire that comes up through the column attaches to a finger on the metal
ring which screws onto the steering wheel, under the horn button (the center
thing).  There's a bulbous rubber thing that pushes the horn button up and
breaks the contact, stopping the horn from sounding.  You might check the
wire (mine was mis-located and the insulation got chewed up), and also the
condition of the rubber thing.  It might be worn out, which would allow the
button to rock somewhat and intermittently make contact.
There's at least one Porsche dismantler up in Sacto--they had a stall at the
PartsHeaven swap meet.  You should be able to get the works in the center
from them, although they may not be cheap....
Good Luck!

- --Dave D.

Date: Tue, 13 Jul 93 14:53:40 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Consumer Advisory -- Stevens Machining

Just had a good friend of mine pop over since he was in the neighborhood,
who related a tale I know you'll find interesting, and hopefully instructive.

About a year ago, he purchased a set of racing heads for his 1.8 liter EP 914
engine from Stevens Machining, since they were on the short list of people
who know how to do this sort of stuff right. Well, they charged his credit
card about $150 for the core charge, and about $700 for the heads. They then
sent the heads and he was to pull the old heads and return them for the
core charge.

Well, the new heads were *JUNK*. I was there when they came in -- there were
serious mis-machining marks, the valves didn't seat or seal (even leaked SAE 
30 oil when we checked with that!), intake ports were butchered, etc. *NOT*
good. Certainly not what you expect for serious money. 

So he sent them back, they credited him for the core charge and offered to 
try again, he suggested that he wasn't that stupid, and when they refused to
refund his money, he pursued via the card issuer, who credited him and 
eventually recovered the money from Stevens.

OK, it's a year later -- and *THIS MONTH* he gets a credit card charge from
Stevens Machining, for the core charge!!! On investigation, they just sort of
decided to "slip it through" and see if anyone noticed. I'd heard that they
were in some sort of financial difficulty, but  that's just ridiculous!!!

So, two good reasons to avoid doing business with these guys. Just an FYI.

Stan

Date: Tue, 13 Jul 93 19:48:44 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 differentials

>I am curious what people who are racing 914's are using for
>differentials.  Open, locked(if so, how), or  limited slip?  How do you
>deal with the understeer of a locked or limited slip diff.  Does
>anyone have any experience with the Quaife? (I've gotta find some real
>sponsorship if I go that way)

I use open in my IT racer and welded in the EP racer. The welded diff
is a real handfull, but could be made much better with more engine...
Also use a Quaife in a 911 autox machine. It's *MUCH* better than either
the open diff in my 911SC, or the locked diff in the EP 914.

>The venue I'm considering, believe it or not, is ice racing.  

Cool! I have a good friend who ice races his M5. Not, needless to say,
near Houston... The idea of ice racing a 914 has a certain appeal,
anyway...

>I always used a welded diff in my Datsun 510, but it was a major pain in
>the neck.  Great for the straights, but very difficult to turn.  I'm
>afraid that the 914 would be even worse.  

You can't autox in a 914 with a welded diff. Period. It just doesn't turn
quickly at all, from differential effect. It's made for sliding the rear
around corners. But then, since you have a little more slip at the rear wheels 
when ice racing, you might be able to make it work out OK. And, if you have 
the lattitude, more power (say, 230+ HP) will make a *BIG* difference.... 
Check your local ice racing rule book first!

Stan

Ice racing is neat in that the "run what you brung" mentality is very
strong.  The rules are aimed at safety and professionalism, go-fast car
prep is wide open.  I have personally seen: V8 240Z, V8 510, 510 with
rotary midships, 510 with in-line 6 midships, X1/9 with 1100cc Honda
beside the driver, two-door R12 (ie shortened) with Ford V6, and a
beetle with a 2.4 or 2.5 litre type IV.  I have also seen documentation
of a Saab Sonet with 911 power and a CRX with a GM V6.  Some of these
hybrids are remarkably successful, some aren't.  It all looks like too
much work to me.

Obviously, I have lots of options available to me when I need more
power.  I like the idea of a stock Porsche 6, lots of power,
reliability and the car's balance shouldn't be upset.  I also expect a
large displacement type IV would live pretty well since cooling
wouldn't be as large of a problem as if it were running on the street
in the summer.  Does anyone have any experience with large (2.2 and
up) type IV's?

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 9:15:14 PDT
From: Mike Weihman <mikew@hpsadpe.sad.hp.com>
Subject: water coolant additives

(Ugh--not those pesky water cooled car owners again!!)

I drive a 1980 931 daily.  The car always runs hot- although it has
never boiled over, the cooling system is clearly wimpy, as judged by
the swing of the temp needle on hills on hot days.

I've heard of an additive that one can run in the cooling water either
with or (preferably) without ethylene glycol.  The stuff I saw was a
red powder that you dissolve in the water.  Supposedly it had three
beneficial properties:

* It raises the water's boiling point somewhat (though it does not
        lower the melting point appreciably)

* It "wets" the water so that the coolant has better thermal
        conduction to metal surfaces of the engine and the radiator, and

* It does not lower the specific heat of the cooling water the way
        that ethylene glycol does.


Sounds pretty slick to me.  has anyone used the stuff or read about it?
Are there other ways to keep the engine temp down?  The car already
has a small oil cooler

 -mike

My 87 944 turbo has just under 90k miles on it and has been tracked
extensively for the past 2 1/2 years. Until now, it's been suprisingly
inexpensive to maintain.

I forgot to knock on wood. 

Some time over the weekend, the idle set screw worked its way out of the
throttle body.  It's long gone and there's no hope of finding it. No
problem, I think to myself. It's just a little screw. How expensive can
it be? OK, so it's a Porsche part: multiply by 4, but still, it's just a
screw, right? $10 max. 

Hah.

YOU CAN'T BUY JUST THE SCREW! You have to replace the entire throttle
body! Dealer price: $400. PAP (used) price: $200.  Kempton Bros. (used)
price: $85.  OK, so $85 isn't too bad, but I'll still have to
dissasemble the intake system...

Ah well.  I suspect this is just a prelude to the storm.  At 90K, I'm
still running on the original clutch and turbo. Surely, their
replacement is also imminent...

Late breaking possiblity: The local dealer thinks that the 944
(non-turbo) idle set screw (which _is_ available separately!), or a
similar Audi part will fit ($6). Since I don't have the old part to
compare it to, it'll be tough to know for sure.  Has anyone been down
this road before? Jim? Steve?

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 14:25:01 EDT
From: lupienj@wal.hp.com (John Lupien)
Subject: Re: water coolant additives

> (Ugh--not those pesky water cooled car owners again!!)

Yeah, who needs it. All my best cars are air-cooled except for 3 of them...

> I've heard of an additive that one can run in the cooling water either
> with or (preferably) without ethylene glycol.  The stuff I saw was a
> red powder that you dissolve in the water.

This is "Redline Water Wetter". I haven't tried it yet, but there's been
a lot of discussion about it on the hotrod list. The upshot of it is that
lots of people have gotten very impressive results from using it, up to
20degrees reduction in "run-hot" temperature, and so far no reports of
problems. It comes in two versions: an oily solution that includes some
kind of lubricant, and the dry crystals that do not.

> Are there other ways to keep the engine temp down?  The car already
> has a small oil cooler

Try a BIG oil cooler. Also, it's possible that the system you have isn't
flowing as well as it should. It is possible to have that checked without
spending too much money on it (less than a rebuild after the overheat...)

- --

John R. Lupien
lupienj@wal.hp.com

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 12:00:27 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924 Turbo Cooling


Mike,

I own a 1981 924 Turbo and have had a few things occur with cooling.

First, make sure it's been flushed recently.
Use 20/50 Valvoline TURBO oil or Kendal of the same type. It really 
works and lowers the temperature about 10 degrees F.

Next, get a thermostat and fan switch calibrated for 185-190 degrees F.
This helps the most. 

Ensure you have a 50-50 distribution of coolant to water. Bleed the 
cooling system per the manual or Haynes instructions.

The product you're writing about is made by RedLine and works well on 
track cars, I use it in a big-block Cobra with good results. 

I've stuck with the factory recommended stuff for my car. I still have 
the original turbo and 165 lbs of compression, so I think the switch 
and thermostat will help the most.

On last thing, this applies to all water cooler Porsche owners: Turn on
 the air conditioning on a hot day, set it to the lowest setting. The
dual fans are on when the A/C is engaged, this airflow helps immensely.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 14:13:02 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 differentials

>Actually, I am one of those funny people who reads the rulebook first.

Yeah!!! Read up, people: if you don't get *ANYTHING* else from the
list this week, this is what you want to remember. *ALWAYS* read the
rulebook first!!!

>Obviously, I have lots of options available to me when I need more
>power.  I like the idea of a stock Porsche 6, lots of power,
>reliability and the car's balance shouldn't be upset.  I also expect a
>large displacement type IV would live pretty well since cooling
>wouldn't be as large of a problem as if it were running on the street
>in the summer.  Does anyone have any experience with large (2.2 and
>up) type IV's?

We've done some 2.3 and 2.6 Type IVs, but they don't like heat very much... 
If you'd like to talk about having a race-grade large T4 or 911 engine done
for you, Ghost Racing can do it. Shoot me a note off-line. Or if you 
just want to talk about it, I can keep the non-"secret" stuff in public
discussion on the list...

>What is the prefered method for welding 901 differentials (what type
>of rod, etc) and who is a good source for differentials?  

Hmmm. Best source of diffs is "dead" 901 transmissions. Pleanty of those
around, usually $150-450, depending on how good the rest of the gears
are. If you shop some, you can find 912 and early 911 transmissions, 
which have different gearing than the 914 had. Buying one of those, grabbing
the diff for your welded unit and keeping the gears to customize your
ratios is one of the smartest things you can do. Call your local Porsche
dismantler to see if they have any laying around.

As to the welding process itself, I don't know. The ones we have were done by
H.G. Bieker Engineering, back before we bought the car. BTW, Galen Bieker
is one of the "good guys" in the Porsche performance world. He's never done
anything for me (nor apparently for a number of other folks out there) that's
less than absolutely perfect.

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 13:12:41 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 916/6 Flywheel


To: Larry Rebarchik C/O Porschephiles

Larry and other interested parties:

The 916/6 Flywheel is identical to the 911 2.0 flywheel with the 
following exception. All of the timong marks that are on the front
crankshaft pulley are in the flywheel. This is because the pulley is
almost impossible to see in a 914/6, so there is a port between the
transcase and the engine case that the marks can be observed from on 
the flywheel. 

By having all of the marks transferred from your original accurately, 
you should be able to find a much cheaper used flywheel. Make totally
sure ALL marks are transferred, the 120 degree marks and all advance
(timing marks)

Hope that helps;

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 14:25:58 -0700
From: albrecht@cod.nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: Re: 911 dual mirrors

     Before you go to the trouble of cutting the dash to install
the left/right rocker switch, consider this observation.  My '83
SC has dual electric mirrors.  The view from the right mirror
left a serious blind spot.  A car could be sitting with it's
front bumper even with my back bumper (in the next lane to the
right), and I could not see it in the mirror, even with the right
mirror adjusted as far out (to the right) as possible.  I finally
purchased a stick-on wide angle overlay mirror which solved the
problem.  It was made to fit the Porsche mirror, and looks
original except for being convex.  With this installed, I leave
the mirror adjusted  to the right limit, and can still see the
reassuring bulge of the fender flare in the near corner, so no
remote adjustment is required.

     The only disadvantage of this solution is that I expect the
mirror defroster doesn't work well through the overlay, but
that's not a problem in San Diego.  I saw the ad for the convex
mirror in Panorama.

     I would recommend wiring up the joystick to the left mirror
only.  However I can measure the location of the rocker switch if
you like.

                              Bob

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 15:29:45 PDT
From: collopy@leland.Stanford.EDU
Subject: Re: Torque Units


	Can anyone explain to me how I can convert torque units ? 
I have a 1/2"  wrench calibrated in Newton/Meter (which I usually use) 
and Foot/Lb. My other  wrench is 3/8" and reads in kgm/m and inch/lb. 
Since 1 foot is 12 inches, can I conclude that 20 foot/lb equals 
240 inch/lb ? Is Newton/m equivalent to kgm/m ?
 	Thanks,
 	Bart

Bart,
 
240 inch-pounds = 20 foot-pounds    where pounds is pounds force.

Multiply Newton-meter by 0.102 to get kilogram-meter

Your wrench is probably reading in kilogram-meters

Paul

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 15:39:28 PDT
From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: Re: Lease Termination.

Mike,
	You inquired as to the availability of exceptional bargains at the
	Porsche dealer, due to returned lease cars.

	The sales price and the asking price are two different things! 8{>

	Seriously, what it means is Porsche Leasing overestimated the resale
	value when they computed the buyout price at the beginning of the 
	lease.
	The lease company, has the local dealer offer the returned car for 
	sale, at local pricing, "high Blue Book". The dealer has to make 
	something to pay for his sales effort, so the lease co. probably
	can't expect more than local wholesale (low Blue Book) for his 
	returned car.
 	After 30 to 60 days, if the car doesn't sell, the lease co. probably 
	will sell the car at auction, or to a dealer at wholesale 
	(low Blue Book) to minimize his "flooring cost"(Time value of money 
	invested in the car.)

	If you hold a lease that is about up, offer something higher than 
	low book, you are likely to be accepted.
	No bargains at the Porsche dealer due to lease returns, as the local
	market determines the price today. My lease buyout price was set
	6 years ago. So all it really means is I get to buy my car for a 
	price that is "fair", as opposed to an unrealistic value determined
	by the market conditons six years ago.

	Long Story made long:
	I purchased my car five years ago, as an early lease return.
	Now here is where the bargains "might" be found! Do your own
	calculations to sastisfy yourself!

	The Original lease holder returned the car early and got no money
	back, (In this case a hefty down paymnet to bring the monthly	
	lease price down to what he could afford). I picked up the car for 
	a minimal front end cost, and carried the lease to completion.
	The buy out cost at the end of one year (when I bought it) was 
	essentially the same as the  car's original purchase price, 
	Approx $36K. (loaded 87 911 Carrera with 8 K miles).

	I did some calculating, five years ago, which "conviced" me that 
	I was spending less money, to take the lease, and buyout the car 
	at the end of lease, than to take the car at full asking price 
	with minumum down payment.
	I still think I did OK, as the loan rate I'm geting now is 6%, not 16% 
	and I got the buyout price reduced by 12%.

	Remember,"I'm emotionally attached to my car!" 

	If you are really lookin for bargains, look at regions which are
	ecomonically depressed. The local market determines the "selling
	price", remember Econ. 101? The Porsche market is changing whether
	"we" like it or not.

John Tilden
Data I/O Corp.
Redmond, Wa
tilden@data-io.com

Date: Wed, 14 Jul 93 18:28:36 -0700
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: torque units


>>From reading my torque wrench, which is cal in both N and ft,
>30ft/lb = 40.7 N/m. This would lead me to assume that 1ft/lb
>=1.356 N/m.  

>This is not very scientific though.


>Alfonso


It is very close, if these calculations are correct

  1 lb = 454 grams = .454 kg

  1 ft-lb = .454kg * 12inch * 2.54cm/inch * 1 Meter/100cm = .13838 kg-meters

  Since F = mass * acceleration   

    in this case acceleration = gravitational constant = g = 9.8 m/sec^2

    newtons =  mass (in kg) * 9.8 m/sec^2

  
  so 1 ft-lb = (.13838kg * 9.8m/sec^2)-meters


  1 ft-lb = .13838 kg-meters = 1.356 newton-meters

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 04:41 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Haynes Manual

There is only one Haynes manual for 944-series cars.  It was updated a
few years agom (1990), ostensibly to cover the 944S and 951.  It doesn't,
really, but it is a great source of practical albeit generic step-by-step
procedures.

If you're going to use the Haynes as your principal source of repair
info, at least pick up a copy of Porsche's Tech Spec booklette for your
car, cost <$10.

ISBN is 1 85010 657 6.

Kevin

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 04:41 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: European Car Magazine

>I have been subscribing to European Car Magazine for over a year now, 
>and feel I can recommend it strongly. If you like to tinker with your
>Porsche, it features many step-by-step articles on how to improve or
>repair your car. The magazine is not limited to Porsches, but they are
>featured in every issue. Last issue covered installing Weltmeister sway
>bars for 914,912, and 911s with step-by-step instructions and photos. 
        
I've received it for a bunch of years, and generally enjoy it.  The tech
articles are generally quite good, and really put Pano to shame.  The
coverage of other Euro cars is neat.

However, EC has come under a cloud IMHO in the last year or two.  They
just never seem to find an advertiser's product they don't love.  Zero
filtering as to what is good and useful (eg, sway bars), and what is
expensive and pointless (eg, modified throttle bodies).  Everything is
great, and you really oughta send all your money to Automotion, Neuspeed,
etc.

Moreover, they keep telling people to go put stainless steel/teflon
brake hoses on their daily drivers, and how much they will improve pedal
feel, etc.  Dangerous bullshit, period.  In one such article, they also
sang the praises of Castrol GT/LMA, and, wow, Automotion (I think) just
happened to have this exotic and superlative brake fluid in stock for them
to order along with the go-fast s/s hoses.  Boneheads!

Kevin

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 04:41 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: water coolant additives

>I've heard of an additive that one can run in the cooling water either
>with or (preferably) without ethylene glycol.  The stuff I saw was a
>red powder that you dissolve in the water.  Supposedly it had three
>beneficial properties:

We ordered two bottles of the crystalline form of Redline Water Wetter.
As others have mentioned, the product has been recently given the big
thumbs up by folks on other lists.

The dry form we got includes a water pump lubricant.  I called Redline,
and they confirmed that mixing in at least 20% anti-freeze is a good
idea.  They also sent along some literature on their products.

I haven't used the stuff yet.  I've done a number of things to make my
car run cooly at the track, the most recent of which was to add an oil-to-
air radiator.  I need to re-install the oil temp sender, then do a few more
track days with the current configuration to get data points.  I'll then
try the water wetter and see how things change.

I did give one of the bottles to two other owners at the Bridgehampton
race, one a fairly stock 944S and the other a, um, highly non-stock 951
("The Beast").  They both were running hot, used the stuff, and felt it
helped.  I didn't get really quantitative answers from them.

Oh yeah, if you mix the pink Watter Wetter with green anti-freeze, it
looks like pooh.  Mercedes sells phosphate-free anti-freeze which is
almost clear in colour; you might want to switch to it!

Kevin

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 09:39:05 EDT
From: Mark_Forrester.LOTUS@CRD.lotus.com
Subject: Porsche Cold Start Problems

Folks,

Last night I picked up the July issue of European Car and
guess what? The TECH PROCEDURE article (page 96) describes
the auxiliary air regulator, why it's necessary, and how
it works.  Also there's a section on trouble shooting the
little bugger.

I too recently experienced this problem on my 911SC. In my
case the blocking plate on the auxiliary air regulator was
sticking.  It was not open enough when cold so I needed to
nurse the gas peddle for a couple minutes until things got
warm.  Once warm the plate was sticking in the open
position which resulted in a high idle (~1600). In my case
I did not need a new regulator.  Jerry (at EPE) did an
"adjustment" which took about 10 seconds.  I wish I had
seen exactly what he did to the regulator since the
pictures in July EC don't show anything which is adjustable.
In any case, it's been a couple months now and the problem
has not come back.

Regards,
Mark

email: mrf@lotus.com

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 09:35:25 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: Re: water coolant additives

| > Are there other ways to keep the engine temp down?  The car already
| > has a small oil cooler
|
| Try a BIG oil cooler. Also, it's possible that the system you have isn't
| flowing as well as it should. It is possible to have that checked without
| spending too much money on it (less than a rebuild after the overheat...)

Another factor in a 13 year old car could be that the radiator is 
gummed up internally & externally (clogged fins), so the thermal 
transfer rate is reduced.  You may also have a defective thermostat 
(maybe you already tried that avenue?).  Lastly, the engine itself may 
have an insulating coat of gunk within it, causing poor transfer into 
the water.

What's hot - the engine or the water or the radiator?  The answer will 
highlight your problem area (hot engine, cool water says water pump or 
engine water galleys).

		JBS

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 09:49:28 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: torque units

Previous conversations expressed Newton-meters, etc. as N/m, which is 
wrong.  It should be Nm or N-m, so as not to imply a division of one by 
the other.  30 ft/lb should be 30 ft-lb or 30 foot-pounds.  (that's a 
hyphen, not a minus!).  Golly, high school physics sure comes in handy!

Thanks for setting the terms and formulas right, John!

		JBS

| >30ft/lb = 40.7 N/m. This would lead me to assume that 1ft/lb

|

|

|
|   Since F = mass * acceleration
|
|     in this case acceleration = gravitational constant = g = 9.8 m/sec^2
|
|     newtons =  mass (in kg) * 9.8 m/sec^2
|
|   so 1 ft-lb = (.13838kg * 9.8m/sec^2)-meters
|
|   1 ft-lb = .13838 kg-meters = 1.356 newton-meters

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 09:52:38 EDT
From: ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu (Nathan Ulrich)
Subject: European Car Magazine



Kevin C. Gross <0004056890@mcimail.com> writes:
>Moreover, they keep telling people to go put stainless steel/teflon
>brake hoses on their daily drivers, and how much they will improve  
pedal
>feel, etc.  Dangerous bullshit, period.  In one such article, they  
also
>sang the praises of Castrol GT/LMA, and, wow, Automotion (I think)  
just
>happened to have this exotic and superlative brake fluid in stock  
for them
>to order along with the go-fast s/s hoses.  Boneheads!

I agree about the stainless-steel brake hoses, which just caters to  
their typical reader's interest in a cheap go-fast (or in this case,  
stop-fast) secret.

However, Kevin and I disagree about Castrol LMA brake fluid.  I think  
it's the best thing since sliced bread, and use it in my race car.  I  
have yet to experience brake fade while using it.  This may be  
because I have good front brake cooling and replace my pads when they  
get about half worn, but I have several friends who also use it in  
their race cars with no problems.  Since it costs about 1/3 as much  
as the more exotic fluids, I completely flush my brake system with  
fresh fluid before every race, which costs me about $5.  If I was  
using the expensive stuff, I probably use it more sparingly.  The  
high boiling-point fluids are much more hydroscopic as well.

Of course, you'd be crazy to buy Castrol LMA from Automotion, since  
it's available at most of the discount auto supply chains.

Nathan
ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 10:38:45 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 916/6 Flywheel


   Larry and other interested parties:

   The 916/6 Flywheel is identical to the 911 2.0 flywheel with the 
   following exception. All of the timong marks that are on the front
   crankshaft pulley are in the flywheel. This is because the pulley is
   almost impossible to see in a 914/6, so there is a port between the
   transcase and the engine case that the marks can be observed from on 
   the flywheel. 

   By having all of the marks transferred from your original accurately, 
   you should be able to find a much cheaper used flywheel. Make totally
   sure ALL marks are transferred, the 120 degree marks and all advance
   (timing marks)

Whoops! Are you sure???

While it is true the 2.0 914 flywheel is similar in appearance and the
stuff about the timing marks is true and both are 215mm with identical
ring gears, they are not interchangeable because the 914 2.0 flywheel
has a different mounting to the crankshaft. The 2.0 6 cylinder crank
uses 6 bolts and the 2.0 4 cylinder uses 5 bolts. I haven't looked at
a 4 banger crank lately but I think this is the case, Stan care to
confirm? 

The only other flywheel that I know which *is* interchangeable is the
'69 "S" 2.0 which will bolt up but the timing marks will be *totally* 
screwed up which you can live with.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

information, think "stainless is better", period.  The right braid can 
be great if installed correctly, but some of these aftermarket places 
sell you crappy stuff and don't tell you the importance of installing 
it properly.  You end up with a mushier pedal, and someday have brake 
failure (I've heard of 2 cases of sudden failure when the braided 
stainless sheathed line breaks due to incorrect alignment during 
installation).

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 12:01:44 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: European Car Magazine

>	I have been subscribing to European Car Magazine for over a year now, 
>and feel I can recommend it strongly. If you like to tinker with your Porsche, 
>it features many step-by-step articles on how to improve or repair your car. The 
>magazine is not limited to Porsches, but they are featured in every issue. 

I too have subscribed to European Car for about 5 years, starting back when it
was VW & Porsche Magazine. It's a good publication, with a couple of caveats
that those of you out there who are not familiar with the publications industry
might need to know:

* most of the articles are "placed pieces", written by or on the behalf of
  advertisers. There's nothing wrong with this practice, most of the time,
  but

* many of the tech articles are *WRONG* and designed to motivate you to
  buy something by their wrongness

>Last issue covered installing Weltmeister sway bars for 914,912, and 911s with 
>step-by-step instructions and photos. 

Case in point. The article was specificly on how to install the front bar on 
a 914. It stated (paraphrased) that the 914 really needs a front bar, and
you should put on a big one. My experience is that the front of the 914 
is *REALLY* stiff, and that if you have a stock engine and only street-
grade tires, if you put on even the stock factory bar, the 914 plows like
a John Deere.

The stock 914 really needs the *REAR* bar, not the front, until you start
setting up the car for racing or autox in a class where you have significant
engine modifications *AND* sticky tires.

This bit of misinformation may cause people to go out and drop $300 for
a Weltemeister (hmm. don't think they had anything to do with the article,
do you?) 19mm bar only to have their cars just skate off the road because
they're understeering so badly. *NOT* good.

Now, if the article had been clear as to the circumstances under which you
need the bar, and had maybe even done a compare and contrast between the
factory bar, the Weltmeister bar, and the H & H bar, I'd have been a whole
lot happier with it. As it is, I think the magazine's tech editor needs to
do a *LOT* better job.

Just an FYI. And it *IS* a good magazine -- just don't believe everything
in it!

Stan

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 12:09:35 PDT
From: Patrick Norris <norrisp@Jupiter.SAIC.Com>
Subject: Re:  Sunroof is acting funny


>It has a pop-up switch and sometime the switch works and the roof 
>pops open and sometime it doesn't. 

>Anybody out there with similar experiences? Any helpful hint?

Yes, I know of the problem.  I've remove, dismantled, cleaned and
reassembled the switch and replaced the fuse.  The problems is
still there.  Another member in our region tracked the same problem
to the micro switch in the roof.  It's in the center where the
roof pivots with the body.  

To test this, use a very small screwdriver and try to trigger
the switch.  I hear that replacing the micro switch takes some
time to remove all the trim and replace everything, but it solves
the problem.  I just waiting a little before I take up the job.

Pat Norris

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 12:32:25 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re:  stainless steel brake lines

 All kind of things. They are (1): very easy to damage, (2)
 high maintenance (must be checked frequently). If you bend the
 line (kink it) you can seriously weaken the Teflon (tm) core
 and then you risk catastrophic brake failure at some time in
 the future. The stainless sheath will abrade anything it
 comes in contact with and yet does not, itself, withstand
 abrasion very well. Should a strand come loose it could 
 pierce the Teflon core leading to catastrophic brake failure.
 You cannot inspect a stainless line visually - there is no
 real way to tell whether it has been kinked (say during 
 installation or maintenance of calipers). A classic problem
 occurs when people allow the caliper to hang by the brake
 line. No problem with rubber lines and mucho problem with
 stainless lines.

 Stainless lines were developed for use in aircraft (aluminum)
 fuselages. As such, they were tougher than the surrounding
 airframe and if anything gave it would be the airframe (better
 than loosing control of your control surfaces). This is not
 the case in cars.

 In my sphere of racing (bikes) I advise everyone to use great
 care in use stainless lines. Personally, I've converted to
 using Frentubo (Italian) Kevlar-shrouded lines. They give the
 same or superior feel to stainless and don't have the drawbacks
 associated. Cost is equivalent. 

 YMMV.

 Stef

From: ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu (Nathan Ulrich)

The 944 parts fiche shows the 944 idle adjustment screw apart from  
the unit and with a separate part number.  The 951 unit does not call  
out or show the idle adjustment screw separately, nor does the 944S  
unit.  Based on how Porsche generally does things, I don't think the  
adjustment screws would be the same.

Also, based on the (admittedly rough) drawing of the part for the 944  
unit, it is reasonably complex.  If the 951 screw is the same, it's  
not going to be a matter of buying a metric screw off the shelf, but  
rather having the piece made up.

>>Moreover, they keep telling people to go put stainless steel/teflon
>>brake hoses on their daily drivers, and how much they will improve  
>pedal
>>feel, etc.  Dangerous bullshit, period.  In one such article, they  
>also
>>sang the praises of Castrol GT/LMA, and, wow, Automotion (I think)  
>just
>>happened to have this exotic and superlative brake fluid in stock  
>for them
>>to order along with the go-fast s/s hoses.  Boneheads!
>
>I agree about the stainless-steel brake hoses, which just caters to  
>their typical reader's interest in a cheap go-fast (or in this case,  
>stop-fast) secret.
>
>However, Kevin and I disagree about Castrol LMA brake fluid.  I think  
>it's the best thing since sliced bread, and use it in my race car.  I  
>have yet to experience brake fade while using it.  This may be  
>because I have good front brake cooling and replace my pads when they  
>get about half worn, but I have several friends who also use it in  
>their race cars with no problems.  Since it costs about 1/3 as much  
>as the more exotic fluids, I completely flush my brake system with  
>fresh fluid before every race, which costs me about $5.  If I was  
>using the expensive stuff, I probably use it more sparingly.  The  
>high boiling-point fluids are much more hydroscopic as well.
>
>Of course, you'd be crazy to buy Castrol LMA from Automotion, since  
>it's available at most of the discount auto supply chains.

I also agree with Nathan that Castrol LMA makes more sense for most
applications. I am intrigued that Nathan changes it for every event,
since to me the most significant benefit is that it maintains a good
boiling temperature over a longer period of time. The only way to
gain the benefit of the ultra-high temp. fluids is to change them
very frequently (argueably every event), which is expensive, but
also less likely to happen in anything but a serious racing program.
I believe that the original post was discussing street cars, possibly
under high performance conditions, where monthly brake fluid changes
just aren't going to happen. Under these conditions, Castrol LMA
(or at least that type of fluid) is the clear choice.

I also wouldn't buy it from Automotion, based on the price.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 17:52:55 EDT
From: alfonso@ee.fit.edu (Alfonso Urdaneta)
Subject: torque units

	Previous conversations expressed Newton-meters, etc. as N/m, which is 
	wrong.  It should be Nm or N-m, so as not to imply a division of one by 
	the other.  30 ft/lb should be 30 ft-lb or 30 foot-pounds.  (that's a 
	hyphen, not a minus!).  Golly, high school physics sure comes in handy!
	
	Thanks for setting the terms and formulas right, John!
	

Wow, there sure are a lot of people out there with nothing to
do.  So I used the wrong notation.  I seriously doubt that 
*ANYONE* on this list misunderstood me.

As for references to my education relevant to high school 
physics, I am an engineer.  Are you?


Alfonso.

The Florida Institute of Technology agrees with everything I say.

Alfonso E. Urdaneta III                     alfonso@ee.fit.edu

Date: Thu, 15 Jul 93 22:28:25 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: RE: Plan to buy/lease 1993 968

>> I feel unresolved about this car, get mixed msg. about reliability of 
>> a starter porsche. PLEASE would like to hear from owners/knowers of 
>> 1992/1993 of the 968
>This is a great car!  You should expect very high reliability, since 
>the 968 is based on the 944 which is based on the 924 from 1976.  If 
>you put the car on the race track (which many voices on this wire do) 
>then the reliability will be lower, of course.

Eeeeep! Eeeep! Eeeeep! (that's the opinion alert sounding...)

It also has *ALL* of the maintenence issues associated with the 944. That
means (based on my experience) that it's about 3x as expensive to maintain
over any reasonable period as a 911, particularly an SC or post-'84 Carrera.

If you want a good measure of what you're in for, call several different
service providers and ask for quotes on all of the scheduled maintenence
items (i.e. the 15k, 30k, etc. major maintenence), the annual tune-up,
replacing the water pump (hey, that's a tasty job...) and replacing the
clutch. Just to name some of the things that are going to cost *A LOT* 
more than you're expecting.

BTW, I highly recommend that anyone do that for *ANY* car you're contemplating
buying. Can be a real eye-opener, I guarantee...

>> to GET ot not to GET ...
>GET IT!

Your call, guy. Obviously, none of us picked out these cars based on
anything in Consumer Reports... I say go with what your heart says. As
an FYI, if you get a 911SC in "sad" shape, around here you're talking
$10-14k. Then if you spend another $4k putting it into spectacular mechanical
shape and pretty good cosmetic shape, you'll have a car that you can sell
again in say three years for probably $10-14k... If you get any new
car, you'll take a *HUGE* depreciation hit as soon as the tires hit the
street. And on resale value, look at how the water pumpers are performing
in general relative to the 911s. You owe it to yourself to price a few
used 944s, 951s, 928s, and 968s (if you can find them -- remember, PCNA
only sold about 3600 cars *TOTAL* in the US last year).

>FYI, a local PCA member, Kim Saal, has a '93 RS America and happens to 
>head the Harvard Medical School Cardiac unit (or something like this, 
>I'm not in that profession).  Thought that might interest you, Yasha!?

Not in that line of work either, but I used to be on faculty at Baylor
College of Medicine. Does that count for something???? 8{P

Stan

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 06:05 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: When a $2 screw costs $400.

I've lost the original message.  If we're talking about the adjustment
screw in the throttle body housing, the part numbers are:

  944, 944S: 930.110.919.00, cost about $5.60
  951:       951.110.919.00, cost about $2.50

I am pretty sure these are the right part numbers for the cars.  The
aspiro p/n seems to be unchaged from '83 through the present.  The turbo
part number may have been changed for the 951S, but I didn't check that
closely.  Your parts guy can double check on the fiche for your exact
model.  It's at location 01/07/00 or 01, if memory serves.

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 06:05 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: European Car Magazine

Hermeneutics are everything...

>However, Kevin and I disagree about Castrol LMA brake fluid. 

No, we agree.  If you can get away with it, more power to you.  Great
stuff; I recommend it and use it in the street cars.  My comments were
simply a cynical remark regarding European Car's editorial style.

I'm a firm believer in Don't fit it if it isn't broken, and brakes are
included.  Example: AP introduced last year a six-pot calliper for use by
the F1 cars.  Most of the teams, perhaps all by now, upgraded to them.
We went to the F1 race in June in Montreal, and scored pit/garage passes
and a guided tour of the McLaren and Ferrari garages.  Yes, I died and
went to heaven.  (We also got thrown out of the Williams and Benneton
garages!)

The interesting thing was that all the cars we saw were using the
old-style four-pot AP callipers.  Why, you ask?  The Montreal circuit is
comparatively easy on the brakes.  The four-pot callipers weigh less.

Kevin

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 15:37:04 +0900

Subject: Question on chassis number of '67 911 (early short wheelbase model)

Dear Porsche lovers, 

My friend in Tokyo has bought '67 911 (early short wheelbase model) from US, 
and soon it will arrive at Yokohama port. This car is informed as S-model from 
the dealer, and he noticed us its chassis number as 30599S ("S" at its end !)
and engine number as 960343. 

However, in the pink slip which we recieved recently, it is shown as 305999. 
This difference made us confused, and might cause some troubles at Japanese 
custom and registration. Much worse, some literature shows that chassis 
numbers of early type 911's start from 30501 (five digits), another shows 
that they start from 305001 (six digits), and both shows S-model has "S" 
at the end. 

We thought two possiblities (we have not yet seen its actual chassis number):
[1] 30599S is correct : Probably on the registration, "S" was misunderstood 
                        as "9". 
[2] 305999 is correct : Probably the car has converted from non-S model to 
                        S-model specification.

We would appreciate very much if anyone could inform us any opinions/ideas. 

Thanks in advance, 

Mizuhito Ogawa (NTT BRL, Tokyo Japan, mizuhito@ntt-20.ntt.jp)

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 09:35:02 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Flaming 914's


I thought I might relate a story which I found amusing, as told to me
by a Porsche mechanic about the early days of the 914:

This guy said he was working as a fledgling mechanic at some
dealership in Colorado in the early 70's when a trailerload of shiny
new 914's arrived. After they were unloaded he was told to set them up
in a row and begin to prep them for sale, and let them all idle for
awhile. There they were, all bright and smelling like new cars, a row
of orange, green, yellow, lime, silver put-putting under the golden
Rocky mountain mid-day sun. (You get the picture). Well this guy
walked away for a few minutes and when he came back they had begun to
self-imolate, that is start up on fire, one lighting up the next in
the row, one by one. By the time the fire trucks arrived they had all
gone up in smoke. I guess incidents like this one prompted the fuel
line recall campaign...

Does anyone else have any flaming 914 stories? I have two others which
I will tell you about as long as I hear one from you. If I hear no
others I will tell you anyway, you can not escape them. (unless I am
in your killfile already).

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 10:40:29 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Torsion Bar

> From: Avery Juan  <netmail!avery@ece.ucsb.edu>
> To: Porsche Internet  <porschephiles@tta.com>
> Subject: Torsion Bar
> Date: Thursday, July 15, 1993 2:08PM
>
> I have a 1979 928 and I had it converted to a Cabrio.  I lowered the car
> by putting Eibach front springs and Koni's all around.  With a convertible
> some of the structural rigidity is lost.  I think the stock front torsion
> bar is 25mm and aftermarket ones are at 28mm.  Is this enough and should I
> also strengthen the rear sway bar?

Strengthening the anti-sway bars will transfer more force into your 
chassis, exacerbating the loss of structural integrity.  Generally, 
"softer" chassis should have softer suspensions.

		JBS

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 10:23:14 EDT
From: nabi@boole.att.com
Subject: Re: Sunroof is acting funny


Affie writes:

 | Lately the Sunroof on my 944 has decided to function at its own will. 
 | It has a pop-up switch and sometime the switch works and the roof 
 | pops open and sometime it doesn't. 
 | 
 | Anybody out there with similar experiences? Any helpful hint?
 | 
 | Thanks,
 | Affie.
 | 
 | ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
 | ahusain@nis.naitc.com                  
 | ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
 | 

Oh yes !
My sunroof started acting funny about a year after I got my 944
(second hand). I had it fixed once by the dealer, which replaced
a couple of plastic gears in roof which tend to loose teeth very
easily.

The problem is a fundamental design flaw in my opinion
(by the way, the 968 I think has the same sunroof design ?
not sure though)

The sunroof gets stuck to the molding around the roof cutout. If
you don't use the sunroof most of the time or don't clean the
interface between the molding and where the sunroof sits on top of
the molding, the sunroof gets stuck to the molding and makes it
hard for the sunroof mechanism to push the sunroof up.

Now, the sunroof is pushed up with two curved pieces of metal
that are inside a gearbox mechanism, inside the back of the roof, 
one on either side. There are two white plastic gears that turn
and push these metal pieces out and these two plastic gears are
turned by a rotating metal rod that is turned by a motor
installed in the the rear driver side compartment behind and to the
left of the rear hatch carpet.

The problem is that when the sunroof gets stuck to the molding,
it represents a load to the metal pieces and the plastic gears
tend to loose several teeth when you press on sunroof switch to 
push it open. By the time you figure out what happened, it's too late.

I've changed the plastic gears in my car myself a couple of times;
they are cheap, I think about $2.00 each. And it takes about 1/2 hour
to replace them and you only need a phillips head to do it.
But the rotating rod mechanism in the rear must also be adjusted,
which I have tried but haven't been successful. The 944 workshop 
manual is not at all clear on how to do this right.
It isn't apparently a trivial thing to do and needs more than 1
person to do it. I haven't tried it lately, but I may try it again
someday. For now, I just don't use the sunroof.

nabi
nabi@boole.att.com

Date: 16 Jul 1993 08:38:13 -0800
From: "Graphics Group" <Graphics_Group@qmgate.arc.nasa.gov>
Subject: 1.8L FI

 1.8L FI
    Well, Stan was right.  (Hmm.  Seems to happen a lot, doesn't it?)  I have
fixed my problem with backfires on throttle overrun.  The Decel Valve was OK,
but it turned out that one or two (or all three!) of the vaccuum lines that
attached to it were leaking.  A couple of minutes with the Swiss Army Knife,
and no more backfires!
    Fortunately, none of those backfires seem to have damaged the Air Meter
Box.  Chalk up another one for Stan, those things are tougher than I thought! 
(Maybe that "pressure relief valve" on the flap really works!)
    Now all I have to do is yank the Axiliary Air Regulator and check it...

- --Dave D.

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 09:29:47 PDT
From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: Re: Flaming 914's

John,
	I nearly had a flaming 914! On returning home after a weekend
road trip in my '70 International Orange 914, I backed into my parking spot
at the apartment, killed the engine, got out, and decided to center the
car up  in the slot. Upon atempting the restart, the engine wouln't catch,
and being a curious mechanic I opened the engine lid, finding the engine
covered with fuel. An injector hose had failed, squirting all over the
engine when the key energized the fuel pump, fortunately the injector rail
pressure never got high enough to allow the engine to start.

	A second near fire incedent was averted a few years later, when I
replaced the battery, and was cleaning up the mess of corosion under the 
battery support, I noticed the nylon fuel supply and return lines were
severly crazed. Upon removal, these lines fractured into many pieces, and
most likely would have failed while running, causing the infamous flaming
914. I replaced the nylon lines with copper.

	I saw any number of charred 914 engine compartments at the dealer's
shop in the early 70's.

John Tilden
Data I/O Corp.
Redmond, Wa
tilden@data-io.com

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 11:05:31 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924, 944,968 longevity

Hi Guys,

I'll put my $0.02 in on the 968. I've owned and maintained my own water
cooled Porsches for 15 years. I have seen, with proper maintenance, 924s
and 944s hit 200,000 miles on many instances. The clutches and gearboxes
in good condition also. The key to any Porsche (and any car) living a 
long time is proper maintenance. Kevin Gross will probably agree.
Conversely, I have seen all cars looking like a piece of s*** in a year 
with and owner who dosen't maintain it.

I think the 968 will give over 200,000 miles of service with minimal 
risk just by observing the maintenance manual and changing the oil at 
3,500 mile intervals. The mechanicals are very improved over my 1983
944, all of the original shortcomings in pumps, motor mounts and timing
belts have been resolved to the point of not being a major concern. The
968 motor doesn't share very much with my 1983, the block is improved,
the head is better, motor mounts are different, clutch stronger, 
different gearbox, etc. It looks similar but isn't, like a Carrera 2 and the preceeding Carrera.

As for water pumps and Porsche as a design group, they had a lot of gall
dumping the 1970 911 clutch design on us, the 1973 911T mechanical 
injection, the entire 928, 944 engine mounts, leather dash covers for
early eighties 911s and the most unresponsive customer service 
organization I've ever seen.

You will note I still own three Porsches, the people I meet and talk to
(E-Mail) help find ways to get past the shotcomings of Porsche's fine 
design team and make the marque a lot better.

I purchased my 944 from a young fellow that thought the slight bad 
vibration in the engine was bad engine mounts. I purchased the car, 
pulled the head off to fix the bent valves and decided to fix everything
that could possibly go wrong. That included the clutch, gearbox, water
pump belts and so on. The bill for parts caost me $2,300 on top of a 
purchase price of $7,500 three years ago. I have autocrossed and time-
trialed the car with minimal maintenance, easily comparable to my 914/6
which was retired when I found too much rust from the battery in the 
right rocker panel.

It's easy to forget that Porsche has had 30 years to finally get the 911
to a point of being bullet-proof. I remember the days of sweating the 
ticking timebomb of chain tensioners after an autocross. It took 22 
years for Porsche to get the Carrera tensioner design to us. We have 
a few more years until they have the bugs out of the 944/968 motor by 
that indicator.

One of the bitches that I have about the water coolers is that Porsche 
always has waffled on their legitimacy, despite their being totally 
involved in the design and execution. They seemed pleased with the cash-
register results while nickel and diming the improvements.

So much for my grousing, if you want a Porsche, just pick one you like 
and go for it, I'll be out ther rooting for you to have a good time 
with it.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

it yourself.  I guess that the spring is rather delicate or some such....

something like $250 for a new unit.

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 14:35:55 PDT
From: Patrick Norris <norrisp@Jupiter.SAIC.Com>
Subject: Re:  Sunroof is acting funny



>From: HARTJE, WILL

>My 944 Roof has never released since I 've  had it, although it raises and 
>lowers just fine,  Could this be the same problem? What does the micro 
>switch do? Does it enable you to retract the two curved arms at the rear of 
>the sunroof? Is the micro switch in front or back of the sunroof?


The micro switch is at the front of the sunroof.  This generally has been
a problem with the raise and lower functions.

As for releasing the roof, I had to ask another PCA member for help
with mine: you see, it's all in the key position - the VERY FIRST position
on the ignition is what you want (read the owner's manual VERY carefully).
The person who showed me was very happy to help; he had the same problem
when he bought his 951.

Pat

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 14:31:00 PDT
From: "HARTJE, WILL" <whartje@smtpgw2.az05.bull.com>
Subject: RE: F1 Pits.


F1 Paddock (pit) Passes are not available for sale to the general public. 
You can get them through being a guest of a "Paddock Club" Sponsor, (the 
executive/corporate suites over the pits) or by some connection with the one 
of the teams, it's sponsors, FOCA, FISA, the local race organisation  or any 
other nebulous connection that you can drum up! The issuance is  very 
tightly scrutinized, you have to give positive (photo) ID for your 
personally named pass and the pits are very rigorously policed. The pass 
also serves as general admission to the event for all three days.

Date: Fri, 16 Jul 93 16:01:46 PDT
From: Paul Dennis Collopy <collopy@leland.Stanford.EDU>
Subject: misc. 914 and 944

Hello gang,

You know it is a good day when there are 41 messages in your mail box.  

On the subject of 944 sunroof problems, sounds like I've got lots
of company.  Setting aside the mechanical and electrical problem
for a while, has any one replaced the weather stripping that is
around the sun roof?  Thinking about getting the parts and doing
it myself, but would like to know what I'm getting into first.
Until I am sure the other aspects (mechanical and electrical)
are going to be fixable at reasonable cost I won't even attempt the
weatherstripping.  Is the micro-switch that is being discussed the
rocker-switch on the console or on or near the roof?  Or is it the
relays in the rear wheel well?

Another 944 question, does anybody know how to simply fix the
window control switch?  I am referring to the rocker switch on
the door.  The passenger side works from the driver side control
panel, but not at the site.

On flaming 914 stories, remember too that the injector O ring can
fail and cause leaks even if your fuel lines are okay.  I had
one fail (after replacing the fuel lines twice) on moving day,
just as the movers were attempting to get the 914 into the van.
The day we replaced the injector, the next one failed.  I am
seriously considering replacement of the other injectors just
as a preventative measure.  

On the discussions of water-cooled vs. air-cooled, I haven't heard
any one mention "lemons".  Paul is the original owner of his 944,
and he has maintained his car.  But, the water pump, the radiator,
the broken rear differential pinion and ring gear, the engine
mounts (three times now), the slave cylinder on the clutch (three
times), power steering hydraulic leak, the sunroof, the rear hatch glass 
problem, the switches, the non-adjusting clutch, etc., are 
things we are living with.  The 914 we obtained in 1988.
Fortunately the fuel pump had been relocated, but we still
had to replace the fuel tank, lines, etc., clutch, pushrod tube
seals, pulling the engine for everything, and are still 
trying to get the parking brake working, the windshield re-installed,
windshield washer working, and fighting the never ending rust
problem.  What I'm trying to say, is we all love our cars or
we wouldn't be talking about them, driving them, and modifying
them.  It is unfortunate that emotions are at cross purposes with
economics.  

Pam

Date: Sat, 17 Jul 93 04:23 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Question on chassis number of '67 911 (early short wheelbase model)

>My friend in Tokyo has bought '67 911 (early short wheelbase model) from
>US, and soon it will arrive at Yokohama port. This car is informed as
>S-model from the dealer, and he noticed us its chassis number as 30599S
>("S" at its end !) and engine number as 960343. 

>However, in the pink slip which we recieved recently, it is shown as
>305999.  This difference made us confused, and might cause some troubles
>at Japanese custom and registration. Much worse, some literature shows
>that chassis numbers of early type 911's start from 30501 (five digits),
>another shows that they start from 305001 (six digits), and both shows
>S-model has "S" at the end.

Mizuhito -

I know nothing about this stuff.  So I'll try to answer the question for
you.  According to Porsche Cars of North America's 1993 Fact Book, the
engine serial number 960343 would come from a '67 911.  That car would
have had a chassis number in the range 305101 through 307350. There was
an "S" appended to some other '67 cars' chassis numbers.  However, they
all started with a six-digit number, eg, 305101S.

Brett Johnson, in his "The 911 & 912 Porsche," gives different information.
According to him, a car with this engine was a Model Year 1967 car
manufactured in 1966.  It would be a 911S, engine type 901/02.  The
chassis number would be numbered in the range 305101S through 307360S.
Johnson's information is more detailed, and I would tend to trust it
better than the PCNA Fact Book.

One thing your friend can do is contact the Porsche factory for the Cardex
information on the car.  He should be able to get correct chassis and
engine numbers from the car itself, and the Factory can verify whether
or not the two were one at birth.

Kevin
