Date: Sat, 17 Jul 93 23:14:38 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Plan to buy/lease 1993 968

>Ah, but you so rarely need to remove a 944 engine, that's the beauty.  You
>can do the bottom end and the top end with it in the car.  Moreover, the
>engine comes out the top, not the bottom, I believe.

Nope. Just helped do a full rebuild. The motor comes out from the top,
*IFF* you do it piece at a time. (i.e. ancillaries, then head, then
block.) It *MOST EMPHATICALLY* does not go back the same way. At least,
not without removing the radiator and most of the front end. 

And the factory manuals say "insert from bottom" and there's a special
tool you can get from Assemacher AG that fits on a floor jack just for
inserting 944 engines (used with a lift, but hey...)

BTW, it took nearly as long to get the engine back in the car in one piece
as it did to disassemble it piece at a time. It was *NOT* a fun experience.

I did it as a favor to a friend, but like the last time I helped this guy
with a 951 clutch, I've learned that once is about twice too many times...

Stan

------------------------------

Date: Sat, 17 Jul 93 23:17:42 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Injector cleaner

>What is the BEST fuel injector cleaner...

It depends. In the car, I pour about 1:4 xylene in the gas tank, but then
I don't have to worry about cooking catalytic converters. BG also makes a
*VERY GOOD* product, and the LM product called "Ventil Sauber" is also good.

Out of the car, I have a pressurized pulse stand that I run trichlor or
carbon tetrachloride through, about 20 minutes per injector.

*NOTHING* works better than that!

Stan

Date: Sun, 18 Jul 93 03:38 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: S/S brake hoses

I made a statement about "stainless steel" (steel-braided teflon) brake
hoses being bad a few days ago.  Stefan neatly summarized a number of
the reasons for such a statement.  I posted some time ago an article
Bruce Anderson wrote for the Dec '91 Excellence.  Here it is again:

(Dom: forgive me for filtching this.  I hope it induces everyone to
subscribe to your excellent mag.)

_________________________________________________________________________

A Word of Caution About Replacement Brake Hoses

Stock rubber hoses are fine for a street car.  In fact, I prefer them. 
They are a low maintenance item and very seldom fail.  Their most common
failure mode is swelling from old age or contaminated brake fluid.

The Teflon braided hoses are designed for race cars where they will
receive heavy maintenance and frequent inspections.  All of the successful
race cars in the world use these braided steel hoses.  However, if you get
an inferior product, or if they are improperly installed, you can create a
worse problem for yourself than the one you think you are solving.  There
is probably no more frightening experience than having brakes fail, so be
very careful and check out the fit of your aftermarket brake lines very
carefully.  If one fails, the results can be tragic.

I know, I know, a lot of people use steel lines for their street cars.
If you feel you must switch to the Teflon braided steel lines, be sure
that they are dash three size hoses.  The dash for size that was some
common a few years ago on the aftermarket actually causes a spongy brake
pedal.  When you install stainless steel brake lines, make sure they match
the same position and follow the same lay that the original rubber lines
did.  Lower the car after you are done to inspect the lines making sure
that none of them are in a binding situation.  There should be some slack
in the brake line in every condition.  If there isn't slack, reposition
the line until there is some.  Recheck the brakes lines after you have run
the car for a while to make sure the lines are free and not kinked.

The problem that I have seen with these brake lines over time is that they
become stiff and try to loosen themselves up and cause a leak or become
brittle and break where the fittings are swaged on.  I have seen and/or
heard of quite a few of these braided steel Teflon brake lines failing over
the years and I only remember seeing one stock rubber line thathad failed
so I have to recommend against steel lines for street use.  For a race car
they are fine because race cars receive a very high level of maintenance.

_________________________________________________________________________


I would add that you should check the lines from lock to lock, on the
ground, at at least try to imagine addition slack needed as a corner would
be loaded out on the track.  Have a friend double-check.

If you secure the front s/s hoses to the strut with cable ties, be sure to
first wrap the s/s hose with, say, a section of slit fuel hose.  The edges
of some cable ties are very sharp and will wear quickly through the steel
and Teflon.

In my track experience, changing to s/s hoses netted me absolutely zero
benefit.  What has helped is:

 - use the brakes less,
 - brake cooling scoops and ducting,
 - higher temp brake fluid,
 - pad compound choice,
 - sufficiently thick pads and rotors,
 - front/rear proportioning valve adjustment (esp. in 914's),
 - eliminate knock-back from pad taper, loose bearings, and
 - bleed, bleed, bleed!

My personal set-up right now is stock callipers, Cool Carbon blue pads,
AP 550 fluid, and Holbert Cool Brake coolers.  This is on a street 944S
which has not be lightened.

Kevin

Subject: RE: Injector Cleaner



> out of the car, I have a pressurized pulse stand that I run trichlor
> or carbon tetrachloride through, about 20 min per injector.

> *NOTHING* works better than that?

  This is probably true.....however for those who don't know trichlor
is nasty stuff and one should take the proper precautions not to
breath it in.  This stuff can collect in the liver and cause health
problems down the road of life.  

I work in the aircraft engine industry and we use Trichlor for cooling
purposes and any time there is a Trichlor leak everyone evacuates the
test cell until the leak is fixed.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 05:57:02 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Injector cleaner

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: Injector cleaner

If your question pertains to a fuel additive (versus a spray or dip cleaner),
I recommend Chevron's Techron (no, not because I work for them)... Through my
own experience as well as friends (plus a little inside info). The fuel
additive really works. The compound is a Chevron developed product that is
sold to many other major oil Co.s as well as additive marketers for rebranding
into there own product lines. If you want to be sure of what you're buying,
get the Chevron branded stuff.

By the way, follow the directions exactly -- this is not a case of "more is
better". The effectiveness is actually REDUCED if too much or too little is
used. Also, if your deposit build-up is bad, you may need more than one
treatment. If this is the case, do not treat successive tanks. A reaction is
started with the first treatment that will continue for several tanks of
non-treated fuel. Best is to skip a few tanks in between treatments...

By the way, Porsche recommends Techron by name.........

 -Scott

From: porschephiles-request

Seriously, I'm out in California, in the SF Bay Area.  The regs are rather
stiff here.  If the "emissions systems" (which includes all the induction and
fuel stuff) have been "tampered with" (read: modified), then there is
_no_limit_ on what you must spend to make the car comply.  Basically, carbs are
illegal, without special dispensation or a Smog Check mechanic who doesn't pay
attention.  And the special dispensation can be rather difficult to get.  And
they may change their minds at any time.  (sigh!)

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 10:53:33 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Self-service 928


A fellow wrote about purchasing a 928 and doing all the work himself,
a little something to be aware of:

The four-cam 928 motors must be removed from the car to remove the heads
and perform any upper-end work. The 2-cam 2 valve heads are removeable 
in the car. This operation is about a 10 hour job each direction for the
four cam car. They are very cheap when they require engine work.

For the people that wonder about which way the 924/944 etc engines come out, the drivetrains are installed from the BOTTOM of the vehicle on 
the assembly line, this is why certain locations can never be accessed 
in the car. The 924/944 engines will come out of the car from either 
the top or bottom of the car, remembering that there are a number of 
items which must be disconnected and/or removed in either case.

Have fun!

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

I'm not sure what state you're in.  In MA my 70 914 has been exempt from
smog testing for a few years now.  I could get it to pass though, although
in MA the fact that I've changed to carbs is not a problem.

Regarding FI vs carbs, my problem with the FI is simply that mine was so
old.  I think that if everything works perfectly the FI may run better
than the Webers, although I'm not quite sure of the top end power.  Would
definitely run better in various anbient conditions.  With all those 20+
yr old gizmos and hoses, I doubt whether my FI could measure up to the
carbs without a serious amount of attention (read time & money).  For the
few hundred bucks and days worth of work to put the carbs on, the car has
been a completely reliable performer for the past 8 years.  Besides, I
love the way they look, the way they sound, and the feel of the throttle
response.  Very different from FI.  More like the way I envision a 356 to
drive.  just my opinions, though.  Either way, a fun car.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 12:15:54 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924 Turbo Bearings

RE: Paul's message of 19 July.

Paul, pull the motor. It's a time-consuming, but not too difficult a
process.

You will have to disconnect the bell housing to remove the clutch and 
flywheel anyway, it's easier to pull the motor with the crank and 
everything in it. The issue here is that your crank is scored and needs 
machining. You will find that the top land on your piston is probably
distored from the heat and there are scores in the cylinder walls. The 
oil pump also has to come off. 

A more pleasant note, if you remove the engine and do your disassembly-
assembly outside the engine, the turbo stuff goes on more easily. Trust
me on that one. 

There's more bad news, you'll want to find out where the particles 
came from, just pull it down and check your cam and followers and 
examine them for wear. You'll also notice wear in your valve guides.

you'll lose the bearings in short order if you just do just that. No one
said these things were cheap and simple.  Do it right and you'll be
rewarded with a nice running car.

If any one else is reading, a 1979-80 924 turbo can be enhanced if 
you're about to rebuild by using the 81-82 8.1:1 compression pistons.
They cost the same, you get more in town driveability because of the 
half point of compression.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 20:06:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: Report on the 'NEW' BFG R! 224's!!!

Item Subject: Report on the 'NEW' BFG R1 224's!!!
Well, I finally did some testing on the NEW R1 224 Road Race compounded
tires:

Car-             Front Engine/Rear Drive  Weight: 2300 lbs
                 Limited slip:  Front/Rear Sway Bar
Wheels:          7x15

Tires:           BFG R1 224's   Tire Pressures:  F: 38 lbs  R: 36 lbs
                 205x50x15's
Comments:

These tires are incredible!  I'm a YOKOHAMA Fan, but these tires are Very
fast. Comments have been made about the way the feel mushy upon initial
turn in (you turn the car in- then the tires react comments...)  Well, I
think the secret to these tires is running the right sized rim for the tire
mounted.  In the past, I would have mounted these 205x50x15's on a 6" rim.
This would have given, as "The Real" Roger Johnson commented: "... the car
would turn in then the tires would catch up!"

Having mounted them on 7" rims, this really stretches out the tire and makes
the side wall very square... This greatly improves the sidewall stiffeness
and make turn in response, IMHO, quicker than the RSII's!!!

And, on top of this, the learning curve for these tires is very steep(quick)!
In no time, I had an in-experienced, never have AX'd, beginner driving the
car at its limit, **SAFELY**, with incredible car control!  I got in it
and drove immediately, what I feel, quicker than my RSII's.  I then took
the car out for some HIGH SPEED (read:tight Mountain switch-back's) and found
them to be very forgiven at the limit and definitely easy to 'read' at this
limit!!! I did get the front to break away slightly, but it was easliy
brought back into shape by simply modulating front tire loading (squeezing
the brakes slightly to transfer load to the front of the car).

All in all, these tire are, simply put, incredible (and,after 130 miles
of low speed/tire scrubbing and high speed drifting: the tires are not
even broken in yet!! See my comment on Ken Mitchel's wear data at the
Wendover NT event using 224's!!).

It could be an interesting '94 if BFG's AX compound tire is as good as
their current RR (224) tire, just much stickier (and I hope they solve
their wear compared to the RSII's!!!!!).

I'll use these at Driver's schools at Portland/Sears/Laguna Seca/Seatle,
as well as a few AX's until I get the October '93 release of the AX
compound tire.  Then I'll do a comparison with my RSII's (still VERY,VERY
fast with GREAT WEAR(!!!!)), so I'll keep you all informed (if you care!)

G'day:

Mr Wizard

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 14:49:48 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 911 Salvage Title

>	I'm looking at picking up a project car to do a complete rebuild to,
>and have pretty much decided on a 1978+ 911. The rebuild would include a
>conversion to a convertible c2 turbo look. Since most of the car I will be
>chopping up - I've pondered whether a salvaged title 911 would be all that
>bad. I have a line on a 1979 targa - hit hard in the front that would be
>cheap. I have access to full frame straightning equipment,mig etc so the work
>does not bother me. The question I have is whether even if the car is
>completely and professionly rebuilt, would a prospective Porsche buyer be
>put-off by a salvage title. I would do a step-step photo shoot of the rebuild.

Answer is: (come on everybody...) "it depends". First things first -- if you
don't have a Hunter or Bosch frame machine, and the factory measurments for
re-aligning the tub, forget it. I've been through this on my personal car. You
can't pull it out "close enough" like you can on a Chevy truck or something.
It pretty much has to be on the money, or you lose *BIG TIME* when it comes 
time to set up the suspension. This *IS* a very high performance sports car,
right?

Second, dealing with a car on salvage title. There is, in most states, a 
mechanism for moving a car from a salvage title to a "real" title. You should 
check with your DMV about that proceedure. Doing this typically involves a set 
of state inspections, to make sure that they're not giving you a title for your 
refrigerator or something. Once you have a real title, who cares? *EXCEPT*
that one of the first questions I ever get asked when selling a car (after "how
many miles does it have") is "has it ever been wrecked?" How you answer that
is a matter for you and your consience. I have always told the complete truth
on my cars.

Third, value. Here's the *REAL* "it depends" part. Ghost Racing raced an MR-2
that we bought on a salvage title, rebuilt, re-titled, and after its retirement
as a competition car, sold it to our crew chief who has put another 30k miles
on it. No loss of value, because it was going to a knowledgible consumer. We
also have a 911 that was the 1990 SCCA Solo II champ in F Prepared. It's 
built out of two different tubs, welded together. On a salvage title. There's
probably $15K + just in the engine, another $4k or so in the suspension
and wheels. It is *NOT* street legal, nor could it be made street legal. But
what's it worth to a competitor? For that matter, is it worth anything to a
non-competitor??

Where you lose on value is when you try to sell the car as a "perfect" or
"near perfect" car to an ill-informed consumer. If I knew a car had been
in a heavy collision, it would not deter me from buying it -- but it *WOULD*
change the way I investigated the car. I'd want to have the tub checked, to
inspect *ALL* the mount points for suspension and drivetrain, to check the
fit and finish a bit closer, etc. And if possible, to talk to the guys who did
the work if I didn't know them. Then I'd pay a fair price for it. However,
if it didn't pass my inspection, I wouldn't buy it. And a bargain basement
price wouldn't make any difference at all.

Finally, conversions and updates. When you change the car from stock 
configuration you have truely stepped into The Twilight Zone. There is 
*NO* rule for value, *NO* benchmark, *NO* standards. What you can get
for a "conversion car" is strictly a matter between you and the buyer.
I've seen people lose their ass *BIG TIME* on such deals. For example
we joke that the VP of the local Porsche Club has the "Worlds Most Expensive
$8,000 Car". It's a '69 911E that has been, ahhhh, modified. To look like
an early 70s S, with a fake Marlborough Racing look, a tail that could have
never come with the car, a bizarre custom interior (including alligator 
inlays on the dash!), an "updated and modified" engine, polished and chromed
wheels, etc. I really don't know how much he has in it, but I do know
what it's really worth -- about $8k, unless he finds someone who wants
*THAT CAR*. Then who knows what it's worth? He's turned down offers in 
the $20k's, but on occassion has tried to sell it in the $15-18k range
and failed. Must vary with the wind, etc. 8{)

The exceptions to this rule are conversions to factory spec for that
model, e.g. putting an electric cab top on a '87 targa, using all factory
parts in a manner that is indistinguishable from a factory installation. Then
it would be treated like a factory cab, value-wise. Of course, there are
*STUPID* things you can do that way too -- I know of a pre-A 356 that was
in very good all original condition which was hacked to add a factory sunroof
top clip, and new paint and interior. Value dropped precipitously due to
destruction of an original car in original condition.

>	I think 60 Minutes did a take on salvage titles that killed their
>resale value. Comments/Opinions appreciated...

Hope I added some info you didn't have here. Or at least confused things in
an interesting way...

Stan

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 13:24:55 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re:  What can be done w/engine in car (was: ..buy/lease 968)

 If you have heavily scored big-end bearings then you should ask yourself
 where that material (that once was part of the bearings) has gone?

 It generally trashes your oil pump and ends up in the pan's filter
 screen (after having trashed the cam tower bearing surface as well).

 I'm afraid that your only sensible option is a complete strip-down,
 check and re-assembly. Otherwise I think you're driving a timebomb.

 S.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 15:47:49 EDT
From: Mark Sirota <mark@greenwich.com>
Subject: Re: 944 Breakin Via Door Handle

Lee Chen writes:
>  Today I had the dubious pleasure of chasing a would be radio and/or car
> thief with a makeshift cudgel after witnessing him break into my 944S.
> 
>  Afterwards, I noticed, upon close examination, that the perpetrator
> utilized his screwdriver to poke under both door handles to release the
> lock (leaving gaping holes in the door skin (and, later, his skin too)).
> 
>  A long while ago, I recall reading about a product in VW & Porsche
> magazine that allegedly prevented this means of gaining access.  Some
> chromoly (chromium-molybdenum) plates or something that could be used on
> 924s/944s/VWs.  Unfortunately, I dismissed that ad at the time because I
> was quite familiar with the boulderdash that gets printed in that rag.
> 
>  Has anyone actually used this device?  If so, is it effective?  More
> importantly, what is the address and phone number for the manufacturer(s)?

1-800-ADP-LOCK.  They're called Armor Door Plates, and they're well worth
the money.  Any water-cooled VW owner can tell you...

These are much better than the other versions.  These are not just flat
plates; they have flanges that go into the door so they can't be bent and
the screwdriver cannot reach the release lever, and they're made of
hardened steel.  Others are not as hard, and are just flat.

They're about $50 for two, $95 for four.  They come with all parts needed,
and take about 30 seconds per door to install.

Highly recommended.  I have no affiliation with the product other than as
a satisfied customer.
- -- 
Mark Sirota, System and Network Manager
Greenwich Associates, Greenwich Connecticut
mark@greenwich.com, (203) 625-5060

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 17:20:56 EDT
From: Dan Solis <dan@inset.com>
Subject: Warm starting problem with '77 924


    I`ve had my `77 924 for the last four years and have had a problem
that will not go away.  (I've only been on this mailing list for the last
three months, and so I appologize if this has been discussed before.)

	- When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
	- When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.
	- When the car has been driven, and then let sit for
	  45 minutes to an hour, it sits and cranks for about 20 to 30
	  seconds before kicking in.

    The starter is going like a ball of fire -- but the engine just doesn't
catch.  It always starts, but it's real embarrassing sitting there while it
happens.

    I don't know much about car engines, but the mechanics I've taken it to
seem to have replaced almost everything except the air in the tires, but
to no avail.  Three different garages have worked on it -- but it persists.

    We started out by replacing the injectors, on the theory that
they were leaking and air was getting into the fuel lines.
We then replaced the fuel pump with a more recent version that had a valve
that keeps the fuel from going back in the wrong direction.  Both these
attempted fixes seemed very reasonable, and were suggested and performed
by a garage that works only on Porsches.

    I've met two other 924 owners with the same problem.  One of them had
the problem go away when he replaced the head gasket.  I've since replaced
my head gasket, but the problem didn't go away.  The other guy was
a guy I met at a gas station while we were both filling up with gas.  His
car had the exact same symptoms, and he didn't know what to try either.
I don't know whether he ever solved it, since I've never seen him again.

    Some of the other things I've had replaced are:
	- fuel accumulator
	- rotor
	- ignition wires
	- filters (all)
	- techron (I have no idea what this is)

     Has anyone else seen this problem?  More importantly, has anyone
solved it?  Since nothing else has worked I guess I COULD go try changing
the air in the tires?!

     By the way, I've also had the gas fumes problem that was recently
under discussion.  I long ago learned to live with that by just not
filling the tank all the way.

     Thanks.  Any suggestions would be appreciated.  I will, however, be
out of the country for about a week and a half starting on July 29, and
will therefore not be able to answer mail during that time.


- ----------------------------------------------------------------------------

Dan Solis
The Instruction Set
Waltham, MA
dan@inset.com

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 17:08:36 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Of 924s and Men (women ,too)


Hi gang,

It's me again. I see some activity around 924s and 924 Turbos, here are
a few comments based on 15+ years of owning one.

Normally these things are bullet proof, the cars tend to go to hell 
mechanically when the oil isn't changed and driven in hot, stop and go 
traffic ( nothing else does well either). The cooling systems work well
unless your typical trip to work is less than ten miles. Then the system
never opens the thermostat and the coolant boils, making the water pump 
seal fail early and lots of rust in the block. This is a common 
occurance in the San Francisco area. 

Improper oil changes, wrong viscosity, traffic jams all cause the cam to
go away, the lubrication tube `cokes', stopping oil from getting to the
cam, then the lobes and followers wear. the metal will eventually 
destroy the bearings, then the crank. 

Turbos hate being idled for long periods as the oil flow from the pump 
is low and insufficient to cool the turbo. If you're stuck in traffic, 
turn on the airconditioning, it flows enough air to keep the turbo cool.

One thing a lot of 924 (and 944) owners do is leave off the gravel 
shield under the radiator, It is very important, this directs the 
airflow through the engine compartment and makes the radiator draft 
correctly. The little grill infront of the radiator directs air around 
the lower spoiler, restricting the amount into the radiator. Removal of it adds a significant amount of drag, but adds to the cooling of the 
car. This can be experimented with at the track, SCCA never noticed that
the two top slats were missing on Barney Gardiners 1981 SSA 924. It 
cured a small overheating problem.

Cracked exhaust manifolds. They crack. Cure: Replace with the late 1980 
and later stock manifold and down pipe. Those are good for 5 hp and at 
least 8 ft-lbs of torque. Also pass the California visual on earlier 
cars. Headers are a mixed bag. Most aren't worth the trouble as one has
to tune the CIS to work correctly. The best exhaust is a stock one with
the cat removed for `track testing'.

Ignition, the stock unit recurved to euro-spec (no vacuum retard) works
best, the curve is in the Haynes manual. Again, Those of us in 
California have the sceptre of the Smog-Nazis for the visual inspection.
On the mid 1980 and later with oxygen sensor, there is an idle 
stabiliztion box mounted with the ignition module on the left inner 
fender.  these have been known to go bad, be aware.

Cams, there's only one stock one that works, if you have an early (pre
1977 1/2) you will have the smog bastard. Put in the later cam and get 
10 hp, it's the same as the euro unit.

If you're interested, I have an article I wrote for Excellence on 
suspension for the 924.

For now,

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

If you want to find their value, call Robert Wood Inc. (310)473-6649
(I have no affiliation with this company other than the fact they've taken
my money...) and ask them how much
it would cost for the ones you have.   You can vary their value depending
upon condition (scratches, dings, etc.).  Stock Porsche wheels are NOT CHEAP.
I bought a set of 16" C2 wheels for race rubber on the RS America from Robert
Wood and they were expensive, but worth it.  In hindsignt, probably should
have bought some Revolutions (multi-piece lightweight) but I couldn't wait
for > 6 months for the gnomes to make 'em :-(.  


If you intend upon doing any sort of racing (autox or otherwise) it's VERY
advantageous to have an extra set of wheels.  If you want to drive it on
the street and autocross it, you can buy yourself a set of sticky tires, 

put them on the stock wheels and put your street tires on the aftermarket
wheels I assume are currently on the car.  


If you intend upon selling the car again some day, having the factory wheels
would probably add to its value.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 22:50:44 EDT
From: rmohan@afit.af.mil
Subject: RE: 928/911 questions

RE:  928 problems.

	Not aware of any mechanical failings common to 928's.  One reason is
lack of sufficient numbers built to establish trend data.  Another is it`s
always been Porsche's flagship (fact which escapes Americans, but not Germans)
and designed/built accordingly.  There are problems, however:

	1.  Complexity in maintenance; ie. removing rear deck carpet and
	spare tire, just to reach battery for a jump start.

	2.  Rare cars = rare parts = low numbers = high capital costs =
	high prices for new parts

	3.  Mind-Wobbling Depreciation:  presently exceeds $1000/month for late
	model 928S4/GT's

Recommendations: (strictly my opinions)

	Look for '86+ 928's (32-valves, ABS), less than 5000 miles/year,
	re-sellable color (red, white, black, maybe silver), 5-speed.
	'86: $15,000-    '87: ~$20,000    '90: ~$27,000

RE:  late-model 911's w/supertrapps.

	As far as headers/heat exchangers go, if you want heat,  SSI's are
	the hot set-up.  If you want to make use of the improved flow,
	you need a new DME chip (ie. Autothority), and a properly-flowed
	muffler (Alan Johnson, San Diego has a popular design).

	As for supertrapps, I use them with 1.75" MSDS headers on my race 
	car (no heat).  They needed custom flanges to mate to supertrapps.
	SSI's would have the same problem.  You would need to design/build
	a stainless steel interface.

	Supertrapps are the double-edged sword.  They are tunable by 
	choosing the number of pressure relief discs on the end.
        I run 8 discs to minimize backpressure and increase high-end power
        at the expense of low-end torque.
        But, I have yet to find someone who has done acceptable R&D on
	how many discs, what size motor, how many HP, how many dB, etc.
	This obviously requires a dyno.  If anyone out there has done
	this, please let us know.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 17:31:32 EDT
From: frankc@teleng.eng.telxon.com (Frank Ciotti)
Subject: Re: Exhaust Modifications

> 
> Any experience out there with upgrading performance with SSI or Pre '74
> exhaust/heat exchangers on late model 911s, in particular post '84s?
> What about mufflers? 
> Anyone use SuperTrap on street or track? one or two?
> Contemplating sources for additional POWER!
> An accelleration junkie!
> 
> John Tilden
> Data I/O Corp.
> Redmond Wa.
> tilden@data-io.com
> 

John, 
I just put SSI's on my carbuerated 2.7L.  The cars runs much better now.
no more popping or flat spots!  Sounds much better too, especially in 
the 2000-3000 range.  Performance is improved however I have no numbers
for you.  Anyways, if you decide to put the SSI's on, be sure the get
the correct styles.  The options are as follows:

	Thick flanges - Cars w/thermal reactors & possibly others
	Extended tubes - These will allow installation on 77 & later
			 911's w/915 transmission clutch assist spring
			 without bending the weld seam on the exchanger.
	Gaster Savers - '77 2.7's, '80-'83 3.0's, all 3.2's, & all Turbos
			These are the sleeves which fit up into the 
			exhaust port on the head.

You should probably call SSI (415-883-2000) and talk with them.  The 
people there are very knowledgeable.  Especially John.  They do not
drop ship so you will have to go through a distributor.  Send mail
if you have installation questions.

last time I had a 4-cam engine around, it was a $30K plus investment.

That is a dual ignition. Brain fade is keeping me from remembering
for sure, but I think it's a dual plug affair as well.  That twin 
distributor was responsible for much of the problems with the 
engine -- it's bad enough to synch dual two-throat carbs in '50s
technology, but to try and *mechanically* synch two distributors
as well was a bit much. Interesting article on this on either the 
June Excellence or a recent Pano.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 23:03:05 PDT
From: montys@mapme19.pen.tek.com
Subject: Re: Problems with 928's (LJWILLIAMS)

There are a number of known areas to watch out for.

Watch out for high-mileage sticks that need synchros.  This'll run you
around $1500.  Try shifting into first above 30 mph.  It should go in
without trouble.  Or try 3rd around 70.  Any grinding or other
difficulty means trouble.

The early cars had a double plate clutch that has (IMHO) an
undeservedly bad reputation.  Sure, you can upgrade it to the single
disc clutch for a couple grand, but not worth it (again IMHO).  

Watch out for timing belt and water pumps.  They're about #500 labor
to change, and should definitely be done together (it's the same
work, so may as well change the other when one goes out.  It'll save
you money in the end).

The Central Warning System has a deservedly bad reputation.  They're
far too sensitive in non-critical areas, such as reading resistance
difference between the brake lights and constantly telling you one
light is out.  Many disgusted owners are rumored to have disconnected
the entire system.

Alternators seem to go often, can be rebuilt, but if not they're damn
expensive!

When driving it, if it doesn't drive/accelerate/handle perfectly AVOID
THAT CAR!  I drove a lot of 928's and they ALL drove perfectly.  Any
problem here probably means no maintenance or serious trouble.

The interiors can't handle the rigors of daily driving well, so expect
the seats, shift boot, etc. to look beat up.

Early cars with the leather dash tend to have the leather shrunk and
pulled away from the vents.  The later cars with plastic dashes tend
to crack in the sun.  (Both of these can be avoided with constant
care)

Compression should be like a new car.  They'll maintain high
compression into the multiple 100,000s.

Many of the electricals go out, like mirrors, seats, locks, etc.
Door switches are terrible.  Sometimes work, sometimes not.
Connectors are also more prone to corrosion more than most American or
Japanese cars.  This is often the trouble in the Central Warning
System (but good luck finding it!)

There are a number of really minor things that I look for:
Heater sliders falling off (VERY common)
Fader knob next to emergency brake gone (VERY common)
Hood or rear deck shocks won't hold the panel up (worst case), or
won't lift it with minimal help from you.  When front shocks are going
the hood tries to jam in the first few inches of opening.
These things can all be fixed cheaply and quickly, and if they're not
they tell me the owner hasn't maintained it too well.

Also look above the spare tire just inside the rear lights for the
tool kit.  All the tools should be there (obviously) but see if they
still have the spare spark plugs and spare bulbs.

Electric antennas go out often.

The alarm system tends to corrode and often go off when you unlock it.
The easy fix is to deny it has an alarm; just use the shorter key and
you'll never set the alarm.

That's about it off the top of my head!  Good luck!

Monty

PS--Aside from all the above problems, they're still my favorite car!
Sure better than those air-cooled things.  Call those a Porsche?  A
Porsche should be water-cooled!  8*)

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 23:34:54 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Super Chargers

>There is a company in Tenessee advertising superchargers for 944's and other 
>Porcshe models. they claim many benefits over turbo's (less heat, less wear, 
>simply operation, etc) Anyone have any experience of these devices in a 
>Porsche, they sound too good to be true.

We talked about that earlier this month, or was it last month? Anyway, 
FTP last months' archives and if it's not there, it'll be in this
month's archives next month... 

In the meantime, the answer is: supercharging is a spectacularly good idea,
but you may have to re-engineer your engine to take advantage of it. Replacing
a turbocharger with a supercharger, however, may be nearly a bolt-on operation
and provide significant power increases, particularly at low RPM. Just
bolting on a kit to any engine may work, at least for a while, but not nearly 
so well as if it's correctly retrofit.

BTW, there's an article in the current "911 and Porsche World" magazine
(a UK magazine -- great stuff, BTW) about someone doing just that with
C2 coupes for racing purposes. As one of my business associates says,
"works like a hose"...

Stan

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 05:03 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Warm starting problem with '77 924

>       - When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
>       - When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.
>       - When the car has been driven, and then let sit for
>         45 minutes to an hour, it sits and cranks for about 20 to 30
>         seconds before kicking in.

Classic hot-start problem, I would guess.  Two things to check.  First,
I believe the car came with two 20 ml. accumulators, and that one should
replace one of them with a 40 ml. accumulator.

The second is to install a check valve around the fuel pump.

Sorry to be so vague.  I lent out my up-to-'84 tech bulletins.  Perhaps
someone else can be more precise.

Kevin

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 05:03 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 968 longevity/Bearings

>I've also heard that the driveline was redesigned so that it's no longer
>necessary to (re)move the transaxle to change the clutch.  Don't know
>for sure if that's true, though.

The 928 has this feature.  Sigh...  My favourite 968 feature is that you
can reach the idle stabilization valve without use of a trained ferret.

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 05:07 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Another 944 engine mount woes

>While changing the front brakes pads last Sunday, I noticed  oil
>on the right-side steering linkage rubber boots. I have not yet
>verified whether it the engine mount or something else that is
>leaking, but considering the 944 engine mount problem, I suspect
>that to be the case.

Could be engine oil, but I would first look for a leak of power
steering fluid from either the reservoir, the feed line to the pump,
or seeping from within the rack boot itself.

I've never actually seen the fluid from a motor mount.  It's always
either been inside the mount or gone.

>    - What is a quick way to verify a bad engine mount beside the 
>      obvious oil leak?

The angle and location of the mount make it pretty much mandatory that
you measure its height with a tool.  There's a factory bulletin on
how to cobble one up out of wire or flat stock.  You cannot really
judge it very well by unaided eye.

>    - What is price & part number of the upgraded engine mount, are
>      these only available through dealer or can they be purchased
>      elsewhere?

I think the .04 part is the latest.  They are available in the after-
market, both wholesale and retail.  Be careful of old stock of older
versions from the retail aftermarket.  I know one source which is still
selling .00 timing belts for 944S' at a really swell price.

>    - Should I replace both mounts or only the defective one. (I
>      recently got this car and don't have the maintenance record)?

The defective one.  The other one, driver side, is much less prone to
failure because it isn't right up close to the exhaust.  Make sure your
heat shield is in place around the passenger-side mount, btw.

>    - How many hours of job are we talking here for a one or both
>      mount replacements?

We did my driver-side mount in less than an hour, last week.

    - Any comments on the do's and don't about this job?

    - Brief list of tasks to be performed to do the replacement. I
      ask this because I couldn't find it in my Haynes Manual and I
      don't have the shop manual yet?

You don't have to drop the subframe (honest, Stan, I swear).  You can
take the load off the mount by carefully jacking the engine using the
oil pan (block of wood between jack and pan).  Probably not a good idea
for 944S2 or 968 with their plastic oil pans.

If you think getting the collapsed mount out is a fiddle, wait till you
try getting the new one in.  Make life a little easier by trimming the
bottom stud of excess (uncaptured) length.

There are two different length bolts to be used depending on the height
of the foot of the engine mounting bracket.  Covered in tech bulletin
book G, group 1, number 9011, dated 13 Nov 90.

Kevin

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 0:25:46 PDT
From: Paul Dennis Collopy <collopy@leland.Stanford.EDU>
Subject: 914 starting, oil?, & VIN

Hey gang,

Any other 914 owners experience the problem of trying to start the car
after it has been sitting for a few days, and it starts, stalls, then
cranks and restarts?  Have noticed it also tends to take more cranking
after it has been run for a while, then parked for a short time (less than
an hour).  Have heard two differing opinions about this.  One version is
to pump the accelerator, other version says don't.  Open for suggestions.

Other question...should I be changing the oil every three months on this
car when I have only put 7k miles on it in 5 years?  I have heard lots of
theories about what engine oil, but I sure don't believe that the engine
knows that technology has changed.  Still inclined to stick with 30 weight
oil.  Temperature range here and mileage is not much different than Ohio.
(car was kept inside garage in Ohio, in case you are trying to figure that 
one out)

Final question... without original records, how can you tell your car's
original point of destination?  I have yet to see another 914 that looks
like mine.  I know it has been re-painted, but welds are smooth.  I've
looked in lots of 'picture books' but haven't found a match.  Anybody have
information on a car with a January '72 sticker?  Rear lights say '69,
vehicle number indicates it was a '72, title says '72.

Pam   collopy@leland.stanford.edu

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 05:03 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 911 fuel line woes

>This leads me to suspect the tank is the culprit.  Is yanking the tank a
>nasty job? What can I do to it once it is out?  Anyone been here before?
>Books galore but enthusiasm is in short supply.  A new tank is not the 
>preferred option here.

Piece of cake.  After you get the lines off it and everything else out
of the way, it comes out with three allen bolts, if memory serves.

You'll find it easier to pull back the filler hose if you lube it with
a silicon spray.

Date: Mon 19 Jul 93 08:42:56-EDT
From: DEHAASM@NAPC-1.NAVY.MIL
Subject: RE: Injector Cleaner



> out of the car, I have a pressurized pulse stand that I run trichlor
> or carbon tetrachloride through, about 20 min per injector.

> *NOTHING* works better than that?

  This is probably true.....however for those who don't know trichlor
is nasty stuff and one should take the proper precautions not to
breath it in.  This stuff can collect in the liver and cause health
problems down the road of life.  

I work in the aircraft engine industry and we use Trichlor for cooling
purposes and any time there is a Trichlor leak everyone evacuates the
test cell until the leak is fixed.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 05:57:02 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Injector cleaner

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: Injector cleaner

If your question pertains to a fuel additive (versus a spray or dip cleaner),
I recommend Chevron's Techron (no, not because I work for them)... Through my
own experience as well as friends (plus a little inside info). The fuel
additive really works. The compound is a Chevron developed product that is
sold to many other major oil Co.s as well as additive marketers for rebranding
into there own product lines. If you want to be sure of what you're buying,
get the Chevron branded stuff.

By the way, follow the directions exactly -- this is not a case of "more is
better". The effectiveness is actually REDUCED if too much or too little is
used. Also, if your deposit build-up is bad, you may need more than one
treatment. If this is the case, do not treat successive tanks. A reaction is
started with the first treatment that will continue for several tanks of
non-treated fuel. Best is to skip a few tanks in between treatments...

By the way, Porsche recommends Techron by name.........

 -Scott

From: "Graphics Group" <Graphics_Group@qmgate.arc.nasa.gov>

 RE>>> 1.8L FI
>I'm not sure what state you're in.
  Gee, I hear that from my friends quite a bit....  8>)
Seriously, I'm out in California, in the SF Bay Area.  The regs are rather
stiff here.  If the "emissions systems" (which includes all the induction and
fuel stuff) have been "tampered with" (read: modified), then there is
_no_limit_ on what you must spend to make the car comply.  Basically, carbs are
illegal, without special dispensation or a Smog Check mechanic who doesn't pay
attention.  And the special dispensation can be rather difficult to get.  And
they may change their minds at any time.  (sigh!)

>Regarding FI vs carbs, . . .   Either way, a fun car.
DEFINITELY!

>- Jeff.

 - --Dave D.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 10:53:33 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Self-service 928


A fellow wrote about purchasing a 928 and doing all the work himself,
a little something to be aware of:

The four-cam 928 motors must be removed from the car to remove the heads
and perform any upper-end work. The 2-cam 2 valve heads are removeable 
in the car. This operation is about a 10 hour job each direction for the
four cam car. They are very cheap when they require engine work.

For the people that wonder about which way the 924/944 etc engines come out, the drivetrains are installed from the BOTTOM of the vehicle on 
the assembly line, this is why certain locations can never be accessed 
in the car. The 924/944 engines will come out of the car from either 
the top or bottom of the car, remembering that there are a number of 
items which must be disconnected and/or removed in either case.

Have fun!

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 1993 12:25:54 -0400 (EDT)
From: Jeff Cerier <jcerier@world.std.com>

On 16 Jul 1993, Graphics Group wrote:

> 
>     Yeah, right!  And just _how_ am I supposed to pass smog?  "I know the book
> says it has to have fuel injection, but it meets the emissions requirements, so
> can't you let me slide?"  They don't buy that.
>     Besides, I've just been reading the Bosch FI book from Bentley, and they
> have gone a good way toward convincing me that, when properly adjusted, FI is
> better than carbs.  And after a set of carb headaches with the Honda, I must
> agree.
> 
> --Dave D.

I'm not sure what state you're in.  In MA my 70 914 has been exempt from
smog testing for a few years now.  I could get it to pass though, although
in MA the fact that I've changed to carbs is not a problem.

Regarding FI vs carbs, my problem with the FI is simply that mine was so
old.  I think that if everything works perfectly the FI may run better
than the Webers, although I'm not quite sure of the top end power.  Would
definitely run better in various anbient conditions.  With all those 20+
yr old gizmos and hoses, I doubt whether my FI could measure up to the
carbs without a serious amount of attention (read time & money).  For the
few hundred bucks and days worth of work to put the carbs on, the car has
been a completely reliable performer for the past 8 years.  Besides, I
love the way they look, the way they sound, and the feel of the throttle
response.  Very different from FI.  More like the way I envision a 356 to
drive.  just my opinions, though.  Either way, a fun car.

 - Jeff.

From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924 Turbo Bearings

RE: Paul's message of 19 July.

Paul, pull the motor. It's a time-consuming, but not too difficult a
process.

You will have to disconnect the bell housing to remove the clutch and 
flywheel anyway, it's easier to pull the motor with the crank and 
everything in it. The issue here is that your crank is scored and needs 
machining. You will find that the top land on your piston is probably
distored from the heat and there are scores in the cylinder walls. The 
oil pump also has to come off. 

A more pleasant note, if you remove the engine and do your disassembly-
assembly outside the engine, the turbo stuff goes on more easily. Trust
me on that one. 

There's more bad news, you'll want to find out where the particles 
came from, just pull it down and check your cam and followers and 
examine them for wear. You'll also notice wear in your valve guides.

you'll lose the bearings in short order if you just do just that. No one
said these things were cheap and simple.  Do it right and you'll be
rewarded with a nice running car.

If any one else is reading, a 1979-80 924 turbo can be enhanced if 
you're about to rebuild by using the 81-82 8.1:1 compression pistons.
They cost the same, you get more in town driveability because of the 
half point of compression.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 20:06:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: Report on the 'NEW' BFG R! 224's!!!

Item Subject: Report on the 'NEW' BFG R1 224's!!!
Well, I finally did some testing on the NEW R1 224 Road Race compounded
tires:

Car-             Front Engine/Rear Drive  Weight: 2300 lbs
                 Limited slip:  Front/Rear Sway Bar
Wheels:          7x15

Tires:           BFG R1 224's   Tire Pressures:  F: 38 lbs  R: 36 lbs
                 205x50x15's
Comments:

These tires are incredible!  I'm a YOKOHAMA Fan, but these tires are Very
fast. Comments have been made about the way the feel mushy upon initial
turn in (you turn the car in- then the tires react comments...)  Well, I
think the secret to these tires is running the right sized rim for the tire
mounted.  In the past, I would have mounted these 205x50x15's on a 6" rim.
This would have given, as "The Real" Roger Johnson commented: "... the car
would turn in then the tires would catch up!"

Having mounted them on 7" rims, this really stretches out the tire and makes
the side wall very square... This greatly improves the sidewall stiffeness
and make turn in response, IMHO, quicker than the RSII's!!!

And, on top of this, the learning curve for these tires is very steep(quick)!
In no time, I had an in-experienced, never have AX'd, beginner driving the
car at its limit, **SAFELY**, with incredible car control!  I got in it
and drove immediately, what I feel, quicker than my RSII's.  I then took
the car out for some HIGH SPEED (read:tight Mountain switch-back's) and found
them to be very forgiven at the limit and definitely easy to 'read' at this
limit!!! I did get the front to break away slightly, but it was easliy
brought back into shape by simply modulating front tire loading (squeezing
the brakes slightly to transfer load to the front of the car).

All in all, these tire are, simply put, incredible (and,after 130 miles
of low speed/tire scrubbing and high speed drifting: the tires are not
even broken in yet!! See my comment on Ken Mitchel's wear data at the
Wendover NT event using 224's!!).

It could be an interesting '94 if BFG's AX compound tire is as good as
their current RR (224) tire, just much stickier (and I hope they solve
their wear compared to the RSII's!!!!!).

I'll use these at Driver's schools at Portland/Sears/Laguna Seca/Seatle,
as well as a few AX's until I get the October '93 release of the AX
compound tire.  Then I'll do a comparison with my RSII's (still VERY,VERY
fast with GREAT WEAR(!!!!)), so I'll keep you all informed (if you care!)

G'day:

Mr Wizard

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 14:49:48 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 911 Salvage Title

>	I'm looking at picking up a project car to do a complete rebuild to,
>and have pretty much decided on a 1978+ 911. The rebuild would include a
>conversion to a convertible c2 turbo look. Since most of the car I will be
>chopping up - I've pondered whether a salvaged title 911 would be all that
>bad. I have a line on a 1979 targa - hit hard in the front that would be
>cheap. I have access to full frame straightning equipment,mig etc so the work
>does not bother me. The question I have is whether even if the car is
>completely and professionly rebuilt, would a prospective Porsche buyer be
>put-off by a salvage title. I would do a step-step photo shoot of the rebuild.

Answer is: (come on everybody...) "it depends". First things first -- if you
don't have a Hunter or Bosch frame machine, and the factory measurments for
re-aligning the tub, forget it. I've been through this on my personal car. You
can't pull it out "close enough" like you can on a Chevy truck or something.
It pretty much has to be on the money, or you lose *BIG TIME* when it comes 
time to set up the suspension. This *IS* a very high performance sports car,
right?

Second, dealing with a car on salvage title. There is, in most states, a 
mechanism for moving a car from a salvage title to a "real" title. You should 
check with your DMV about that proceedure. Doing this typically involves a set 
of state inspections, to make sure that they're not giving you a title for your 
refrigerator or something. Once you have a real title, who cares? *EXCEPT*
that one of the first questions I ever get asked when selling a car (after "how
many miles does it have") is "has it ever been wrecked?" How you answer that
is a matter for you and your consience. I have always told the complete truth
on my cars.

Third, value. Here's the *REAL* "it depends" part. Ghost Racing raced an MR-2
that we bought on a salvage title, rebuilt, re-titled, and after its retirement
as a competition car, sold it to our crew chief who has put another 30k miles
on it. No loss of value, because it was going to a knowledgible consumer. We
also have a 911 that was the 1990 SCCA Solo II champ in F Prepared. It's 
built out of two different tubs, welded together. On a salvage title. There's
probably $15K + just in the engine, another $4k or so in the suspension
and wheels. It is *NOT* street legal, nor could it be made street legal. But
what's it worth to a competitor? For that matter, is it worth anything to a
non-competitor??

Where you lose on value is when you try to sell the car as a "perfect" or
"near perfect" car to an ill-informed consumer. If I knew a car had been
in a heavy collision, it would not deter me from buying it -- but it *WOULD*
change the way I investigated the car. I'd want to have the tub checked, to
inspect *ALL* the mount points for suspension and drivetrain, to check the
fit and finish a bit closer, etc. And if possible, to talk to the guys who did
the work if I didn't know them. Then I'd pay a fair price for it. However,
if it didn't pass my inspection, I wouldn't buy it. And a bargain basement
price wouldn't make any difference at all.

Finally, conversions and updates. When you change the car from stock 
configuration you have truely stepped into The Twilight Zone. There is 
*NO* rule for value, *NO* benchmark, *NO* standards. What you can get
for a "conversion car" is strictly a matter between you and the buyer.
I've seen people lose their ass *BIG TIME* on such deals. For example
we joke that the VP of the local Porsche Club has the "Worlds Most Expensive
$8,000 Car". It's a '69 911E that has been, ahhhh, modified. To look like
an early 70s S, with a fake Marlborough Racing look, a tail that could have
never come with the car, a bizarre custom interior (including alligator 
inlays on the dash!), an "updated and modified" engine, polished and chromed
wheels, etc. I really don't know how much he has in it, but I do know
what it's really worth -- about $8k, unless he finds someone who wants
*THAT CAR*. Then who knows what it's worth? He's turned down offers in 
the $20k's, but on occassion has tried to sell it in the $15-18k range
and failed. Must vary with the wind, etc. 8{)

The exceptions to this rule are conversions to factory spec for that
model, e.g. putting an electric cab top on a '87 targa, using all factory
parts in a manner that is indistinguishable from a factory installation. Then
it would be treated like a factory cab, value-wise. Of course, there are
*STUPID* things you can do that way too -- I know of a pre-A 356 that was
in very good all original condition which was hacked to add a factory sunroof
top clip, and new paint and interior. Value dropped precipitously due to
destruction of an original car in original condition.

>	I think 60 Minutes did a take on salvage titles that killed their
>resale value. Comments/Opinions appreciated...

Hope I added some info you didn't have here. Or at least confused things in
an interesting way...

Stan

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 13:24:55 -0700
From: sfi@netcom.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: Re:  What can be done w/engine in car (was: ..buy/lease 968)

 If you have heavily scored big-end bearings then you should ask yourself
 where that material (that once was part of the bearings) has gone?

 It generally trashes your oil pump and ends up in the pan's filter
 screen (after having trashed the cam tower bearing surface as well).

 I'm afraid that your only sensible option is a complete strip-down,
 check and re-assembly. Otherwise I think you're driving a timebomb.

 S.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 15:47:49 EDT
From: Mark Sirota <mark@greenwich.com>
Subject: Re: 944 Breakin Via Door Handle

Lee Chen writes:
>  Today I had the dubious pleasure of chasing a would be radio and/or car
> thief with a makeshift cudgel after witnessing him break into my 944S.
> 
>  Afterwards, I noticed, upon close examination, that the perpetrator
> utilized his screwdriver to poke under both door handles to release the
> lock (leaving gaping holes in the door skin (and, later, his skin too)).
> 
>  A long while ago, I recall reading about a product in VW & Porsche
> magazine that allegedly prevented this means of gaining access.  Some
> chromoly (chromium-molybdenum) plates or something that could be used on
> 924s/944s/VWs.  Unfortunately, I dismissed that ad at the time because I
> was quite familiar with the boulderdash that gets printed in that rag.
> 
>  Has anyone actually used this device?  If so, is it effective?  More
> importantly, what is the address and phone number for the manufacturer(s)?

1-800-ADP-LOCK.  They're called Armor Door Plates, and they're well worth
the money.  Any water-cooled VW owner can tell you...

These are much better than the other versions.  These are not just flat
plates; they have flanges that go into the door so they can't be bent and
the screwdriver cannot reach the release lever, and they're made of
hardened steel.  Others are not as hard, and are just flat.

They're about $50 for two, $95 for four.  They come with all parts needed,
and take about 30 seconds per door to install.

Highly recommended.  I have no affiliation with the product other than as
a satisfied customer.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 17:20:56 EDT
From: Dan Solis <dan@inset.com>
Subject: Warm starting problem with '77 924


    I`ve had my `77 924 for the last four years and have had a problem
that will not go away.  (I've only been on this mailing list for the last
three months, and so I appologize if this has been discussed before.)

	- When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
	- When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.
	- When the car has been driven, and then let sit for
	  45 minutes to an hour, it sits and cranks for about 20 to 30
	  seconds before kicking in.

    The starter is going like a ball of fire -- but the engine just doesn't
catch.  It always starts, but it's real embarrassing sitting there while it
happens.

    I don't know much about car engines, but the mechanics I've taken it to
seem to have replaced almost everything except the air in the tires, but
to no avail.  Three different garages have worked on it -- but it persists.

    We started out by replacing the injectors, on the theory that
they were leaking and air was getting into the fuel lines.
We then replaced the fuel pump with a more recent version that had a valve
that keeps the fuel from going back in the wrong direction.  Both these
attempted fixes seemed very reasonable, and were suggested and performed
by a garage that works only on Porsches.

    I've met two other 924 owners with the same problem.  One of them had
the problem go away when he replaced the head gasket.  I've since replaced
my head gasket, but the problem didn't go away.  The other guy was
a guy I met at a gas station while we were both filling up with gas.  His
car had the exact same symptoms, and he didn't know what to try either.
I don't know whether he ever solved it, since I've never seen him again.

    Some of the other things I've had replaced are:
	- fuel accumulator
	- rotor
	- ignition wires
	- filters (all)
	- techron (I have no idea what this is)

     Has anyone else seen this problem?  More importantly, has anyone
solved it?  Since nothing else has worked I guess I COULD go try changing
the air in the tires?!

     By the way, I've also had the gas fumes problem that was recently
under discussion.  I long ago learned to live with that by just not
filling the tank all the way.

     Thanks.  Any suggestions would be appreciated.  I will, however, be
out of the country for about a week and a half starting on July 29, and
will therefore not be able to answer mail during that time.

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 17:08:36 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Of 924s and Men (women ,too)


Hi gang,

It's me again. I see some activity around 924s and 924 Turbos, here are
a few comments based on 15+ years of owning one.

Normally these things are bullet proof, the cars tend to go to hell 
mechanically when the oil isn't changed and driven in hot, stop and go 
traffic ( nothing else does well either). The cooling systems work well
unless your typical trip to work is less than ten miles. Then the system
never opens the thermostat and the coolant boils, making the water pump 
seal fail early and lots of rust in the block. This is a common 
occurance in the San Francisco area. 

Improper oil changes, wrong viscosity, traffic jams all cause the cam to
go away, the lubrication tube `cokes', stopping oil from getting to the
cam, then the lobes and followers wear. the metal will eventually 
destroy the bearings, then the crank. 

Turbos hate being idled for long periods as the oil flow from the pump 
is low and insufficient to cool the turbo. If you're stuck in traffic, 
turn on the airconditioning, it flows enough air to keep the turbo cool.

One thing a lot of 924 (and 944) owners do is leave off the gravel 
shield under the radiator, It is very important, this directs the 
airflow through the engine compartment and makes the radiator draft 
correctly. The little grill infront of the radiator directs air around 
the lower spoiler, restricting the amount into the radiator. Removal of it adds a significant amount of drag, but adds to the cooling of the 
car. This can be experimented with at the track, SCCA never noticed that
the two top slats were missing on Barney Gardiners 1981 SSA 924. It 
cured a small overheating problem.

Cracked exhaust manifolds. They crack. Cure: Replace with the late 1980 
and later stock manifold and down pipe. Those are good for 5 hp and at 
least 8 ft-lbs of torque. Also pass the California visual on earlier 
cars. Headers are a mixed bag. Most aren't worth the trouble as one has
to tune the CIS to work correctly. The best exhaust is a stock one with
the cat removed for `track testing'.

Ignition, the stock unit recurved to euro-spec (no vacuum retard) works
best, the curve is in the Haynes manual. Again, Those of us in 
California have the sceptre of the Smog-Nazis for the visual inspection.
On the mid 1980 and later with oxygen sensor, there is an idle 
stabiliztion box mounted with the ignition module on the left inner 
fender.  these have been known to go bad, be aware.

Cams, there's only one stock one that works, if you have an early (pre
1977 1/2) you will have the smog bastard. Put in the later cam and get 
10 hp, it's the same as the euro unit.

If you're interested, I have an article I wrote for Excellence on 
suspension for the 924.

For now,

If you want to find their value, call Robert Wood Inc. (310)473-6649
(I have no affiliation with this company other than the fact they've taken
my money...) and ask them how much
it would cost for the ones you have.   You can vary their value depending
upon condition (scratches, dings, etc.).  Stock Porsche wheels are NOT CHEAP.
I bought a set of 16" C2 wheels for race rubber on the RS America from Robert
Wood and they were expensive, but worth it.  In hindsignt, probably should
have bought some Revolutions (multi-piece lightweight) but I couldn't wait
for > 6 months for the gnomes to make 'em :-(.  


If you intend upon doing any sort of racing (autox or otherwise) it's VERY
advantageous to have an extra set of wheels.  If you want to drive it on
the street and autocross it, you can buy yourself a set of sticky tires, 

put them on the stock wheels and put your street tires on the aftermarket
wheels I assume are currently on the car.  


If you intend upon selling the car again some day, having the factory wheels
would probably add to its value.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 10:56:07 BST
From: ben@wet.sbi.com (Ben Evans)


>>I went down to the local wrecking yard over the weekend and saw a
>>carrera 4 engine sitting there for sale ($12K, list price $50K True?)
>>What cought my eye was a twin distributor driven by a single shaft.

>That is a dual ignition. Brain fade is keeping me from remembering
>for sure, but I think it's a dual plug affair as well.  That twin
>distributor was responsible for much of the problems with the
>engine -- it's bad enough to synch dual two-throat carbs in '50s
>technology, but to try and *mechanically* synch two distributors
>as well was a bit much. Interesting article on this on either the
>June Excellence or a recent Pano.

>	Is this an engine from a "'90's 911 Carrera 4" or from a "'50's
>550 spyder"? Don't the new 911's have dual spark plugs now?  It sounds
>like an engine from a new 911 to me.  Also i thought the V-type twin
>distributor cured the timing problems caused by the original two separate
>distributors driven from the ends of the cam shafts.

I suspect this has to be an engine from the current 911 Carrera 2/4,
this has dual distributors, driving two spark plugs per cylinder.

If it really is the 4 cam, dual distributor unit which went into
a very few 356 cars and some other racing beasts, it would be worth huge
money, as are the cars that it went into.

I was reading about this engine last night, and can confirm that the early
versions did have dual distributors driven from the camshafts later
revised to a drive from the crankshaft to cure timing problems.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 06:03:39 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Re: Warm starting problem with '77 924

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: Re: Warm starting problem with '77 924

Please bear with me you 924 experts...
I had a '77 Scirocco that had the exact same symptoms.  It had the Bosch
K-Jetronic fuel injection (not sure if the 924 had this).

The problem was bad ball checks in the injector heads.  Replacement was easy,
part cost was $18 ea (circa '79).  Mine had two bad ones.

Shade tree mechanic diagnosis method was to run the car for a minute or two,
shut down and check each injector (they popped out of the rubber grommet on
mine quite easily).  Replace the ones with a wet or dripping tip.

Hope this helps....

*** Forwarding note from PORSCHEP--INTERNET 07/20/93 03:12 ***

From: Kevin C. Gross
To: "porschephiles" <porschephiles@tta.com>

Subject: Re: Warm starting problem with '77 924
>       - When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
>       - When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.

Classic hot-start problem, I would guess.  Two things to check.  First,
I believe the car came with two 20 ml. accumulators, and that one should
replace one of them with a 40 ml. accumulator.

The second is to install a check valve around the fuel pump.

Sorry to be so vague.  I lent out my up-to-'84 tech bulletins.  Perhaps
someone else can be more precise.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 09:38:44 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Another 944 engine mount woes


   >    - What is a quick way to verify a bad engine mount beside the 
   >      obvious oil leak?

   The angle and location of the mount make it pretty much mandatory that
   you measure its height with a tool.  There's a factory bulletin on
   how to cobble one up out of wire or flat stock.  You cannot really
   judge it very well by unaided eye.

One thing I've done to spot a mount that has split is to jack up the
engine with a floor jack very carefully and inspect the deflection of
the mount, if it is really split through you'll see it very plainly.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 06:20:56 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Re: Exhaust Modifications

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: Re: Exhaust Modifications

I have put SSI's on our '84 Carerra.  It requires new oil lines and some heater
line mods (if you need a lot of heat - we get plenty without the mods).

I disconnect the oxy sensor for AX use (quicker throttle response as Motronic
fails rich (I believe)).  I reconnect for normal use (starting is rough OTW).

In a previous Excellence, Bruce Anderson was dyno testing performance chips and
found the SSI's with a free flow exhaust gave the biggest performance boost
for a single upgrade (I have the issue, but don't recall the date -- late '91
I believe).

I put on a Bursch free flow muffler -- works fine & not too loud...

BTW, I am selling the whole set up as we plan to sell the car (another note).
Contact me at sjje@chevron.com if interested....

*** Forwarding note from PORSCHEP--INTERNET 07/20/93 00:13 ***

From: Frank Ciotti

John,
I just put SSI's on my carbuerated 2.7L.  The cars runs much better now.
no more popping or flat spots!  Sounds much better too, especially in
the 2000-3000 range.  Performance is improved however I have no numbers
for you.  Anyways, if you decide to put the SSI's on, be sure the get
the correct styles.  The options are as follows:

        Thick flanges - Cars w/thermal reactors & possibly others
        Extended tubes - These will allow installation on 77 & later
                         911's w/915 transmission clutch assist spring
                         without bending the weld seam on the exchanger.
        Gaster Savers - '77 2.7's, '80-'83 3.0's, all 3.2's, & all Turbos
                        These are the sleeves which fit up into the
                        exhaust port on the head.

You should probably call SSI (415-883-2000) and talk with them.  The
people there are very knowledgeable.  Especially John.  They do not
drop ship so you will have to go through a distributor.  Send mail
if you have installation questions.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 10:10:58 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Warm starting problem with '77 924


	   - When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
	   - When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.
	   - When the car has been driven, and then let sit for
	     45 minutes to an hour, it sits and cranks for about 20 to 30
	     seconds before kicking in.

Assuming you are having a fuel system problem, you are going to have
to measure the system fuel pressures with an accurate guage (or have
your mechanic do it) and compare it under the several operating
conditions described above. The leaking injectors/fuel pump check
valve *seems* to be the problem, and is common on CIS cars, where the
"rest" pressure drops off and the car is tough to start after the
pressure "bleeds down" when hot. But since this didn't help you're
going to have to take a methodical troubleshooting approach. Ask your
mechanic what the fuel pressures are (both system and control) for
each condition, cold, hot and warmup. Hopefully he has recorded these numbers.
	
- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 10:21:15 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 914-6 evaluation?


   Last friday I got a chance to look at an original 914-6. Original
   owner, paint, interior, engine, etc. 67K miles. I drove it a bit,
   but the engine pinged at anything over half throttle from 2-5K RPM
   (loudly), so I didn't get a chance to evaluate performance. The

Hmmm, a *loud* pinging from a 911 flat six at 2-5K? Sounds like trouble
to me...

   are performance rather than originality, what does an original have
   to offer that a conversion won't have? (apart from a heater). Asking

Well, for one you get the ignition key on the left hand side of the
steering column on the original. You get the *single* front rubberized
engine mount. But the thing you are really paying for is that VIN
number starting with 914XXXXX instead of 471XXXX. Other then that
you're better off in terms of most value for the dolar to pick up an
already "debugged (I like that term) conversion with the best motor
you can find, IMO if you're not concerned about investment potential.

You can have a heater in a conversion if you use original heat exchangers btw.

From: "HNET4::MRGATE::\"A1::HINCKLEYS\""
Subject: Tires

From:	NAME: Scott H Hinckley

To:	"porschephiles@tta.com"@MAILGATE@ADA3@MRGATE

Paul wrote:
>Has anyone tried the Dunlop D60A2?  If so, how did it run in the rain?

I ran Dunlops and they ran great in the rain...at first. Unless they have 
improved the design there is a flaw. Inbetween EVERY rib on the edge of the tire 
there is a sidewall-stiffener (looks like a tread wear mark, but is the wrong 
height.) When the tire wears down to this level it loses most all wet-weather 
traction. This is not true of other tires I have tried that only put the 
sidewall stiffeners every 2-3 ribs.

>second choice is the Goodyear GT+4

This is commonly called the Gatorback, nicknamed the Skatorback due to its low 
level of handling water.

Could be seomething simple, like they left the vaccuum hoses connected
when they timed it, or they're using 87 octane....  Or it could be 
something expensive wrong, or it could be covering up the fact that
something expensive is wrong....

>Given that my interests
>are performance rather than originality, what does an original have
>to offer that a conversion won't have? (apart from a heater).
I thought that some of the conversions still had heaters?  It may be a
question of shelling out the $$ for 914/6 heat exhangers...
Anyway, the 914/6 was only sold from 70-71 (maybe a few in 72?).
As such, it had the tail-shifter, which may account for the vague shifting
you noticed, and the "spartan" interior, with the immobile passenger
seat.  The HP benefits are limited over a 2.0 4-cyl, unless you go in for
modifications (as many do).  US-spec 4-cyl HP was about 95, and the six
had about 110.  2.4's and 2.7's can have a *lot* more, but if you are
going that route, why not just put that engine in a /4 chassis?  The main
benefits seem to be the fact that the installation has been debugged,
the brakes are a bit better (??I think), and that the wheels are 5-bolt
already.  If you are looking for the best performance, try to find a 
conversion that's been done *right*, which can be hard to do.

>Is $15K for this car reasonable? I see it asked a lot, but
>what are these cars actually selling for?
I recently saw several original sixes at a couple of swap meets
selling for $10-12K.  They seemed to be in pretty good shape,
but they didn't seem to be getting many offers.

>Thanks for your thoughts on this.
>
>Yves

As usual, these are IMHO, YMMV...  I have been known to be wrong before...

 - Dave D.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 09:14:59 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 924 Starting problems


To the fellow with the starting problem, The auxillary air bypass 
operates when the engine is warm, also. If it has been removed and
replaced without coating the surface that mates to the manifold, it 
will operate incorrectly when the engine is warm.

One other tip to you CIS freaks, the sensor plate needs to be free of 
grease, oil, gasoline grunge and dirt. The boot should be removed and 
the plate inspected at major services. The height also needs to be 
checked as it will cause warm start problems. The O-rings around the 
fuel injectors get hard and leak air, another source of rough idle or 
hard starting, those can be chedcked with the engine idling and 
squirting the area around the nozzle with water. If the engine idles 
rough, replace the seal. The plastic (phenolic) seats are threaded in 
and sometimes vibrate loose, check those.

The water method can be used with the timed injection cars to check for 
injector air leaks, be careful about the wiring harness.


944 engine mounts, the best ones are the non-hydralics that have 
appeared on the late 944 Turbos and S2s. Forget the others and I'll 
have to recommend, replace both at the same time. The driver's side 
can partially fail and take the passenger's side out without totally
going. 
Remove the crossmember if you're doing the mount change in your garage, 
a hoist to allow you to stand while doing the job allows a better 
working angle so the crossmember isn't a problem at that height. The 
only pain is the power steering breather line which some damn engineer
put on the top side of the crossmember. It's a 2 1/2 hour job to change
the mounts. I find it easir to wedge the mounts in the crossmember out 
of the car when the car in low, as it would be in my garage, then lower 
the engine so the member and mounts can be bolted back to the engine.
Then riase everything and reassemble.(I've left out a few steps).

944 oil cap and dipstick. If you have a slightly rough idle and the 
motor mounts have been changed, check your dipstick. The crankcase needs
to be closed (like a 914). the rubber seal gets hard and leaks air. 
Ditto for the rubber seal in the oil filler cap.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 10:01:58 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Cracked Exhaust Manifold


Mike,

Sad to say, if you have had it welded before and it's cracking again,
the manifold has gone past the point-of-no return and has begun to
crystalize along grain seams from the casting process. If you replace it
with a nex one, there is an upgrade (read expensive) that updates the 
1980 to the 1981 with the stainless steel bolts. It works and is
much more reliable than the 1980 manifold. Look for a donor 1981 931
first, buy a new one as last resort. The bolts, spacers, gaskets and
misc hardware will run an additional $250 unless someone helps on the 
deal. 
While you're at it, check the high-pressure oil-feed line to the turbo
and make sure it's clear of coked oil. Replace the oil feed O-rings on 
the top and bottom of the turbo, when the mainfold comes off it's 
another couple of hours to get rid of a pair of potential leaks. (Most
turbo leaks are those two pesky seals, they get cooked early on, then 
leak).

Let me locate the suspension article file first, then I'll get back on 
line and ask for your FAX #.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA
