Date:         Tue, 20 Jul 93 13:47-0400
From: Ken.Stanton@QueensU.CA
Subject:      924's and demonic possession

regarding the trouble starting the 924 under partly cooled down conditions.
My 931 has this problem as well. I still think there is a leak somewhere.
I have made it much easier to start by setting the mixture somewhat richer
than it should be. This helps. A lot. Look for air leaks. If the air enters
without passing the sensor plate, then you will get insufficient fuel. When
cold, the cold start valve offset this condition, and you can start it.
When it's hot. It starts easier anyway, but in that gray area of partly
cooled down, the mixture is too lean for easy starting. As an experiment,
when you are experiencing one of those tough starting fits, try sticking
a pencil into the air flow sensor plate <gently> to hold it down a bit.
Then try starting. Gary Cook recommended this procedure to me, and it
does indeed work. Look for leaking fuel lines in that hell hole area
under the distributor around the warm up regulator.
My only other guess for likely candidates is the cold start valve itself.
Comments welcome here from any CIS experts: If the cold start valve is
leaking, you won't have full pressure to start the engine when it is partly
cooled down. It may take a lot of cranking to get the thing going again, since
you won't have the benefit of the additional fuel from the cold start nozzle
until the engine cools down further.
Happy hunting.

  Ken Stanton,   Doctoral Program, Sch. of Business,
                 Queen's U., Kingston, Canada K7L 3N6
                 Tel:(613)545-2377

The dealer history and PCA membership are a good sign. It is essential
that you have the vehicle inspected by an expert if you are not buying
through an official Porsche dealer. Some people would argue that you should
have an expert inspect it even if it is from an official Porsche
dealer, they might be able to tell you if you are getting value for money.
 
> The purchase circumstances were such that I was unable to do more
> than test drive at moderate speed and look at it on a lift. It
> drove very well a bit hard into 2nd gear (perhaps synchros will
> need work). The car was traded in for a Corvette (Chevy Dealer!);
> the interest level was very high (competing multiple Porsche philes
> buyers in the same room). My decision was colored with desire. 

I really think that you are in danger of making an impulse purchase here.
Porsche have made over 300000 vehicles, 85% are still running. There are
plenty out there to choose from. My advice:

 1 Decide on the exact vehicle spec you want
 2 Follow up every example you can find of your spec
 3 Engage expert help if you are a first time buyer especially
 4 Find the one that offers best value for money, go in bidding
   low, don't exceed your target price.
 
> Please share with me your appraisal of the reliability and
> maintenance requirements of this car. I am most eager to hear from
> you!  

Your 911 will *probably* be reliable and economical. If you don't take
a lot of care in purchase this may prove not to be the case, the more 
thought out your purchase strategy, the better the result IMHO.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 19:09:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: autox@autox.team.net digest #845 Tue Jul 20 01:05:02 MDT 199

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 11:27:25 PDT
From: "Michael Butler" <mcb@vnet.IBM.COM>
Subject: BFG 224s

I tried a set of the new BFG road race design tires this last weekend.  I'm
impressed.  We had a Saturday practice/Sunday championship event at Pt. Magu
which is mostly coarse asphalt.

On the long, fast Saturday course, I took 7 runs on the 224s followed by 4 runs
on the 206s (autocross compound).  Both sets were in the 205/55-14 size mounted
5.5 inch rim on my CS Miata.

Here's a summary of my impressions by surface:

 - coarse asphalt: 224s feel more linear than the 206s. Better turn-in and
  smaller slip angles.

 - concrete: more difficult to modulate braking with the 224s.

 - sealed asphalt: 224s run at larger slip angles than 206s.

When I put on the 206s, I thought they were much faster.  The 206s give
excellent feedback (better than the Yoko RSIIs, which tend to feel nervous
at the limit).  However, I was never able to equal my best time on the 224s.
The 224s had the greatest advantage on the coarse asphalt.

I thinks it's too early to assess longevity.  Unlike Paul Brown, I did manage
to get significant wear on the 224s in my first outing (but Pt. Magu is tough
on tires -- I'll never run my 206s there again).

Now, here's a question for the Net concerning tire temperatures on the BFG
"plug" tires.  I spent part of Saturday trying to optimize tire temps on the
BFG 206s.  I dropped a lot of pressure but was still getting higher temps
in the center.  Later I was told that the BFG "plug" tires (like the 205/55-14
which was created by putting a plug in the 185/60-14) will always run hot
in the center and that pyrometer data on these tires are useless.  Any
comments?

If I run the BFGs, I may have to realign my car.  Unlike the Yoko 205/55-14
RSIIs which run extremely hot on the shoulder, the BFGs tend to run hot on
the inside (especially the 224s).  If you are camber-limited, the BFGs may
be the ticket.

Michael Butler (mcb@vnet.ibm.com)

Date:     Tue, 20 Jul 93 15:51:45 EDT
From: "Hugh A. Huntzinger" (CCAC-LAD) <huntzing@PICA.ARMY.MIL>
Subject:  '85 911:  Replacement Key for factory alarm FOUND

The factory alarm system for a '85 911 has controlled keys/blanks.

I've unsuccessfully called all over creation to try to get a second key
or blank, since I only got one when I bought my '85 this last winter.
The only option appears to be to shell out ~$75 for a new tumbler (with 2
new keys).  I finally broke my sole key a few weeks ago (bonehead!).

At my locksmith, he starts searching his key racks looking to make me a
replacement (Ha!).  Behold, he comes up with a blank that works!

The ID# of the blank that he got to work is YM22...a SAAB key, I think.
It took him a second try on the cutting machine to get it right, so it's
not perfectly straightforward, but it's a LOT cheaper.  $10 later, I had
*three* new alarm system keys.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 14:13:29 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: BFG Tires


Mike, 

The BFG people are correct, most "plug" tires do have that problem from 
the method of construction. On most tires, especially autocross or fast
slalom, the sidewall and shoulder temps will tell you more than the 
center anyway. 
This is as a result of the cornering loads placed on the tire. Autocross
and slalom put more abrupt loads on the suspension, causing the tires to
immediately load in the corners. The sisdewalls tend to heat and stay 
heated more than the center tread in a SS 944. Cars with power to light
the treads tend to blur the readings because of the traction issue, 
iseally you should run the tires in a straight line to determine the 
traction correction factor to adjust you cornering data.

All of this is moot is you have a stiff suspension set-up or trashed
shocks, etc. Generally speaking, across the spectrum of users, the BFGs
are as advertised. You feel and how your car responded as you drove it
are the most important factors. I used some trash Firestones 
successfully against the Yokohamas for sometime, it's whatever works 
when you're winning.

You are running in a Miata, which has some better front suspension than
a 924/944/968. You should be running about 3/4 to 1 degree of camber in the front with 4 degrees of caster and 30 minutes of toe in. Your rear
 should be 1 to 1 1/4 degree of camber and less than 30 minutes of toe 
in but more than 15 minutes of toe in. Try not to lower your car, use 
KONI sport shocks. Adjust the suspension after installation of the 
shocks. When setting up the front, insure that the fronts wheels are 
getting  camber gain on the outside wheel under load (requires a friend 
to watch). With this set-up you should get more footprint cornering and 
most wear on the inside of the tire.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 13:46:03 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: HELP: buying 1986 911 Carrera

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: HELP: buying 1986 911 Carrera

A Carerra will most likely need a clutch at that mileage. Figure $1000 -
$1200. Also be aware that the alternators are a failure point (generally go
between 60K and 80K miles). It's the brushes that go on the alternator, so
your warning light won't come on (I found out by being stranded with a fully
drained battery). Figure $500.

Motors are very durable if not chipped. I know of several AutoAuthority
chipped cars that have cratered (I'm sure some of you will have comments on
this).

Trans is extremely durable -- unless the car was thrashed, I doubt the syncros
are bad. The '86 was the final year for the fully developed and very debugged
915 box.  All the later 915s with beefed dog teeth are harder to shift.

I'd question why the combo of 15s and 16s, as 7s and 8s will fit in either
diameter -- a larger contact patch would be with the 16s....

You didn't mention price. I wouldn't pay over $20K for this car with the
current market.  In Houston, lower mileage cars are going for around $20K.

By all means, have ANY car you consider checked out by an independent and
reputable Porsche shop.

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 16:43:53 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: RE: Titles, etc


The thing about frame straightening on a unibody is that the rippled or 
folded material has a point where the body will break. For a track 
vehicle that will only have a passenger compartment roll bar, I have a 
problem with that car.

When I built a pair of 914/6s for the track in the late 70s, I purchased
tubs that had had engine fires, but stayed away from a couple that had
burned badly enough to have buckled. Scorched is O.K., badly burned 
ignore. One tub had been hit in the front and had the front pan 
rearranged, I had it pulled knowing that I would be using a complete, 
full cage for the real work.

If you are going to weld in a cage, have someone knowledgeable with 
Porsche sheetmetal construction do it. The body structure will be pulled
out of line by the welding process. If you use a Thyssen steel chassis,
be advised that the flux material may have to be different than a normal
inert gas Chrome-moly tube frame. You WILL have to recheck all meaure-
ment points after the process and adjust the body structure accordingly.

This also applies to an undamaged tub. Remember, any point that you make
stiffer than the rest of the structure will have the load transferred to
it. Most of the cars I've built since those two cars had the tube 
frame built first, then the appropriate body panels added as necessary.
I never believed that SCCA really wanted "stock" vehicles in the 
production class anyway.

As far as title goes, it's a state issue. In california the smog Nazis
make you bring emissions  up to the year of the engine or the chassis, 
whichever is newer, whenever you  retitle a salvage, so be careful.

It brings up an interesting point, one could build a 1969 911T for SCCA
Vintage Trans AM out of a '89 or earlier Carrera tub which is much 
stronger. Just a few simple serial number changes......


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 20:52:49 EDT
From: rmohan@afit.af.mil
Subject: RE: buying a 911

Porsche dealers currently perform 90 point inspections on used Porsches to
determine eligibility for 1-year used car warranties (nationwide).  If your 
local dealer will do this for your prospective next car, a failing grade 
should definitely influence your purchase decision.

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 20 Jul 93 20:47:11 EDT
From: rmohan@afit.af.mil
Subject: RE: 944 engine mounts/buying a 911

Diagnosing bad 944 engine mounts:

	A quick seat-of-the-pants indicator of a bad motor mount is the level of vibration at low idle with no load on the engine.  It is most noticeable when the RPM reaches its lowest point after a quick rev with the cluth depressed.  Another test is coasting around a parking lot (motor running, cluth depressed, 10 mph).  When you straighten out after a 90 degree turn, and the vibration picks upsubstantially, you have a bad motor mount.

Buying your first 911: (again, mostly just my opinion)

- --83,000 miles is relatively high compared to competing cars available today.  My rule of thumb is 5000 mi/year.  Any more is a work/school car, and is over-exposed to the elements.  A lot less indicates a stored car, with its own special problems.
- --Does the car have a whaletail or a carrera tail.  The difference is critical in
determining OEM vs. aftermarket options.  A whaletail lip forms a tray on 3 sides, and the back of the base drops nearly straight down to the decklid.  A carrera tail lip curves up only at the back, and the base has a horizontal surface beneath the rubber lip.  The carrera tail can be a factory option.  The whaletail can be part of a special order option known as a turbo-body.  This option includes the whaletail, turbo bumpers (f&r), 9"&11" fender flares, turbo rockers, and turbo suspension pieces (a-arms, struts, torsion bars, sway bars, etc.), and turbo brakes.  A rule-of-thumb for factory turbo body options are the brake calipers and rotors visible thru the wheel centers.  If the rotors are cross-drilled, and the calipers are big, black, finned, and say PORSCHE in silver letters, its a factory job, or a good aftermarket job.  The only way to confirm this is to locate the vehicle paint/option code decal (location unknown) and cross-check the codes with a Porsche dealer's option catalog (not brochure).  Aft



ermarket cosmetic conversions can result in a number of different tails on the car, and are unfortunately all too common.  If the tail is a factory-installed item, recommend it stays with the car, or at least removed such that it can be replaced.
- --Tough shifts into second is no surprise at 83K miles.  All my 911's have had this problem prior to that distance.  Possibly the 2nd gear synchro, but probably the 1st-2nd gear slider, and naturally, more expensive.
- --'86 is a good year:  first 911 with a real air conditioner, last year for Euro Cars (I've had two, and no complaints), turbo-bodies were recognized for what they were, and were more common, but still rare.
- --'87 is my favorite (next to '91):  11 more HP with new DME map, G50 transmission with hydraulic clutch, and H7 headlights (much prettier).  No significant changes until '89 Carrera 4.
- --An '88 Carrera Targa w/45,000 miles, spoilers, black/linen leather, polished fuchs recently sold off my local VW dealer's lot for $17,500.  It needed a new muffler.

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 1993 09:14:04 -0400 (EDT)
From: RICHARD3300@delphi.com
Subject: RE Steve W Speedster Problems

My experiences with my Beck 550 might be of help to you.
First, cooling problems and oil temps. It's imperative that you locate the cause of the elevated temps.
After my cooler let go I found that it was improperly installed, blocking oil flow to the cooler.
Check it and the grommets before it's too late! Also make sure your shrouding is correctly installed (hopefully it's all there!).
Any leak around the spark plug boots and shrouding will elevate the engine temps.
Next check your timing - too much advance can be a cause of elevated temps and lousy offline acceleration.
I've no experience with the 050 dist. but the 009 seems ok and is retarded 3 degrees on #3. Make sure
(if you install one) that the number 1 mark on the dist is set up to fire on no. 1.
Otherwise you'll retard another cylinder and that doesn't do #3 anygood!
If your exhaust system isn't coated (jet hot or HPC) I'd recommend it as it 
really helps lower the engine compartment temp resulting in lower engine
temps - your engine will pull in cooler air this way. It helped my Beck
which has no engine campartment sealing. 

As for the Dell's - they publish a good book on them but you'll have to call
CB Performance for it (800/274-8337). They are good carbs and easy to rebuild
You can also install insulators under the carbs to help with your hot soak
prblem but if you lacate the cause of the higher temps that will help 
more than the insulators. I use em and they do work. They're made out of a 
high temp polymer and are about 1/8th" thick. Your fuel pump should have a
regulator (even the electric ones) as I've found they put out more than the 
rated 3 psi. In fact the Dell's like as low as 1.5 psi at idle. When you 
get the Dellorto performance book there's a section on how to change the 
venturi size and a chart based on engine displacement to locate the correct
venturi. I'd say form my experience you are probably in the ball park on 
venturi's. However the idle jets are ultra crit as most (80%) of driving is 
on the idles not mains. Too fat and off idle and mid range dies like you will
not believe! The mains are only in use at 3/4 to full throttle so they are
not as critical. Also float level is absolutley got to be right on. I had
mine to low and thought I had idel jet problems that I chased for a month.
When set up the carbs are good. Buy a Unisyn and sync left and right carbs 
as well as each barrel. This is described in the Dell book. Hope this helps
out.

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 09:14:35 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 931

Kevin, 

Nice to hear from a fellow 924 Turbo owner. I've owned mine since new.

On your J-pipe, you can weld it, however, it will break at the other end
after a period of time. The reason is that the pipe is swaged into the 
small cast that acts as a flange to allow for expansion and contraction 
in use. Normal vibration will cause this to fail.

There is a cure, I reweld the broken pipe to the flange, then draw a 
straight line on the Bottom part of the J. I use this for an alignment
mark as then I cut the pipe. I then weld a collar(a piece of stainless
steel exhaust pipe enlarged or fitted to slip tightly over the J-pipe)
to one of the halves. I slip the other piece in and reinstall on the car
using a u-clamp to tighten the pipe to seal the passage to the 
wastegate. This allows for thermal expansion and some vibration 
isolation.

There is a modification to the breather system on the US cars, the line 
from the trap on the Turbo oil return was routed to the head to 
eliminate this problem, we had a recall, I'm not sure it extended out of
the US but the bulletin # was 83-01, 16 September 1983.

931 suspension is identical to 924 and 1982-1985 1/2 944s. The article
does cover everything there, but is directed to 924/931 owners.

Door handles and parts can be derived from Volkswagen Golfs, 1975 to 
1982, when you're in a pinch.

Private messages are O.K.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo region PCA

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 12:49:59 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)


   I'm trying to replace the timing belt on a 2v 928, and was wondering
   what is the best way to remove and install the huge bolt that connects
   the main pully.

   I think this bolt is like 28mm and the torque for tighening is like 300Nm!

   much?

No way. You are going to have to lock the flywheel. You can improvise
and use a large screwdriver or prybar locked onto the teeth of the
ring gear, but it will require 2 people, one to lock the flywheel and
one to turn the bolt off with a breaker bar and cheater pipe if
necessary.

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 12:47:26 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: 912/356 engine stuff

>You seem to be knowledgeable regarding 912 issues.  You mentioned the "heat
>soak" problem to the guy with the 924 warm starting problem.  You said you
>knew the fix for 356's.  Can you let me know?  

It's the insulating block that someone else previously mentioned. This was
a stock item on the '68 and before 912s, and helped a lot. 

>I'd also like to know if there would be a substantial increase in acceleration
>if I changed from 36mm Dellorto's to 40mm Dellortos or Webers.  

I'm not a fan of Dellorto's except on BMW R90/S motorcycles... Again, contact
CB Performance for info. They're the acknowledged experts on Dellortos.

>I'm surprised that my 1600 lb Speedster Replica can't even keep pace with
>my girlfriend's Mazda Miata (9.5 sec/0 - 60mph right?).  I don't know the
>0 to 60 time for a 912, but it must have been VERY slow (12 seconds?).  I'm
>guessing that my Speedster's acceleration time is around 10.5 seconds/0-60
>even with the 1720cc kit.

Yup. That's about right. The 912 was *NOT* a very fast car, but it was a
spectacularly well balanced one. It's big problem was that it wasn't as
fast as either the 356SC or the early 911s (the obvious things to compare
it to at the time, right?). But people tried to drive it like it was anyway,
leading to some *AMAZING* engine failures. 

Something to remember is that the 912 engine made about 103 HP (SAE), and 
that even modified with the NPR kits, it will likely only make about 115 HP. 
You can trivially build a 130+ HP VW Type I engine, for about what you'd pay 
for a new 912 crank and set of pistons! Plus the VW has the offset "doghouse"
oil cooler, and a zillion aftermarket parts available. That's why most
Speedster replicas don't have 912 or 356 engines...

>One more question on distributors for the 912.  I've heard arguments for both
>the Bosch 050 and the 009.  Harry Pellow (The Maestro) says the 050 is 
>definitely the choice.  But I've heard other Porsche owners say the 050 will
>destroy your #3 cylinder, because unlike the 009, the 050 does not retard
>the spark on the #3 cylinder.  I guess the #3 cylinder runs the hottest on
>356/912 engines.  Any thoughts on this subject?  By the way, I'm currently
>using an 050.

Use an 050 or an 031. The advance on the 009 is just plain wrong for the
356 engine (and isn't too great for Type Is in hot climates either, BTW).

One more thing: Duanne Spencer, a 356 vintage racer guy, has written a book
called "The 356 Performance Handbook". It's worth owning if you have a 356
or 912 from which you want more power.

Stan

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 15:10:22 EDT
From: Hurf Sheldon <hurf@graphics.cornell.edu>
Subject: re:928 Damper bolt


   I'm trying to replace the timing belt on a 2v 928, and was wondering
   what is the best way to remove and install the huge bolt that connects
   the main pully.

   I think this bolt is like 28mm and the torque for tighening is like 300Nm!
   Do you think the compression of the engine is enough to counter act that
   much?

Ways I know of that work:
Put the car in gear with the brake on.

Put socket and breaker bar on (3/4" drive preferred ) with bar 
extending down below the car.

a; Brace yourself on the ground and push with your foot on the 
breaker bar (a kick or two should break it loose) or
b; hit the end of the breaker bar hard with a hammer a few times
in the right direction.

Remember to realign the timing marks before removing damper assembly.
If damper sticks and you don't have a puller just warm it with a propane
torch. It will slide off. A good hint is to take nail polish and
mark the cam drives before you remove the belt just for a sanity check
as the cams will 'pop' off their mark very easily and lead to some
confusion. It IS possible to have the crank pulley correct and the 
Cam pulleys 180o out with the belt on.

For retorquing use the reverse of method a;

A piece of pipe can be slipped over the torque wrench handle to give you
a little more leverage or you can put a 1ft drive extender 
(not an extension) between the torque wrench and the socket. This will
double the indicated ft/lbs. 

There may be an access hole in ther bell housing for locking the flywheel
but I was not able to discover one. ('79 928 )

hurf

------------------------------

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 09:17:00 TZ
From: Jim Harriger <jimharr@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Need help in 928 pully screw

john,

	what fun, eh? i did mine several years ago, but i'll pass along what i 
remember.

	i don't think the compression will hold the engine against this bolt. 
what i did was put all the spark plugs in, set the partking brake, and 
put transmission in 5th. then i blocked the rear wheels as well as i 
could, and used this to hold the crank. then i leaned on the bolt with 
a 24" breaker bar, both feet and some carefully chosen 4 letter german 
words :-).

	once you get the bolt out, you still have the problem of getting the 
pulley off. do you have a puller that fits?  i rented one, but it just 
*barely* fit betweent the pulley and the radiator. be very careful 
here, so that you don't dink up the radiator.

	one last admonition: double check the cam timing after you get it all 
back together, and get the belt tightened. i had my left cam one tooth 
off ( i don't remember which way) when i put camshaft seals in mine, 
and the engine started but ran really strangely. the nice thing is that 
the 2v 928 motors have clearance for the valves built into the motor; a 
mis-timing, or a broken belt won't cause major broken parts.

	good luck. if you need any other advice, drop me a note, and i'll see 
what i can remember about fixing mine.

						jim harriger

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 17:20:05 EDT
From: Paul B. Booth <paul@eye.com>

Howdy all-

What a flood of mail!  Looks like some serious pent-up frustrations are finally
getting let loose.



> An FOP (Frequently Occuring Problem) for ye all:
> 	Cracked J-Pipe.

My buddy George Hussey at Auto Atlanta actually recommends welding over a new
pipe -- he swears that the welded pipe will outlast a new one.
> 
> BTW: I had a problem with billowing smoke out of the exhaust for some time.
> It used to belch out by the time I got to the traffic lights near my house, but
> 
Kelvin-  oil smoke and goo in the filter box - got that too.  I've always
figured that it means the turbo bearing seals are going on both the exhaust and
the intake side.  The stuff that leaks into the exhaust smokes, and the stuff
that leaks onto the intake side gets blown all through the intake/throttle
system.  I've got a sneaking suspicion that all this oil film has a lot to do
with the idle problems we've all been seeing lately -- the smoky exhaust can't
do the O2 sensor any favors, and I know my aux air regulator is really sticky
inside due to soot and such...
> 
> 	OK it is the LH door handle.  Where you press the opening trigger it
> 	has a ball goint which goes into a little cast piece which then lifts a
> 	connecting rod to the opening/latching mechanism.  I want this little
> 	cast alloy piece, it is connected to the door handle by a small split
> 	pin which it rotates on.  If they possibly have it I will send a fax
> 	with a diagram (i used it here in OZ) and credit card details,
> 	basically I just want to find out if it is worth sending the fax first.
> 
On my doors that cast piece is plastic.  On the passenger side that piece
cracked, and I was able to fix it by wrapping it with one of those plastic
rachet ties.  Pulled the thing really tight then clipped off the tail.  Seems
to hold.  Your mileage (kilometage?) will probably vary.

> Thats about it for now, let me know what you guys are up to.....
> 
I'm good. My car sucks.  I've decided to pull the engine and (at least) change
the main bearings and probably the rings.  Will get the turbo rebuilt while the
whole thing's out of the car.  If I run into trouble (never done anything more
involved than a head gasket before), I'll part it out.

Regards,

Date: Mon, 12 Jul 93 13:13:35 PDT
From: Patrick Norris <ucdavis!Jupiter.SAIC.Com!norrisp@ucdavis.edu>
Subject: Re: 914 column 


After removing the steering wheel, there are four (4) standard headed
screws that hold the switch assemblies in.  The heads are small, just
about 3mm in diameter.

On the other end of these assemblies is what keeps them from being 
removed easily.  There are two electrical connections (one for the
turn signals/lights and the other for the wipers) and the hoses for
the pnumatic window washers.  The electricals are easy, take note how
it should look and remove them (actually, this will take some work
while you are upside down under the dash, but...)  The hoses are a little
different.

If you don't have the washer stuff anymore, you can cut the hoses and
remove the switch assemblies.  Otherwise, relieve the pressure from the
system(!) and pull the switch assemblies out (stetching the hoses) enough
to get at the phillips head scress to remove the hose connections.

As for the contact for the horn, try to flex the copper part to make
better contact.

If you have to replace them, these parts look a lot like the same parts
that are used on VW Superbeetles.  Does anyone out there know if they
work?  Can the VW units (somewhat easier to find at a salvage yard) 
be used?

Pat Norris

Date: Mon, 19 Jul 93 23:07:50 -0500
From: ucdavis!tta.com!stan@ucdavis.edu (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914-6 evaluation?

>Last friday I got a chance to look at an original 914-6...
>...
>Given that my interests
>are performance rather than originality, what does an original have
>to offer that a conversion won't have? (apart from a heater). 

If you take an original, and severely modify it, I'll personally hunt
you down and kill you... in the nicest possible way, of course 8{)

Seriously, there were fewer 914/6s made than 356 Speedsters. Think about
it. Hard. Do you really want to destroy something that rare and beautiful
just for some more go-fast??? Particularly, when as you note, you can
get a lot more a lot cheaper by getting a conversion???

One of the things that Ghost Racing does is restore 914/6s, both for ourselves
and for customers. We own, at last count, 7 or 8 (depending on how you count 
the little box of metal numbers and matching title). Some are concours grade 
restorations. Some are "drivers" (one took 2nd in it's class at the Little 
Le Mans high-speed autox event this year). But all are meticulously original, 
at least where it counts -- transmission and engine numbers match the chassis 
build sheet, or where that's not possible, are at least from the 914/6 series. 
No visible updates except for Carrera chain tensioners and Weltmeister swaybars
on the "drivers" are applied. Paint and interior match the build sheet.

Anything that deviates from the factory build sheet is something that
could have been done in '70-71 with factory sanction and parts (like
there are two which had factory steel GT flare kits added after the
fact, but that was how most of the cars got GT flares, so it's OK).

And as such, these cars are worth something. As examples of how 914/6s
should actually go together. As examples of the breed. As about 1 - 1.5% of
the surviving 914/6s. 8{)

Another thing we do is build conversions, either for race or street.
And that's a whole different ball of wax -- those cars have value mainly
because of the engine, which is a $10K+ investment, typically.

>Thanks for your thoughts on this.

Hope I didn't put you off too much with my opening line, but I do have
*VERY* strong feelings about modifying 914/6s, or '73-4 2.0s, particularly
the '74 Special Edition. Anything that's rare should be left as it is so
it can be enjoyed by posterity.

Stan

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 16:11:13 PDT
From: sfi@verity.com (Stefan Fielding-Isaacs)
Subject: my fuel injection problems



 As you all know (I think) I've been having problems
 with the fuel injection system on my 1.7l 914. It's
 been cutting out sporadically and coming back in just
 as sporadically. For a while I thought I had solved
 it (re-routing suspiciously poorly routed wires in the
 ignition system). Today, however, I was driving down
 the freeway and it started cutting out every 30 seconds
 or so (for several seconds at a time).

 I was at my wits end. My mechanic had thought it was
 a short in the wiring harness (because when he moved the
 harness around the motor cut out). I wasn't so sanguine.
 I considered a bad ground, a problem with the fuel injection
 trigger points, a flaw in the optically-triggered ignition
 (a retrofit item).

 The light finally dawned when I noticed that the car choked
 out a cloud of black smoke when it finally re-started after
 a low-speed cutout. A full rich condition was indicated.
 
 So I asked if there were any sensors or anything which could
 be malfunctioning. "Head sensor" was the immediate answer.
 So I fiddled with the head sensor while he sat in the driver's
 seat and lo and behold it (the fuel injection system) dropped
 out. Consistently. 

 So an oil change, a head sensor and $150 later I'm back on 
 the road. Drove into work and it works fine. Crossing my
 fingers that it solves the problem.

 Much better than replacing the harness.

 Thanks for all the net.advice..

 cheers,
 Stefan

Date: Wed, 21 Jul 93 17:25:54 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 931 Grunge.



Mike, 

Some of the grunge is generated by Turbo blow-by and the remainder by 
compression blow-by. To find out which is the culprit, you must do a 
leakdown test on your engine. 5% or less is good on a Turbo. 
The test is accomplished by using a special gauge and pressurizing the 
cylinder when the cylinder is at it's TDC. The guage attaches through 
the spark plug hole. After pressurizing the cylinder, one waits per the 
gauge instruction and reads the residual pressure. The difference is the
percentage of leakage in the cylinder. Usually this indicates rings but
the valves contributes also.

I would suspect rings on the pistons and cylinder wall wear in your 
case, fortunately, these are the least expensive (hard to believe, 
isn't it) of all the Porsche Turbos to repair or overhaul.

Make sure that the breather modification was done to your car per 
bulletin 83-01. This mod directs the turbo breather up to the valve
cover area and not to the seperator.

The 931 does not have a crankcase pressure sensor, the dipstick should 
be tight anyway. If in doubt, buy a new on (o-ring kludges don't work 
there, the fit is wrong).

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 93 04:58 GMT

Subject: Re: warm 924 hard starts

Mike -

>On my 931, the PCV return hose spews some gnarly looking oil-laden air
>into the air cleaner when the car is hot.  This fouls the air filter
>and, eventually, coats the whole air-mass sensor and intake plumbing
>with grunge as well.  Is this to be expected in a 13 year-old motor?
>Is carb cleaner an appropriate way to flush it out?

This was a problem, I think, with a 924 GTS race car I've had my hands
in recently.  Solution was to vent to a catch bottle instead.

>>  944 oil cap and dipstick. If you have a slightly rough idle and the 
>>  motor mounts have been changed, check your dipstick. The crankcase needs
>>  to be closed (like a 914). the rubber seal gets hard and leaks air. 
>>  Ditto for the rubber seal in the oil filler cap.
>Does this go for the 924/931 as well?  My dipstick is not quite air
>and liquid-tight, despite my o-ring kludges. :-|

Goes for any car.  Vacuum leaks from the filler cap, dipstick, induction
manifold, and any other possible source of "false air" (ie, air which is
not metered) will mess up idle noticably and overall performance less
obviously.

Kevin

models or designs.

Although Black cars are hotter to the touch in the sun, recent studies here 
in Arizona concluded (surprisingly) that paint color had little effect on 
the interior temperature of the vehicle. The Interior heat is caused 
primarily by the radiated heat through the windows. If you cover the windows 
 while parked.and crack open the sunroof or a window, you can lower the temp 
quite a bit but you probably know that already.

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 93 10:41:38 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: 911 Torsion Bar Bushings

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
Subject: 911 Torsion Bar Bushings

A couple tricks I learned while doing this recently:
Burn (yes, with fire) off the old bushings from the spring plates.  This is
simple: use a propane torch or even lighter fluid to start the rubber burning.
Make sure you place the spring plate outdoors in a spot where leaving a mark
on the ground won't matter (the rubber becomes molten and can drip). Wait until
the flame is completely out (make sure the bushing burns all the way too). Just
ashes will be left which are easily wiped off.

Clean the spring plate and it's corresponding bushing hold-down plate on the
bushing contact surfaces (if there is rust, remove with fine emery cloth).

I used a good coating of anti-seize as the lubricant on the bushing.

Make sure the new torsion bar is COMPLETELY coated with a thin layer of grease.

Once assembled, make sure there is NO binding of the spring plate.

I have a '72 that sees similar use. I found the combination of 22mm front, 27mm
rear torsion bars, with 22 mm sway bars front & rear works best.  I've tracked
and AX'd the car and can handle drives up to 200 miles or so (it IS stiff).

I have a combination of Sport Bilsteins up front and adjustable gas Koni's
in the rear (set to medium).

The Weltmiester bushings I used are the hard ones, and they just started
creaking after trailering the car to Parade and Evansville Nat'l Tour event.
(about 2300 miles of trailering).  Don't know why the creak, but it isn't
bothersome -- it is a race car after all.  I'm more interested in performance
than comfort, so you may want to opt for new or good used springplates or
the Weltmiester adjustable units.  I know of several people that use them
with no complaint of noise (the bushings wear in a couple of years, though).

Good luck.         -Scott

    Steven Stomski



------------------------------

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 93 10:59:50 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Kids and Interior heat.


The radiated heat in a closed car is UNBELIEVEABLE! Will Hartje is 
correct in how to disperse the heat to save the interior.

I recall that someone complained about the leather on their 928 dash 
"cooking" and going away. Test have shown that on a 90 degree day in 
direct sunlight in a closed car, the dash on a 944 can have a surface 
temperature of 250 degrees. That will melt most plastics in an hour's 
exposure.

By cracking the windows and using a car cover the effects can be 
minimized but mot eliminated. Over time, even a well protected and cared
for 924/944 dash will go away.

I use Armor-all for my dashes, seems to work. My turbo is original after
11 years, the 924 was replaced when I bought it 18 months old in 1982 
and is still in great shape. I know that the replacements are up to $700
and are no better that the originals. This is one of those bitches I 
have  with Porsche, you know you put out an inferior piece on the car, 
yet  you make the cost unbearably high on a cosmetic item that greatly
affects the value of the car for the owner. In this case, they haven't 
earned a repeat sale to that customer. I bet marketing wonders why I 
won't buy a new Porsche anytime soon, I need the value of the 
depreciation  to justify the purchase and pay for the repair of the 
badly done pieces when the car is two years old.

Anyway, use of the car cover and opening the sunroof/opening the 
windowsa crack helps a lot to keep the interior from going to hell in 
the heat.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal,com
Diablo Region PCA

Date:         Thu, 22 Jul 93 15:36-0400
From: Ken.Stanton@QueensU.CA
Subject:      924 warm start

Forget who mentioned the commonalities with the Scirocco, but I think he
may be bung on. I have had this problem with the 931 on and off for quite
some time. It's easy to troubleshoot the check valve at the fuel pump end.
Run the car. Shut it off. Immediately clamp the fuel line downstream from
the pump. Wait the required amount of time for the problem to usually
occur. Have someone unclamp the line as you attempt to restart it. I'd almost
take bets that the check valve at the pump is not faulty. I've been there.
The idea of fuel leaking at the injector end of the system sounds more
likely to me given the way my car has been checked out. The 924 injectors
do indeed just pop out of their holders (unlike 931 threaded versions) so
that is another easy thing to check. If the fuel accumulator doesn't
have fuel leaking through the side of it, I am told it is very unlikely
to be at fault. (Comes from a handful of useful tidbits put out through
Auto Atlanta -Thanx Paul). Otherwise, you are in for a thorough methodical
piece by piece test of the whole fuel system. Not as bad as it sounds.
The warm up regulator is a simple little thing that you can easily test
 -refer to Haynes manual for example.  Last resort: Change the air in the tires
;-)
   Ken.

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 93 14:49:06 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Armor-all


The favored method at the moment for removal of Armorall from the 
windshield and other glass in my garage is Dupont wax and grease 
remover (extreme cases) in a clean cloth rubbed on all areas of the 
glass. You will note the grunge that comes off. On hot days when the 
A/C is used there is some outgassing from the vinyl when the cool air
makes the vinyl contract, this hits the glass also.
Next, I use a window cleaner (Gunk brand, it's cheap) and wipe the 
residue from the wax and grease remover.
Lastly, I wipe the glass with a very damp (water) rag, then wipe before
it drys until the glass is nice and sparkles.

Read the Armorall instructions, apply it, then wipe off ALL excess, I 
usually rub it until there isn't a film. Do not leave a wet film, it
attracts everything like a magnet. Used correctly it isn't bad, it does 
retard deterioration.

I try a lot of different things for polish and concours prep, some work,
quite a few aren't worth the money. My current favorite for tire prep
is No-Touch when I prep with the wheels off the car. It gets in the 
tread and `floats' most of the grunge out. Makes a worn tire look 
pretty new. I don't like getting that stuff on the paint, it leaves 
smears when you wipe it. 

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 93 18:25:23 MST
From: john@sickbay.az05.bull.com (    John Jones)
Subject: 911 Top End Noise


I am looking for suggestions relating to a problem with my 1977 911S. A while
ago I took the 911 to a higher speed than was usual. Shortly there after I
noticed a very metalic noise comming from the area of the cam chain housing
of number 4 cylinder. Thinking I had lost a tappet I removed the top cam cover
but alas all was well. I removed the cam chain cover and with the exception 
of the chain rails all was well. Perhaps it was the chain and rails! I took the
opportunity to replace the rails and at the same time fitted later pressurised
chain tensioners. To my dismay when I restarted the engine the noise was still
there. So out came the engine. I stripped the right cam housing and checked
it. All ok! About this time I read an article describing the noise one gets
when a cylinder/head stud breaks. The description seemed similar. So I checked
the torque on all cylinders, some were low by a few ft.lbs so I re-torqued. I
had rebuilt the engine myself some 3000 miles earlier and had case inserts
fitted. The studs are however the originals. The only other observation I can 
make is that I noticed slight abrasion on the chain on the outer side on just
a couple of links. The engine is now sitting on the garage floor awaiting its
return to the car. Not satisfied that I have fixed it I just can't bring myself
to put it back. The only alternative I can come up with is to progressively 
strip the engine. Which of course I will have to do if the noise persists. My
reluctance to strip the engine is partly the time and effort and that I would
feel compelled to replace the oil pump with a higher capacity unit, chains etc
and on a 74-77 engine I'm not sure I want to invest the cash although the
general condition of both car and engine with only 69k miles is good.
Any comments??????

Date: Thu, 22 Jul 1993 21:46:29 -0400 (EDT)
From: RICHARD3300@delphi.com
Subject: The Real SteveW Speedster Info File

Opps. Sorry everyone, the SteveW fiel I've been trying to get to him for
hours is jinxed. I apologize for the garbage.

One more time.

Steve:

Some additional thoughts on the Speedster problems.

#1	I'm in agreement re the HP issue with Stan. However since your
Speedster should be lighter than the 912 was your power to weight ratio 
will be more advantageous. I think with the proper carb jetting and ignition
you'll come closer to the Miata.

#2	After thinking overnight onthe fuel/carb/hotsoak/starting/running
and performance issues a couple of other ideas came to mind. The bulk of 
your problems stem from excess heat and fuel. The engine ducting/shrouding
is important. i don't know what you have done re the heater and ducting but
the factory manual states that the engine will overheat in a big (destructo)
way without the proper back pressure provided by the heater ducts. In fact
they show a reducer that can be fabbed to produce the back pressure (dwell)
required for the airflow to be optimized over the heads. Can Fax this if you
like.

#3	You really must run a regulator, all the electrics put out too much
pressure for Webers or Dells. The Dells in fact like as little as 1.5 PSI
at idle. Put one on - instant big difference. Additonally the hot soak is
causing the fuel in the float bowls to expand and push past the factory
needle/seat assy. The previously mentioned insulators will ehlp but until
you get to the root cause of the elevated engine temp it's only a stopgap.
CB also has a rotary fuel pump with pressure relief line back to the fuel
tank to help withthe fuel expansion problem. Youmight also consider the 
ball/needle/seat assy sold by Berg that's superior to the factory part and
as an added benefit lets you run higher float levels benefitting fuel flow
into the emulsion tube well for better off line perf.

#4	Timing. If your's is to far advanced off line will be weak and the
engine will overheat. all air cooled engines run about 1 point over stated
CR that means 9.5CR=10.5. with today's fuel that is marginal at best. What
you want is optimum cyl charge to cool the head and jugs without too much 
fuel. If we retard the timing the bigger charge will cool the cylinder but
performance will suffer. With advanced timing you will destroy the engine
and you won't even hear the pinging! So to strart set the timing at factory
specs (static method) and then play with all the other variables.

If you do that, then put on the pressure regulator, sync the carbs and so 
forth it should start to come into line.

Oh, if the engine is back firing during trailing or partial throttle decell
the idle jets are partially blocked and need to be blown out. You probably
have a filter in place and it should be replaced if any doubt exists to it's
effectiveness. i like the clear onesyou'd never believe the junk in the gas
today.

Finally, if everything gets dialed in and it still doesn't run the only other
solution I know of is to buy a drum of 114 octane race fuel (got mine after
Sebring) and cut it 50/50 with pump fuel. Sometimes depending on who built
your engine they get the CR up too high and there's no other cure short of a 
rebuild.

BUt cheer up I don't think we have to do that just yet!

Richard

Porschephiles #459                         Fri Jul 23 23:04:24 CDT 1993
Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 03:28 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 911 Top End Noise

John -

Could you be more specific about the metallic noise?  We talking single
clunks or continual grinds?

Given the wear, when you rebuilt the top end did you restore the shims
properly to the correct cams?  There is a procedure for checking the
cam sprocket offsets.  Generally you don't need to do it during a rebuild
if you restore the shims and sprockets to their original location, I
believe.  When we take an engine apart, we put put a cable tie through
the hardware for a given side, and scribe an "L" or "R" on the sprocket.

Kevin

>can lead them to it, but I've got a three year old complaining about the

>a black car.

1. Lie to them about the colour.  "It's not black, its really dark blue."
You can get away with this.  You're the big person.

2. Promise to do "voy," or whatever your kid's ideoglossic term is for
a roller coaster ride in the Porsche.  We have a few twisty, curvy roads
which the little ones love.  The elevation changes where the car gets
really light are highlights.  I think seeing Mom turn green is part of
the fun for them too.

Dysfuntional and damn proud...

Kevin

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 10:33:17 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: warm starts


	   I am actually posting this for a friend of mine who owns a
   1980 911 SC (European model, 3.0L)....

   After driving for an extended period of time (when the engine has
   been heated up for a while) and the car is parked for over
   fifteen minutes, the car has a problem starting.  It seems to be
   leaning out of fuel.  After a few cranks and waiting a minute, the
   car will start but runs roughly(seems to be leaning out..?!)  After
   running fora while, the car returns to its normal running state.  This
   has been a frustrating problem for the past three weeks.  I took the car
   in to a local mechanic who said the car was running lean and adjusted 
   the air-fuel ratio.  This seemed to help for a few days only.  Please help.

Check the residual system fuel pressure by tapping a gauge into the
fuel system between the warm-up regulator and the fuel distributor.
You may have a defective fuel pump check valve which is allowing
system pressure to bleed off as the car sits for a few minutes. The
fact that cranking solves the hot-start indicates renewed fuel
pressure after the fuel pump activates at each turn of the ignition
key.

Another way to check is to jump 12V to fuse #4 (I believe this is
correct for the fuel pump) as the car is sitting and leave it running
for a few minutes while the car is off. Then try a hot-start and if it
fires right over, you definately have a leaky fuel system pressure.

Then adjust car back to proper air/fuel ratio...

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

------------------------------

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 10:48:12 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 911 Top End Noise


   Given the wear, when you rebuilt the top end did you restore the shims
   properly to the correct cams?  There is a procedure for checking the
   cam sprocket offsets.  Generally you don't need to do it during a rebuild
   if you restore the shims and sprockets to their original location, I
   believe.  When we take an engine apart, we put put a cable tie through
   the hardware for a given side, and scribe an "L" or "R" on the sprocket.

In addition to Kevin's recommendation:

With regard to cam sprokets, make absolutely *sure* that you put the
cam sprokets on in the proper orientation. Although the cam sprockets
are identical, one of them goes on with the convex side *out* and the
other goes on with the convex side *in*. Check the manual for the
exact details. If you accidently put the both on the same way,
everything might *look* OK but if you didn't measure sprocket offsets
like Kevin says then your chains will die a grinding death eventually.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

------------------------------

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 10:59:30 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 911 Torsion Bars - Continued


       1- Any suggestions on setting the ride height once I've changed
       the bars?  (I am going to be using stock spring plates)

Hmm, last time I discussed this Chris Welty got mad at me...

       3- I have adjustable Konis on the rear, can I stiffen these
       up enough to compensate for the heavier torsion bars (from 23mm to
       26mm)?  Also, how do I adjust these, do I just push them in all
       the way and turn?  How much do I turn?

Push them in *all* the way and then turn clockwise until you can't
turn anymore. When you get to this point you are at maximum stiffness
(or vice-versa I can't remember). Then turn all the way counter-clock
while pushing all the way in and *count the turns* until you reach the
stop again. Now you have some idea how many turns there are
lock-to-lock. If you turn but never reach the end then you aren't
pushing in hard enough. It takes a while to get a feel for it.

       4- I have not secured a set of front torsion bars yet so I will be
       running the stock ones (19mm) until I have.  I understand that
       with an increase in the rear stiffness but none in the front I
       will be increasing the oversteer tendency of my car- is this true?
       If yes, with his setup is it a serious (i.e. "dangerous") increase
       or something I'll be able to deal with?

Depending on the combo (you say 19/26) you might render the car
undrivable on high speed turns, unless you actually *like* backing
into your accidents or you want to experiment with a  bit of 
agricultural racing!  Then again sideways can be fun (not fast) 
in an autocross.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 09:33:01 TZ
From: Jim Harriger <jimharr@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 928 Exhaust Parts sources wanted

the frog? hmm... mine is nicknamed The Pig, mostly due to its rather 
prodigious thirst for petrol. yea, i could understand how seeing a 
blue-green one with the lights up could result is jeers of 'Kermit the 
Porsche' from passerby :-).

anyway, i'd go stainless if you can afford it. beware that if you go 
with ANSA or Bursch, you may wind up with a car that is loud and 
'boomy' at low rpm, esp. idle.
i have an ANSA rear muffler on mine, and i can hardly stand to drive it 
into a parking garage. at speed (>2k rpm) it sounds OK, but i like the 
oringal muffler with its 'sleeper' sound.

i think brian allman of northstar motorsports quoted me about $1700 for 
a borsch stainless, from the cat back, for my 928. if you're not too 
concerned about originality, you might see if you can find someone who 
does custom exhaust work and see what he can build for you. i saw a 928 
once that had a standard oval muffler of some sort stuffed the back 
left corner, instead of the steel dumpling, and it sounded almost like 
original.

good luck.

   saying not to do a 4->6 conversion that I wanted some input on
   that. Just to see if there were any good reasons not to do it from
   an end result point of view. As long as apart from having the key
   on the right and a 475.... VIN I end up with a car that has that

This is always an interesting subject, Yves. Many Porsche enthusiasts
object to hacking at original 914/6's, and that's fine. I don't quite
liken the crime to melting down an original Roman sword haft to make a
bracelet, but some hardcores might equate the two events.

But what really facinates me is the argument from original 914/6
owners that goes like this:
"Don't make a conversion 4->6 because you will be using parts such as
exhaust, oil tanks, engine sheet metal, etc., which will subtract from
the available pool of parts that can be used to sustain the original
sixes."

So you see either way you're gonna offend someone down the line. Best bet is
to take all original  914/6's and seal them in a converted aircraft hangar
filled with argon and everyone buys an MR2.

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 13:54:18 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 cages/PCA legal

>2. Any problem's with 914 cages in general, and Autopower's in particular??

Well, they don't fit right (i.e. you can't put the top on) if installed the 
way they should be installed (i.e. legs terminate on the door sills), and 
aren't bent right to install the other way (i.e. legs terminate on the 
floor)... I usually solve this by bending things so that the legs terminate
on the floor, but touch the vertical part between the floor and door sill,
then weld all metal-to-metal contact points for maximum stiffness.

>3. I wanted to initally run in pruduction class PCA. I was 
>   told that the Autopowers cage was a "structural cage"
>   ie. the cage attaches to the shock towers. Is this accurate,
>   and is it temporarily(sp?) avoidable??

Not on the 914. It's a four-point cage. Note that *ALL* of the shock
towers are inaccessible from inside the passenger compartment, and that
it's pretty hard to get to them in general (engine compartment in  way
going to the back, gas tank to the front). Unlike, say, any of the 
waterpumpers...

>4. I would prefer to have a mild steel cage, as apposed to 
>   a chromoly cage. Does anyone have any experience to deter
>   me from this idea.  ie. That's what I lack.

Do the mild steel. For starters, chromoly is illegal in SCCA and I think
PCA club racing...

>Hope it's not too much. Forgot to say that I have ofcourse
>read the PCA rulebook thought I have not memorized it. What 
>I should really have asked is wether or not I could, purposely 
>not install the links to the shock towers, so as to make me 
>production class legal, and later if I make it put the rest in.

I'm working right now to figure out where to cut and weld to make it
possible to install rear loop supports that tie to the rear shock
towers on a 914. It's not looking like a fun job. Just getting the

Stan

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 13:11:53 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Kermit the 928

Hi Guys, 

There is a very tired looking 928 in the San Jose area with Audi-X
stencilled on the side. No, it isn`t an AMC product masqurading.

About 928 exhausts, years ago I took the cat off a 928 with the rest of
the  exhaust system. I used two 27" glasspacks reversed where the cat 
went and a pair of Corvair turbo mufflers where the original tin can 
went. I connected the two tracts at the 24" point with a crossover, 
this is about 6" ahead of the glasspacks. Worked very well and was 
quiet until top revs were hit. About a 15 hp increase on the chassis 
dyno. The 928 was a '79.  The reversed glasspacks cut the resonance and the tendancy to pop under deacceleration. They also balance the CIS by
providing a little backpressure.


Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 12:46:33 -0700
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject:  911 Torsion Bar Bushings


    >I am getting ready to change out my rear torsion bars on my 1971
    >911 (from stock to 26mm).  In the process I would like to replace
    >the bushings as well (I am pretty sure the existing ones are
    >spent).  Weltmeister makes a poly-graphite bushing (2 hardnesses-
    >one performance and one street) but I understand they are nosiy
    >and offer less than a smooth ride.  I track this car but it is
    >also a driver (I drive it to the track) so I don't want too harsh
    >of a ride.  Are Weltmeisters that bad?  Does anyone know of any
    >other alternatives?  Can Weltmeisters be made quiet (noise bothers
    >me more than a harsh ride)?

I replaced the rubber bushings on the rear spring plates of a 69 911T
with the Weltmeister "softer" bushings. 

A sharp kitchen knife was used to cut off the bushings, and a wire
wheel on a drill cleaned off the small pieces.   

>From my experience, the aftermarket bushings WILL squeak. 
I even spirally grooved one of the bushings before smearing in grease 
and it still squeaked.

The bushings should come with a strong "squeak" disclaimer.  

Some time ago, someone mentioned that zerk fittings can be added to allow later
regreasing of front bushings.  For the rear bushings, zerk access
to the inner bushing would probably require a grease access hole
to be drilled through the spring plate.  Maybe someone has done this.

Have you considered swapping the left/right spring plates?  The rubber
bushing's wear surface is the top, so you should have a relatively
good bottom on the stock spring plate's rubber bushings.  
The loading on the internal splining of the spring plates will reverse
direction, I don't know if this is a problem.  

I talked to one owner who used the swap method and was happy with the results.
But I have no experience with it myself.


John Wright


------------------------------

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 13:28:05 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 914/6 Hacking


John, 

The source for the quotation in your Email Is me, I engaged in a running
arguement with John Goodspeed about his conversion kit. I have done 
conversions  myself using wrecked 914/6s for the source, I prefer the 
original method for mounting the engine in the chassis. I consider these
cars rebodies rather than conversions as there is a provision for an 
extensive unit-body replacement in the Cal Vehicle code. All of these 
cars were for track use.

At the time (about 1976), heat exchangers were about $290 each, pretty 
steep. There weren't any oil tanks excepting the limited supply of
Sportomatic tanks built and not used. Porsche's attitude was that they
wer not interested in producing parts for a discontinued unsuccessful
model. At the time, there were some parts, maybe enough for 150 cars in
critical areas. So, if you used all this stuff for counterfit 916/6s, 
then what was i supposed to do when I needed parts. Goodspeed was
creating a run on the only source I had to support my 914/6.

Fortunately, in the wacko world of Porsche, a few Southern california 
guys started making and seeling all these pieces except copies of the 
heat exchangers. Porsche's continuous raising of prices has insured that
fewer people will buy genuine parts from them. Have you checked the most
recent price of those heat exchangers thqat were built in 1979?  

The quote can be found in one of the Upfixin' books. Some remembrance 
for posterity.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Fri, 23 Jul 93 15:10:21 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914-6 hacking

>But what really facinates me is the argument from original 914/6
>owners that goes like this:
>"Don't make a conversion 4->6 because you will be using parts such as
>exhaust, oil tanks, engine sheet metal, etc., which will subtract from
>the available pool of parts that can be used to sustain the original
>sixes."

Sure. And it's also *EXPENSIVE* to get original 914/6 stuff to use in
a conversion. So, *DON'T*. Use real racing-grade oil tanks, and Aeroquip
lines with the Pegasus 911-to-AN-fitting adapters. Use the fiberglass
"sheet metal" kits. Use the "Quick-Six" mounting bar. This is *NOT*
a hard problem. And, interestingly enough, you wind up with a *BETTER*
car than the original 914/6.

So you really can do this, and not piss anyone off. And get a fun car.
Cheaper than if you'd done an "authentic" conversion or started with
a real 914/6!

>So you see either way you're gonna offend someone down the line. Best bet is
>to take all original  914/6's and seal them in a converted aircraft hangar
>filled with argon and everyone buys an MR2.

Now that's an idea!...

>
>The thing about frame straightening on a unibody is that the rippled or 
>folded material has a point where the body will break. For a track 
>vehicle that will only have a passenger compartment roll bar, I have a 
>problem with that car.
>
>When I built a pair of 914/6s for the track in the late 70s, I purchased
>tubs that had had engine fires, but stayed away from a couple that had
>burned badly enough to have buckled. Scorched is O.K., badly burned 
>ignore. One tub had been hit in the front and had the front pan 
>rearranged, I had it pulled knowing that I would be using a complete, 
>full cage for the real work.
>
>If you are going to weld in a cage, have someone knowledgeable with 
>Porsche sheetmetal construction do it. The body structure will be pulled
>out of line by the welding process. If you use a Thyssen steel chassis,
>be advised that the flux material may have to be different than a normal
>inert gas Chrome-moly tube frame. You WILL have to recheck all meaure-
>ment points after the process and adjust the body structure accordingly.
>
  Welding induced warpage can be a BIG problem if you are not using the
  proper jig to force the body into rigidity, even then deflections occur.

>This also applies to an undamaged tub. Remember, any point that you make
>stiffer than the rest of the structure will have the load transferred to
>it. Most of the cars I've built since those two cars had the tube 
>frame built first, then the appropriate body panels added as necessary.
>I never believed that SCCA really wanted "stock" vehicles in the 
>production class anyway.

Thanks Jim, but where were ye when I really needed ya?

This thread brings up a few things that have been eating me.

How much rustoration can a 914 withstand and still be considered OK for
autoxing?  Assuming of course the work was done well.
After seeing Matt's thread about his amateur repairs and relegating his 914
to ICE RACING only (but dont track or ice racing create the same loadings?).
How amateur and what was repaired?

At a recent tech session, Bernard Von Nolting (of 356 restore fame in FRG.)
was asked why he gas welded everything.  His reply was gas welding left the
steel as 'soft' as original and MIG welding 'hardens' the affected zone.

I have heard of this before, but the hype claims MIG is good for anything.
What is the truth?  Are we splitting hairs?

Having done the most on a 914 [in the whole world?] with respect to rust
repairs, I used both oxy-acetylene and mig as needs dictated.
There are times when a torch just makes things worse, that's when mig
to the rescue, then finish with the torch to smooth and penetrate.

After three years of effort, am I in for a letdown since my tub is 80%
MIGged?  Will my car toast me the first time it hits something harder
than a deer?  

One last item:
The body stiffener kit weltmeister offers for 914's...
Are ALL the plates neccessary?
Reason I ask is, i can beleive flexure at the inner top of the wheel well
and a reinforcment plate for it, but the 'boxing' plates for the suspension
and engine mounts -that area is fairly robust, does it really need the
extra stiffness?   I suppose I can 'heat-n-beat' the bastards in place...
Its already two thicknesses of steel and 3 in some places (trust me i know).

Date: Sun, 25 Jul 93 18:40:07 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: installing 911s suspension in 914

>Next weekend I'll be installing a 911S (early '70s) version
>suspension in my 914. Are there any gotchas that I should watch
>out for?
>
>The rear suspension looks trivial - I'm just replacing the disk
>with one drilled for five bolts and bolting on a 6x15 Fuchs. The
>front looks quite a bit more complicated as I'm also replacing
>the brake calipers along with the struts.

Umm.  If you're *REALLY* doing a swap to 911S, including the vented
rear rotors, then you have a surprise coming.  It is *NOT* as simple 
as it looks. The vented disks are quite a bit thicker than the solid 
ones, and as a result, you have to put spacers in your rear calipers. 
Of course, once you find/fab a spacer you have a *BIGGER* problem, 
since the little rubber O-rings that seal the caliper halves are NLA 
and you'll need a second set too (one for each caliper/spacer interface). 
You also have to machine some amount off of the diameter of the disk. 
Somewhere I have the exact dimension, but that shouldn't be too 
hard -- just measure a solid disk and match calipers. You also have to shim
the calipers from their mount points too, something like 1/2 the thickness of
the spacer you add between the caliper halves, for obvious reasons.

If you're keeping solid disks in the rear, you just drop the new one on
and don't have to do anything to the caliper.  And, as a time-saving hint,
you can use 912 disks again having to remove some from the diameter.  *BUT*, 
if you drill the hub for 5-bolt instead of using real 914/6 hubs, and it's 
not a '70 hub with the majik reinforced lumps where you are supposed to drill 
for 5-bolt, you may have hub failure at some point when you least need it 
(if it's a track/autox car that get stressed a lot).

The front is a routine bolt-on operation. You just swap (at minimum)
the struts and reconnect the brake lines. You *can* also (and should
it it's possible to do so) swap the A-arms, the steering rack, and the
brace at the same time. Just un-bolt the top of the strut, unbolt the
A-arm mounts, and disconnect the steering. In-and-out.

stan

Date: Sun, 25 Jul 93 20:26:34 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914-6 Dashboards

>I just stumbled upon a small gathering of the PCA Central Coast region.
>I looked at an original 914-6 that was there and I spoke to it's owner.
>The car had a very nice wooden dash?  He said that this was very rare
>and I have never herd of this before.  Does anyone know of the numbers
>of these wooden dashes that were installed or were these installed at
>dealers?  Where any put into the other 914's (non sixes)?

Wooden dashs were not offered as options on any 914s, even 6s, from 
what I can tell. In general, wooden dashes were things found in luxury
cars, *NOT* sports cars, until the exotic and semi-exotic cars started
turning into GTs and not road-going versions of race ready cars. And
that really didn't start until the mid-70s.

The 914 also fell most decidedly into the "economy" part of the Porsche
product line. It's not logical that they would have offered leather interior
or wood dashes on main production cars. However, both seem to have found their
way onto the "family" cars and some of the 916s.

My guess is that if it *IS* original, then it's one of the Peich family cars
that got loose and drifted to the US. But best guess is that it is one of 
those numerous aftermarket conversions done way back in the sands of time,
possibly when the car was nearly new. Only the factory cardex can tell us
for sure...

Stan

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 09:18:26 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: misc. 914 and 944

> Another 944 question, does anybody know how to simply fix the
> window control switch?  I am referring to the rocker switch on
> the door.  The passenger side works from the driver side control
> panel, but not at the site.
> Pam

Sorry for the late reply - I've been travelling for a week.

The passenger switch runs as a slave to the driver's side window 
switch.  A defective driver switch could still operate the passenger 
window but would inhibit direct passenger operation.  Alternatively, 
you could have a bad passenger switch too - try flipping them all 
around and see what happens (they're interchangable).

		JBS

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 10:10:49 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: Re: Warm starting problem with '77 924

> >	- When the car is cold -- it starts up fine.
> >	- When the car is hot  -- it starts up fine.
> >	- When the car has been driven, and then let sit for
> >	  45 minutes to an hour, it sits and cranks for about 20 to 30
> >	  seconds before kicking in.
>
> This is the "heat soak" problem. I know how to fix it in 356 and 914s,
> but don't have a clue on the 924. I can ask around.
>
You can confirm if it's heat soak by getting the car hot (as you 
describe), then let sit with the hood open for 45-60 minutes, and 
restarting.  If the cooler engine starts fine, then you've likely got 
an engine-compartment heat problem that vaporizes the gasoline in the 
fuel line.

It's strange that the car has always done this - sound like there's 
still something wrong in your 931 vs. other 931's.  Have you examined 
all the fuel lines (bent, crushed, etc.)?  Check for missing insulation 
on fuel lines, or incorrect cooling of turbo or radiators.  Many older 
cars get hacked by unknowing owners, and important ducting is removed.  
Just some thoughts...

		JBS

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 10:52:20 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Camber on a 1971 911


       suggestions I am planning on changing the driver's side trailing
       arm back to the original to see if that changes the camber.  I
       would prefer not to do that so please suggest away.

Definately a possibility of a bent trailing arm. Even a small warpage
can account for several degrees of camber/toe.  Make sure the car is
settled by driving around for a few miles. I had a similar problem
which was completely cured with a fresh steel trailing arm.

       accurate- if so what is the conversion rate?  What should I be
       getting for camber readings?  What is optimal for track, street,
       etc.
I would suggest starting out with -1 degree camber and -.5 degree toe.
Then you can begin to tweak these settings. I assume you are running
6x15 wheels. Toe will probably have a more profound effect on your
perception on the car's "handling".

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 11:16:44 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 944 waterpump


   How do you know that a waterpump is getting bad. Is inspection and observation 
   of leakage the only way ?. Do thermostats go bad like from 0 to 100% or is this 
   a gradual process of opening later and incomplete ? 

Other than to check for leakage, you can remove the upper plastic
timing belt cover without too much difficulty and feel for play in the
main waterpump drive pully. It's the smooth one which is driven by the
back of the timing belt. If you grasp the top of the pully you should
not feel any movement at all, if you do then the bearing is on the way
out and must be replaced.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 09:08:10 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: 914 rebody


Kyle,

I suppose the limits of resoration are up to you, it might be more
cost effective to dip the car in a stripper like Harvey Smith use
to do to the 356s. It's a matter of worth to the owner.

As for gas welding versus tig, mig and inert gas, well, on the poor 
quality metal (mild steel) Karmann used for 356 911 and 914 panels it
doesn't matter. There will be more hardness with tig or mig in the 
welded are, but I suspect that Von Nolting is employing an assembly
method similar to the original at the factory, plain gas welding allows
for working a little to blend into existing panels. By the way, most
Porsche models are welded with an electro-gas robot these days at the 
factory.

If you have a 914 that was rusty and you're repairing it, stick with
complete panel replacement. More expensive but stronger. I retired 
my 914/6 in 1981, repaired all the panels ( both rockers, frame member
under the battery and jack pockets) qand the present owner time trials
the car with not apparent problems. That car had the 914/6 GT stiffener
kit installed early on, seemed to be the right thing to do.

A little story about the trailing arms, if you time trial or race, 914
(all) will bend the trailing arms. Race rubber will do it very quickly,
so do it early. I bent a right rear arm at Laguna Seca years ago, I 
couldn't  understand why I was going faster until I realized that the 
right rear had 3 1/2 degrees of negative camber. 

One other thing to you low-rider 914 fans, teh rear train arms don't 
have a lot of travel up-ward. The arm will hit on the engine compartment
sheetmetal and bend when it makes contact under hard cornering. Besides,
the cars handle and ride like buck-boards. Bends shocks, too.

As long as your welds were even and consistant to good metal, you should
have no problems. Just remember to note  where the factory welded. I 
diped my 914 so I could get to the welded seams and see what Karmann 
did, I wound up miging all the seams as they did not do a good job. Or
maybe they did, realizing how poor the mild steel was. Just doing that 
made the  race car structure very strong. 

have fun,

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 10:08:54 PDT
From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: 911 interior light prob.

	Why is it that when we experience a failure we/I "always"
	expect it to be a) something complex
			b) something expensive
			c) something done by an after-market supplier?

	I fixed my interior dome light problem!

	To recap, the dome light door switch position was shorted "ON".
	This disenabled the alarm system, and other systems that sensed 
	"door opening". This is on an '87 911 Carrera.

	The first assumption that I made,"It must be one of the expensive
	control boxes" proved false.

	The assumption the dealer made "You have removed the buzzer that
	sounds when the key is left in the ignition, what do you expect?"
	Obviously false!

	The assumption sugested by several P-philes, "The jerk who installed
	the burgular alarm screwed up the wiring" although most likely,
	proved false!

	So you ask, "What was the problem, John?"

	Well, .... long pause. Scene moves to Stutgardt.

	Wilhem the welder, welded the ground lug on the passenger side 
	interior roof connection in the wrong position, shorting the 
	switch/sense ground on the lamp housing to the chassis ground.

	Leonardo the lamp installer, found the short, and called Igor the
	inspector who agreed that there was a problem, summoned Tino the 
	tape installer over, who applied a length of black fabric tape over
	the offending connection, and voila everthing worked,...for five years.

	So,.... The Factory screwed it up.

	Anyway, all is fixed with a couple if well placed pieces of heat-shrink
	tubing, hopefully for another five years!
 
	When all else fails, Persevere!

John Tilden
Data I/O Corp.
tilden@data-io.com

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 12:25:54 CDT
From: ahusain@nis.naitc.com (Afsar Husain)
Subject: Re:  Sunroof is acting funny

 |
 | Lately the Sunroof on my 85/2 944 has decided to function at its own will.
 | It has a pop-up switch and sometime the switch works and the roof
 | pops open and sometime it doesn't.
 |

I was the original poster and wanted to thank to all those who
responded. I just wanted to follow-up and let the folks know my
solution. 

Well the problem with my sunroof was not the micro switch but the
triangular plate on the sunroof that activates the switch. The plate
was very lose and hence was unable to depress the micro-switch properly.
All I did was to remove the three rubber caps (these rubber caps are
located in the inside middle and the front of sunroof) and tightened the
screws behind it.

Now my new problem is that when the ignition key is in the 1st position
(first click where electrical items are activated), the arm does
not retract away from the panel and I cannot get the roof to detach.
I haven't checked the switches in the back yet (next weekend task) and
just wanted to get the group wisdom beforehand. Is there any other way
to detach the sunroof.

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 12:57:23 CDT

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 11:42:09 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Wooden Indians and Dashboards


For you spotted owl freaks:

MG Mitten and JC Whitney offered aftermarket wood panel dash 
enhancements (enchanments?) for a variety of cars, the 914 being one of
them. They sold a lot, judging from what I've seen in Porsches I've
judged at concours over the years. There was a choice of oak, mahagony,
teak and monkey wood. They left a little in fit and finish quality.

It is a certainty that any you find in any Porsche are aftermarket. It 
is one option the factory SHOULD have offered in the 914.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA
