Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 11:40:00 PDT
From: Sherri Seybold <Sherri@asymetrix.com>
Subject: RE: SOS 944 Bay Area mechanic needed


Though I now live in Seattle, I bought my '74 911 from my parents,
who still live in San Carlos.  When they told me they'd had the engine
rebuilt at San Carlos German Motor Works, I figured they'd probably
chosen the shop by driving down El Camino until they found a shop
with "German" in the name.  So, I called up Bruce Anderson, (PCA
Nat'l Tech Chairman) who lives in Sunnyvale and asked him what he
knew about the shop's reputation.  He said that if he were to choose
any shop on the peninsula (maybe he said Bay Area) that would be
the one.

Now that I've had the car for a few years, I'm not sure I'd agree.
There's two things I have a complaint with.

One:   2.7 911 engines are notorious for the studs pulling, and though
they replaced the stock studs with what appear to be Dilavar studs,
they didn't use Time serts, so now the really expensive Dilavar studs
are pulling out of the really cheap stock serts.

Two:  the second-most notorious flaw in a 2.7 engine is chain tensioner
failure.  When I called them up and asked them why they didn't replace
the stock tensioners with the Carrera tensioners, they said they didn't
believe that the tensioners were really the true root of the problem, that
the ramps (I think) were really the cause of tensioner failure and they'd
used the new, better ones.  OK, so 5,000 miles after the rebuild, why did
my left timing chain go from sounding perfectly normal to sounding like
my garbage disposal does when there's a spoon stuck in it, in the space
of about 5 minutes?  Since it also happened about 100 miles from home,
on grid for an autocross that I couldn't afford to lose the points for, I 
was
doubly ticked off.

Though I know there are flocks of people out there who can attest to their
2.7 running for 100K miles without either of these updates, my personal
opinion is that a competent shop would do them as a matter of course
during a rebuild.  Wish I could recommend an alternative shop . . .

Sherri@Asymetrix.com

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 14:58:43 EDT
From: sundt@cc.bellcore.com (Mark Sundt)
Subject: 911S cooling (was Re: Top End Noise (911S))

>On a different subject. Living in Arizona I have a problem with the engine
>running hot in the summer. The car is fitted with the standard cooling pipe 
>under the front fender. I should say was. I fabricated a cooler from a couple
>of VW auxillary oil coolers. It improved the running temerature some but I
>would like it lower. Has any one sucessfully used an aux oil cooler with
>100+ temperatures on the 74-77 engine? The engine oil cooler is clean, clear
>and in good shape. The temperature diverter valve under the rear fender was
>re-built. The Fan on the car is an 11 blade. It is equiped with air
>conditioning but the compressor belt is removed. It is run on synthetic
>oil for the added temperature margin. This car is non-catalytic, has an air
>pump and no egr.

John,

I used to own a '77 911S with factory "temperature diverter valve" and
lines to the front fender well. Instead of the "trombone" cooling coils, I
had installed a radiator-type cooler from Alan Johnson Racing. All by
itself, it may not have been better than just a trombone. The problem is
getting air through the cooler. Depending on the type of cooler you
installed, you might have the same problem. Since most of my stressful
driving was at speed around race tracks, all I needed to do was remove the
front right turn signal and box-in the cooler with sheet metal to make sure
all the air coming through the turn signal was going through the cooler
(just removing the turn signal was not enough!). This had a dramatic result
and all my heat problems went away. If your problems are in stop and go
traffic (or you really need your turn signal) then you might note that '84
and later Carreras (I'm pretty sure) had a thermostatically controlled fan
that blew air through the radiator-type cooler in their front fender well.
I'm sure, though, that any of these Carrera pieces are very expensive...

Hope this helps.

Mark
- --
Mark Sundt, Bellcore, (908) 758-3134
sundt@cc.bellcore.com or uunet!bcr!sundt

My pick is HP Carrera Motors in Burlingame, Henry Paszkiewicz - Owner/Tech.

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 1993 16:29:45 +0100
From: kopp@noao.edu (Greg Kopp)
Subject: Hot 2.7l (was: Top End Noise (911S))

> Living in Arizona I have a problem with the engine running 
>hot in the summer. ...  Has any one sucessfully used an aux oil cooler 
>with 100+ temperatures on the 74-77 engine? 

John--

I have a similar set-up to what you've described: live in Tucson,
black-on-black '77 911S, A/C & EGR & smog pump removed, 11 bladed fan.  I
did a full engine rebuild 13,000 mi ago and haven't had any problems with
running hot since on either freeway or around town driving.  

Normal temp has been ~190; the only time I've seen the temp even get near
210 degrees was idling for 15 minutes in line for a smog check on a warm
day.  I credit this to the front fender oil cooler (Terbatrol, I think,
installed by previous owner, so I couldn't tell you how hot it ran before).
 Anyway, thanks to that cooler, it is possible to have the 2.7l cool in AZ.
 

You've probably already gotten rid of your engine-searing thermal reactors?

I like your idea of the more reasonably priced VW oil coolers!  (BTW, my VW
Squareback doesn't have cooling problems for the simple reason that I don't
have any gauges on it to tell me how hot it's running...)

Greg

Date: Mon, 26 Jul 93 18:55:35 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: OK, *LISTEN UP*!!!

I've noticed a trend that I want to stop *RIGHT NOW*. What I've seen people do
is reply to a note, then include the note to which they are replying in
its entirity tagged on just above or below their signature. This is adding
TREMENDOUSLY to the actual traffic volume on this list and *HAS TO STOP*.

When you reply to someone's note, you are tto do one of three things *ONLY*:

1) include their note with your comments interspersed, as most of you
   seem to be doing. Use something like ">" or ":" as the first character
   of the lines of their message to indicate that it's included or
   referrenced.

2) include some portion of their note -- edited for brevity down to the 
   key issue, followed by your comments

3) do not include any portion of the original note.

Under *NO* circumstances are you to include headers from the original
message other than "From:", "Subject:", or "Date:", and you are DISCOURAGED
from including them.

Sorry to be yelling about this, but in the last two weeks, we've gone up from
about 15 messages a day to nearly double that, and about half of those seem
to be twice as long as they need to be.

Stan

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 04:34 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 944 waterpump

>How do you know that a waterpump is getting bad. Is inspection and
>observation of leakage the only way ?.

Partially reiterating what others have said, look for:

 - leaking from the pump,
 - noises from it, and
 - overheating

>Do thermostats go bad like from 0 to 100% or is this a gradual process of
>opening later and incomplete ? 

I believe it's usually gradual, although a catastrophic failure may be
possible.  In either case, the symptoms may be more subtle, in that the
car's coolant (temp gauge in dash) will tend to run hotter, for example,
when stuck in traffic.

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 04:33 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: New battery needed

My original Porsche battery lasted three years, and the replacement
Porsche battery lasted another three years before sulfating a cell.
Feh!  And I took great care of it, believe it or not.

I recommend getting an Interstate battery.  They came out on top of
a Consumer's Report survey a few years ago.  And they also have a great
recycling program for the shops which job their product.

Btw, there _is_ an Interstate (or Sears, or whatever) battery which
correctly fits your Porsche.  I cannot remember the number of cars I've
tech'ed over the last few years which have had loose batteries, because
the owner's mechanic said that you cannot get the properly-fitting
battery.  Nonsense.

Kevin

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 04:34 GMT

>While cleaning the rust of my 85/2 944 Rear CV-joint rod I

and dandy.  It would be play when trying to turn the half-shaft by hand
which would indicate wear.

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 09:18:52 +0200
From: Kari Eloranta <eloranta@janus.hut.fi>
Subject: RE: misc. 914 and 944

> Another 944 question, does anybody know how to simply fix the
> window control switch?  I am referring to the rocker switch on
> the door.  The passenger side works from the driver side control
> panel, but not at the site.
> Pam

This switch is quite simple. Just pry it off with a thin steel  
plate/knife so that the edges do not crack, disconnect the three  
wires and pull it apart. There is a little spring which you don't  
want to jump on your face so be careful. Luckily it isn't that stiff.  
After removing that you see the contacts under the rocker. Since the  
switch is open these tend to attract dust which is the likely cause  
of your problem.

Kari

------------------------------

Date:         Tue, 27 Jul 93 12:45:41 MEZ
From: Wulf Moennich <FK90@DLRVMBS>
Subject:      Re: misc. 912 engine problems


 Hi Steve,

 The most common 912 engine failures, as far as I know, are broken
 crankshafts, cracked cylinder heads and broken rods. If you'd like
 to see some *IMPRESSIVE* photographs, buy Harry Pellow's books
 (at least "The ABC's (and 912's) of Porsche Engines" and "Secrets of
 the Inner Circle"), they are worth the money.

 Leakage at the main pulley is very common and due to a worn pulley
 seal. The seal can be changed without pulling the engine.

 Regarding your spark plug problem: the cylinder heads have helicoil
 inserts. Therefore, I think the threads cannot be stripped all that
 easily.

 Best regards,

 Wulf

 (Wulf Moennich; FK90@dlrvmbs; Internet: FK90@vm.bs.dlr.de)

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 16:13:19 BST
From: ben@wet.sbi.com (Ben Evans)
Subject: New 911 - triumph of development over design


More things coming out in the motoring press from Porsche
about the new 911. The beast is on show in September at
Frankfurt Motor Show, and lucky Germans can expect deliveries
to start in October. Shipping is due to start in the UK in
early 94 and some time after that the new 911 will be available
in the US.

Currently Porsche are manufacturing 60 911, 30 968 and *3*
928 per day. The firm is in a critical position with German 911
sales (the major market) down by 50% from a year ago. The company 
hopes that the new 911 will turn around the business in
domestic and foreign markets.

The new 911 looks even cleaner in body style than the existing
Carrera 2/4, the glass is all flush or semi flush, the front and
rear bumpers now look like proper body panels rather than retrofits. The
front headlights are steeply raked a la 968, and  make the front
look pretty radical in 911 terms. The standard body now is slightly
wider as the arches have to contain even wider wheels and track than 
the current C2/4. The only things that have not been reworked are 
the doors from what I can see in the pics.

Suspension is double alumininium wishbones at the rear, with modified
Macpherson (sp?) struts at the front. Brakes are Bosch ABS 5 and wheels
are 16 inch with a 17 inch option.

Engine HP is up to ~270 through a close ratio box
with six gears. Initially coupe only, shortly followed by turbo and
cab. versions. The Turbo has a wacking 400HP!

According to the article that I cribbed all this from (Autocar 28 
July 1993), there will be no more Targa versions, just when the 
1970s are back in fashion as well!

Date: Tue Jul 27 10:08:29 1993
From: xgg2356@dcmdc.dla.mil (James Fuerstenberg)
Subject: 912 failures


Re:

912 engine failures....since 912s have 356C motors, I am not
surprised to see broken cranks.

I know about half a dozen people racing 356s and I believe each of
them has broken at least one crank.  These cranks are magnafluxed
every year and do not exceed 7500 rpm.  One of them broke right
between the words mag and OK from the last magnaflux.  These are
full race motors putting out around 150HP on the dyno (1600cc, not
big bore).

Jim

jfuerstenberg@dcmdc.dla.mil or jfuerstenberg@zz-link.dcmdc.dla.mil

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 10:34:45 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Play in 944 rear CV Joint

>  While cleaning the rust of my 85/2 944 Rear CV-joint rod I
> noticed that the cv-joint has a play of about 1 to 1-1/2 inches
> when jiggled between the differential and the rotor.

This is normal - the half shaft moves because the CV joint inner piece 
moves.  It's just the regular action of the ball bearings.

		JBS

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 10:46:23 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 

No, you can't just get *any* battery.  You need one which clamps in on 
the ends, i.e., it must have "feet" on the narrow ends of the battery.  
Many batteries (for American cars) have smooth feet, and are mounted 
via a clamp that hooks over the battery top.

I've had good luck at Sears for my 951, which has a slightly different 
style mount but is still "special".  A friend with a 930 also got one there.

Porsche factory batteries are more expensive and have less capacity 
and/or cold cranking Amps than many aftermarket batteries.  I wouldn't 
recommend one unless you're a serious concours person.

Not all batteries are alike!

		JBS

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 08:53:08 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: RE: RE:Wooden Indians


Frank,

Yes, it does include very early 911s. Volkswagen dealers (they usually
dealt in Porsches in those days) tended to fancy up the foot traffic
attraction that the 911 was for them. The wood dash was usually done
to remove the stigma of "plain Porsche plastic", a term the late, great
Henry Manney III wrote when he described the unveiling of the 901 (as
it was originally called) at the Frankfort Auto Show.

Henry Manney was a roving columnist for Road and Track for many years, 
his prose was quite entertaining.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com

Date: Tue, 27 Jul 93 07:22:24 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Camber on a 1971 911 (continued)

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
SUBJECT: Camber on a 1971 911 (continued)

>   rear is screwed up.  The passenger side wheel is much more
>   negative (probably at least 3 degrees negative) while the drivers
>   side is probably 1 degree negative or maybe closer to Zero
>   degress.  I recall when I had the suspension set last year that

Ditto other's comments, if you've adjusted for max camber and you're only
getting ~1 degree, your trailing arm is bent (IMO).  Get it on an alignment
rack at a reputable performance shop (best if familiar with 911's).  I had to
replace one of mine when I first started setting it up.

>   I understand that lowering the car decambers the wheels- and the
>   before and after passenger side camber settings seems to support

Opposite is true.  Lowering will give you MORE camber.  Raising will
cause decamber.  Your passenger side sounds pretty good (you may be too low if
you're getting ~3 degrees). Remember you have to have some suspension travel
or you'll have no control in bumps (to say nothing of streetability)..

>   Also, does anyone know how to check camber?  I think yoy put a

I use a camber gauge on a perfectly level garage floor.  The gauge is basically
a bubble type level indicator, so the surface the car is on has to be near
perfect.  I level check the surface 3 ways (forward/back, side/side and cross)
this becomes more critical for weight balance.  Also agree with earlier poster
on rolling the car after changing to settle suspension.  If you drive over
bumps just before parking on your level surface, bounce each corner by hand
3-4 times.

>   what should I be
>   getting for camber readings?  What is optimal for track, street,
>   etc.

Send me a note with your direct mail address (internet).  Sorry others, not
willing to share this with all......  I'm at: sjje@chevron.com


 -Scott

Subject: dream job

People,

This is not related to porsches but I figure that you will be
interested.  My roommate's sister in Spain wanted to buy
a 300ZX Twin Turbo and take it back.  However, new cars are
very heavily taxed over there and she didn't want to buy
a used car.  Solution:  Buy the car, and let my roommate drive
it for the required time for it to qualify as being used.
She ( roommate ) gets insurance paid, gas paid, and on top of
that a very cool car to drive.  ( Even though it's not a 
Porsche ).  Howdya like to have a job like that?


Alfonso

Porschephiles #464                         Wed Jul 28 23:06:23 CDT 1993
Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 03:51 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 911 Ball joints

>Does anyone know where to purchase the tool for removing ball joints from a
>'72 911 (by this weekend)?

>Or

>Does anyone know how to remove the ball joint without the special tool?

A number of the aftermarket places, like Performance Products, will sell
you this tool for big bucks.  I bought one a bunch of years ago, silly me,
when they went for only sorta-big-bucks.  I recently donated it to my
mechanic.

Rather than shelling out, I would recommend either using a hammer,
carefully but authoritatively.  Or buy or rent one of the generic ball
joint "pickle forks."

Kevin

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 03:51 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>

labels from PCNA.  Sounds like you need a vert label from your description.

You can get this tech bulletin from your dealer, or I can send you a
photocopy.  PCNA will want money and proof.

| ...They felt the 911 was too hard to drive fast.
The problem here is that most of these mags don't have adequately
qualified drivers to test these cars.  It's only when they hire someone
like Danny Sullivan or that caliber to do a comparison test that they get
worthwhile results.  Witness the test last year when Danny drove the 911
Turbo, Vette, 300ZX, NSX, Miata, MR2, et. al. - guess which one was
fastest on the road course and judged the best "race" car by Danny!  When
driven properly, I'm positive the 911 Turbo would leave the Supra Turbo in
the dust...  Same applies to the test of the modified riceburners... gotta
have real drivers, not writers, testing those cars or the results could be
anything.

T.H.

can extract the best from a temperamental (flawed?) car).

------------------------------

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 11:29:53 BST
From: ben@wet.sbi.com (Ben Evans)
Subject: Re: 911 vs world comparisons


>From: kevinh@hasler.ascom.ch (kevinh)
>911's handling have been criticised from the beginning and even though this 
>has been mostly corrected (recently) they are still a harder car to drive 
>fast than some of the recent japanese (and other) competition.

I am not sure that there is a competition, it's a no contest I suspect.
I can never understand where this `911 handling is bad' idea comes from.
I think that the record of the 911, which I suggest has more significant
motorsport victories than all other marques put together, indicates 
otherwise.

I would support the notion that the 911 design leaves a low margin
for driver error though. The margin is pushed right out to the extreme
edge of the performance envelope, which may be what gives the 911 the edge.
You really can take the 911 to the brink, at which point you can turn back
or go over if you have not spotted where you are. The 911 allows you
to put more in and get more out.

And then the asthetics: the howl of the redlining flat six, the roaring
of the air cooling, the squat of the rear as you nail the throttle and 
then the eyeball sucking deceleration as you stamp on the brakes scrubbing 
100MPH is scant seconds, the stench of the red hot brakes, the gforce and 
the sense of a massive pendulum effect swinging you through the bend,
and then nail the gas again and pure traction claws you through. Ah, the 
joy of the 911! Of course get this wrong and enjoy countryside scenic
views while travelling backwards very fast though fields and hedges.

Date: 28 Jul 93 04:40:57-0800
From: /PN=STEVE.STOMSKI/O=GSA2/PRMD=GOV+GSA2/ADMD=TELEMAIL/C=US/@sprint.com
Subject: 911 Camber

    Kevin,

    In response to your question, no I did not replace my spring
    plates with Sway-away (I assume those are the "adjustable
    aftermarket spring plates?).  Do you think that my spring
    plates might be the problem?  I have thought about swapping spring
    plates from side to side to see if this would change my camber
    readings.

    Yesterday (and the day before) I changed out my driver's side
    trailing arm for my original one.  I now have a little more
    negative camber (at least the tire is not rubbing on the fender
    lip), but not as mucj as I have on the passenger's side (yes, I
    did try adjusting the eccentric).  I realized that I lowered the
    car a little too much (by about an inch), so I will be raising it
    again and I assume that it will take away some of my new-found
    negative camber.

    BTW, changing out a trailing arm on a 1971 is a *@#%^$ of a job!
    Apparently on the earlier and later cars it is simpler but on
    some cars the factory manual says you have to drop the
    engine/trans to back out the bolt (there is not enough clearance
    between the bolt head and the case of the transmission to remove
    the bolt completely from the end of the arm).  I "simply"
    disconnected the mounting bolts and the trans and engine and
    wiggled the whole unit around until I had enough (barely, I might
    add) room to get the bolt out.

    As always, any comments/suggestions are welcomed.



    Steven Stomski

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 09:30:35 -0400
From: alfred@nyquist.bellcore.com (Alfred Kwan 21342)
Subject: Re:  911 Ball joints

Len:

J.C. Whitney sells a "deluxe heavy-duty lever type ball
joint & tie rod end remover" for $15 and a "screw type ball joint 
remover" for $10. I have both and perfer the lever-type.
Unlike the screw-type, it doesn't "walk" on top of a
ball joint bolt when you use it.  For tight working area,
the screw-type is a bit smaller than the lever-type.
For $15, I would highly recommend this tool.  I also have
the standard ball joint fork and always manage to break
all the rubber grease boots with it.


Alfred

20+ years of "development"!


> I can never understand where this `911 handling is bad' idea comes from.

I didn't say it was "bad" as such, I said it has always been criticised. The 
911's handling is good in that traction is good and high g cornering has
always been the norm but is bad in that for quite a number of years they
were considered overly light at the front end (then again the steering
and its balance has always been praised so you can't have it both ways)
and the rear suspension was always a weak point in that it responded violently
to, what must be said, are natural reactions in certain situations, eg in an
understeer situation sharp throttle unloading would cause the back to jump 
out which on wet roads caused many accidents. Now you might say that you 
shouldn't do this anyway - but why should you put up with it when cars are 
available which don't exhibit such "features"


> I think that the record of the 911, which I suggest has more significant
> motorsport victories than all other marques put together, indicates 
> otherwise.

Like i've already said in a previous article - consumer cars should be judged
with "consumer type people" and not experts. I would also contend that 
the 911's many many victories were in spite of the handling and not because
of it. The victories seem to be in two areas - 1) rallying (in the early years 65-69)
 - which were due to the durability of the car and a power to weight ratio (don't
forget their main competition at the time was 1300cc Mini's! and 2) the
track years(74-80) which, like a lot of Porsches victories, seem to be based upon
a superiority in turbocharger technology - not to take credit away from Porsche
for the 911 but I think it's more a case of development and not original design.

> I would support the notion that the 911 design leaves a low margin
> for driver error though.

Agreed.

> And then the asthetics: the howl of the redlining flat six, the roaring
> of the air cooling, the squat of the rear as you nail the throttle

Yes, that's why i've still got mine - damn unpractical (anybody know
a kiddie seat (9m-4yrs) for the rear of a 911 that's obtainable in Europe!) and
to drive the thing more than just occasionally drives me nuts (bloody
uncomfortable) but for that short period a week it's worth it (I think!)


> then the eyeball sucking deceleration as you stamp on the brakes scrubbing 
> 100MPH is scant seconds, the stench of the red hot brakes,

Well, mine worry me sometimes - I find the fronts lock a little too easily, mind
you they are twenty years old and they must have been wonderful in 1973!

kevinh@hasler.ascom.ch

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 09:29:14 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: to decamber or not to decamber...

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
SUBJECT: to decamber or not to decamber...


>   I am not sure if I understand your comments on lowering giving
>   more camber.  From what I have read I thought that the lower the
>   car the more negative camber, am I misinformed?  Please advise.


Public apology due:  I had my cambers and decambers and my negatives and
positives intermixed in my comments on Steven's 911 questions.  The clan I
hang with don't use proper terminology much -- since positive camber is
verboten, we talk in more absolute terms; camber becomes negative more
camber is more negative, etc.  Thus my decamber statement meant "to have
less negative camber".  You're all suitably confused by now...

Bottom line: As you lower a 911, you get more negative on the camber.

Sorry for any other confusion....

 -Scott

Carl DeSousa asked about road tests,

Well, the modified cars were probably done by some fellows who have 
their own ideas of how to abuse a already compromised design. Face
the facts, most people that own Porsches put on comprimises in tires
and wheels to suit their taste and wallet. Not a bad thing but:

Many pieces for suspensions that are marketed don't work. Most just 
waste your time and money, some really do work, but they require a
substancial investment  and don't add to ride or comfort and are a 
bitch to  drive on the street. 
 A car that can do 1G on the skidpad may not have good off-throttle 
transient response (spell that squirrelly) which will make you go slower
in the slalom. The key to the whole thing is to learn how good you are
behind the wheel and set your car up accordingly, fact is, most of the
cars are far better than the drivers so they should stay in Hondas and 
the right lane.

As I have just written, adding cubic money to your Porsche doesn't make
it go faster. Some of the worst-handling pigs I have ever driven have
been owner-modified Porsches( and Corvettes). One comment I will make 
is that most of those people were well-meaning and got pooned by a less than honest shop making big-bucks on the margins for some of that junk.
It was frustrating telling an owner that one of the reasons he couldn't
get the car to handle was that stuff.

I read that road test, by the way. The 911 is hard to drive because the weight bias and the training most motor vehicle operators have makes
the tactil feel of the 911 appear foreign to their senses. A Driver
(there is a difference between the term `driver' and `operator') will 
have the background to adjust to the environment. Operators tend to
back off and complain if anything is different than their limited range
of feel and responses.

99% of all road test people are operators. Sorry Csare Csabe, you are
an operator. Bedard is only one rung above you. The magazines take 
advertising so they are biased when a manufacturere leans on them 
dangling a big ad contract. The other outfit that takes pride in not 
having advertising doesn't know how to test cars and willingly parrots
information about reliability without questioning the source's sample
database. 

So who to believe? The Fantacides or the ccommunists? Try something 
yourself, you have to live with it. 

So much for my rumblings this morning.

Jim Pasha

Date:         Wed, 28 Jul 1993 12:05:39 CST6CDT
From: "Thorwald -T.H.- Herbert"  <TH@csc-mstg.tamu.edu>
Subject:      Re: 911 vs world (lengthy)

> Why should they have to hire somebody "special" to drive the cars? Surely
> the best test is a driver with good ability - and not necessarily the best
> (who we know can extract the best from a temperamental (flawed?) car). 
 
I believe the problem is the conceptual idea behind what a car magazine 
should be.  Aren't they supposed to bring us news, tests, information, etc. 
about cars?  ALL cars?  Now leaf through your last ten issues of R&T or C&D 
(et. al.) 80% testing on sports and performance cars and 20% everyday driver 
type cars.  When's the last time they tested a Sentra or a Ford Escort?  
Because the reader's want it, the mags will test the Porsches, Vettes, 
Ferraris, Jap Turbo cars and even Lamborghinis in every single issue.  So, 
we see four "tests" a year of the 911 Turbo each of which says something 
about how hard it is to drive the car fast.
Now look at the typical 911 Turbo buyers:
     a.  people with too much money who want to LOOK good
     b.  people with too much money who love to DRIVE fast
The folks in group a. could care less how the car handles or if the Supra is 
faster or easier to drive.  The others enjoy the challenge of driving one of 
the worlds fastest production cars and could care less if they COULD 
theoretically stomp on the brakes in the middle of a hard turn without losing 
control.  I'm sure there'll be a few stragglers, but what it really boils down to 
is that the writer's in the mags that "test" the cars don't drive the cars for 
the people who ultimately buy them.  They emphasize performance in their 
tests - because that's what the reader's want to see - but they're really not 
qualified enough to JUDGE performance (there are exceptions of course).  So 
why don't the mags hire people who are qualified to JUDGE performance?
A good example was a test done a few years ago where a whole bunch of 
big name magazine writer's drove a Vette on a skidpad.  The resulting 
numbers ranged from somewhere around .88 to .92 I believe.  Then 
someone like A.J. Foyt or Sullivan or the like got in the car - he did .97 or .98 
under identical conditions.  Point is - the numbers you see don't mean much 
and the information that's printed ultimately boils down to the opinion of the 
writer and their (limited?) ability to extract the highest performance out of 
the car.

'nuff said - IMHO of course...

T.H.

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 11:46:20 PDT
From: Patrick Norris <norrisp@Jupiter.SAIC.Com>
Subject: Re:  911 Ball joints


> Does anyone know how to remove the ball joint without the special tool?


This should be close to that of the 914 setup.  Something that I've done
when the ball joint won't come out of the strut is support the front on
jackstands (shake the car to make sure it's secure) and put a floor jack
under the brake rotor and push the strut up off the ball joint.

As for the threaded ring that holds the ball joint into the a-arm, first
pull the pin and the keeper plate (round with fingers all around the edge)
and then i use a spanner wrench to unthread the ring.  Remember to be
sure the notch for in the ball joint is possitioned correctly in the 
a-arm so it locks in properly.  

Assembly is reverse of disassembly (sort of).

Pat

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 16:02:23 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: RE: to camber, etc.


"Alas, poor Yorrick, he was cambered well!"

Camber and de-camber. Adding camber is cambering, removing is de-
cambering.

zero camber is dead-nuts straight up in the alignment scenario, adding
to either plane is adding camber, removal from either plane is 
decambering. 

The mis-use of this term comes from the universal ignornce that grew up
around the beetle and the desire to remove the dreaded oversteer from it
and the 356 in the *old* days. Most of the ignorant poops didn't realize
that that made matters worse and the proper way to get things under 
control was to shorten the hoop and relocate the trans mount up 1 1/2"
and add 1 1/4 degree negative camber to the rear. This little piece of 
work relocated the mass and lowered the car without destroying the ride and making the rear tires look like the axles were bent. This was the 
real trick among men to make the 356 work.  In the front one needed to 
machine the front links to reduce the positive camber to 1/2 degree 
negative and negative camber gain. They were sporting but very evil 
handling any other way, the competition, fortunately, was much worse.

Old Harry Weber used to do this in the fifties and Timenaus never caught
the ruse. I did it to my street 356B coupe and would never go back to a 
stock setup. This isn't a modification for an amateur, however.

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Wed, 28 Jul 93 17:53:42 -0700
From: stevew@tamri.com (Steve Watson)

Subject:  more on DELLORTO carbs (really carbs in general)

Gutentag all!

A couple of people recommended that I purchase "The Dellorto Superformance
Tech Book" as an aid in setting up my carbs installed on a 912 w/NPR kit.

Well I bought it and it is very good.  I highly recommend it for anyone with
Dellorto's or Weber's or any carbs for that matter.  Anyway, I currently have
a set of 36mm's installed and am looking for more top end acceleration.

After reading this guide, I believe I know why my 1600lb Speedster replica
is so slow.  First, 40mm's may have been a better choice.  But the 36's were
on the engine when I purchased the car and they
are like new, so too bad.  The existing carbs are setup from CB Performance
as follows:
                       Venturi-30
          	       Main/Air- 180
                       Main/Gas- 122
                       Idle Gas-  55
                       Acc/Pump-  50

There is a graph on p. 56 of this guide that compares Main Venturi Diameter
with Single Cylinder Capacity.  The results will vary according to the 
rpm redline of your engine.  Using the 6,000 rpm max line and .430 for the
SCC, I should be using 34mm Venturis.  The guide states to "Put in larger
venturies to go fast, stay with smaller or mid-size venturies for improved 
everyday driving.

Then there is the 4.0 Rule.  To select the proper size Main/Gas jet, multiply
the 34mm venturi by 4. (34mm Venturis x 4 = 136 Main/Gas) So go a bit to the
richer side and you end up with 140 Main/Gas.

From what I've read the Idle, Main/Air and Acc/Pump should be okay at their 
present sizes.

I'll let everyone know how great an effect this will have on the top end
acceleration.  Let me know what you think.  Is this a waste of time?

Auf Wiedersehen
Steve

P.S.- Learned all this German stuff while working at Siemens.

Date: 28 Jul 93 22:05:41 EDT
From: Shel Hall <76701.103@CompuServe.COM>
Subject: '83 SC sunroof or not

Folks-

Sean Namazi <sharam@ole.cdac.com> asks ...

>>> Did porsche make any non-sunroof coupes in 83? <<<

They made at least one; one of my co-workers had one.

He bought it _cheap_, too, because of that.

It seems that someone had ordered an SC, with a sunroof, navy leather,
etc., and wanted it painted Mercedes white to match his wife's car.

So the dealer puts in a special order, Porsche whangs out a shell, gets
some paint from the guys across town (or maybe trucked the shell over
there, I dunno), builds the car, sends it to the dealer, and the
salesman calls to owner to pick it up.

Owner shows up with his wife in the white Benz, takes a look at the SC,
and says "Where's the sunroof?"

Salesman blanches whiter than the Merc, checks the order, checks the
build sheet, and sits down.  The order says "sunroof" but the build
sheet doesn't.

Owner says "Call me when _my_ car comes in," and drives off.

The car did demo duty for a couple of months, then my friend bought it.

 -Shel

fact a VW part.  No idea on price.

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 06:38:08 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: Re: carbs in general....

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
SUBJECT: Re: carbs in general....


Steve Watson wrote:

>Dellorto's or Weber's or any carbs for that matter.  Anyway, I currently have
>a set of 36mm's installed and am looking for more top end acceleration.

>with Single Cylinder Capacity.  The results will vary according to the
>rpm redline of your engine.  Using the 6,000 rpm max line and .430 for the
>SCC, I should be using 34mm Venturis.  The guide states to "Put in larger
>venturies to go fast, stay with smaller or mid-size venturies for improved
>everyday driving.

Experts I've conferred with tell me that it's a common mistake to use engine
redline for sizing venturis/jets/etc.  You should use max HP rpm instead.
You'll perhaps lose some top end HP, but the torque/HP curve will be flatter
and stronger overall, resulting in a faster car...

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 11:41:26 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 944 longevity


       A friend of mine is looking at a 1983 944.  The car has 107,000
       miles on it and is very clean.  From what I understand the car has
       had the expected mechanical problems (head gasket, motor mounts,
       water pump, etc.) but no real major work (i.e. "rebuild").  Does
       anyone know what the life expectancy is for the earlier 944s?  IF
       my friend buys the car can he expect to run it for another 30,000
       miles or is it going to need a rebuild soon?  The asking price is
       $6,800.

Tell your friend that the life expectancy of the early 944 is 143,480
miles before it disintegrates. So if he gets it *right now* then
it can be driven for 30,000 miles and sold with absolutely no
problems, with several thousand miles to spare. 

Seriously:
I think what you're really asking is "Assuming proper maintenance and
moderate duty useage, what is the average interval for bottom end
overhaul for the early 8V 944 motor?" I would put that in the ballpark
of 150,000 to 170,000 miles based on the few overhauls I have
witnessed. This takes into account cylinder liner wear.

- --

John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 12:25:28 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 944 Questions + A Sale Item (Act Now!)

> 1)When driving 60-80 mph, there are 1 or 2 little zones (63-65 mph is one)
> where the steering wheel gets a jiggle.  A few friends indicate this can be
> corrected by balancing and/or rotating the tires.  True/part true/ false?

What year is the car?  Very early ones (82/83) had this problem, and it 
was very hard to get rid of.  Your friends are basically correct, but 
you also need to check your alignment (esp. toe-in/out) and if your 
wheels are true (not bent).

> 2) When driving at low speeds, occasionally (esp. on hot days)Jthe
> speedometer needle will wobble. This problem has not been seen over 50
> mph.  Would the logical starting place to test be the speedometer unit in the
> dash, or the connections on the other end?

A little wobbling is normal, due to the speedo mechanism's design.  
It's worst at low speeds.  Can't say for sure without seeing yours.  
85/2 cars have electronic speedo's so this characteristic is gone.  
Replacing the [old] cable *might* help.


		JBS

------------------------------

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 12:30:00 TZ
From: jimseld@microsoft.att.com
Subject: RE: 944 clutch noise

> What does a worn 944 clutch sound like? I have an '88 944 with about
> 51K miles, and now when I step on the clutch pedal, I hear this
> scrunchy whirring noise. The whirring continues as long as I have the
> clutch depressed. Could it be the hydraulics part of it?

Sounds like the throwout bearing.  If the noise persists in neutral 
with clutch depressed, it's highly likely that the TO bearing is the 
culprit.  Pretty cheap part, but ~$1500US of labor to replace it!  
(it's a good idea to replace lots of stuff if the clutch area is opened!)

Hydraulics wouldn't do this - they just move the two discs apart, so as 
long as you're disengaging completely there would be no noise.

		JBS

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 14:22:11 EDT
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject:  944T shocks


   I am interested in the 944 lowering kit myself and wanted some
   comments on the merits and demerits of lowering the car. Also,
   is this task for the shade-tree mechanic with professional alignment
   or should the lowering task be left for the Guru shop.

I am a big fan of lowering, it is one factor that *directly* improves
the cornering of your car by lowering the C.G. and thus reducing
lateral weight transfer, no question about that. Reducing lateral
weight transfer makes each tire carry a more even distribution of
load. Lowering also [usually] accompaniess an increse in stiffness at each
axle which may reduce body roll and provide better suspension
geometry. I also like the way it looks on most cars. The bad points
usually involve scraping exhaust and spoilers when entering the
driveway or (much worse) bottoming out the suspension against the
stops which can be bad (causing essentially infinite roll stiffness)
if you haven't designed for it.

On the 944 you have to replace the springs in the front with the lower
variety, and in the rear, you have to replace/reset the torsion bars
just like on a 911. If you can change the front shocks you can lower
the front. The rear is a different story, quite a bit more involved, 
but possible to do under the proverbial shade tree given enough will 
and sweat.

An alignment is absolutely necessary afterwards. Corner balance optional.

PS: I am looking for a good used early ('84) 944 air conditioning
compressor. If you have one that you want to sell please email to me
ASAP (like today or tommorrow latest). Thankyou.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 14:07:24 PDT
From: desousa@cisco.com
Subject: Re: 944T shocks 

John,
> From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
> I am a big fan of lowering, it is one factor that *directly* improves
> the cornering of your car by lowering the C.G. and thus reducing
> lateral weight transfer, no question about that. Reducing lateral
> weight transfer makes each tire carry a more even distribution of
> load. Lowering also [usually] accompaniess an increse in stiffness at each
> axle which may reduce body roll and provide better suspension
> geometry. 

I have to raise a flag here.  A lowered car induces bump steer and other 
suspension geometry problems.  Even after an alignment, tha angle of the
lower control arm is permanantly in the wrong position.  And when it travels,
up under compression, the angle gets worse.  This is explained in the book
"How to make your car handle".  This is part of the reason why a lot of
showroom stock cars put in faster times than lowered cars.  I think one
will feel perceived better handling because of less body roll and better
response.  I like lowered cars too.  My 911 is lowered, but even with
the steering rack raised to compensate, steering gets twitchy on bumpy roads.

btw, I'm amazed how far 944's can be lowered.  

Carl

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 17:48:14 EDT
From: tommywmson@aol.com
Subject: Re: Dellorto carbs

Steve,

A few comments on your planned experiment with the 34mm venturis.
I currently use Weber 40 IDF's on my 914 2.0.  I use the car as a daily 
driver as well and for the occasional autocross.  The carbs came with 
28mm venturis and was fairly quick up to about 4500 rpm.  I switched to 
32mm venturis which moved the power peak to about 5500 but gives me 
less power below 3000 rpm.  The point I am getting to is this.

You mentioned that with your current setup, your car was "slow".
If you meant that top speed was "slow" then bigger venturis may help.
However, your low end driveability will most likely suffer and make your car
less responsive in day to day driving.  It may also be slower at some
time-trial events where low end power is more critical than top end.

If you mean that your car does not accelerate quickly or seems low on
power even at low rpm, something else may be the problem.
(Carbs not synchronized, improper timing, worn cam etc...)

At some point, the ratio of the size of the venturi to the size of the
carb is important. (34mm in a 36mm may be pushing it)

Also, your cam plays a big part in determineing where your "peak" output rpm
is.
Doesn't it? 

Hope this helps.

Tommy Williamson
Jackson, Mississippi



------------------------------

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 14:57:09 PDT
From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: Re: Fuel and ignition questions on 76 911s

Scott et all,
	Scott posed the question of modifiing a CIS injection system with 
	an alternative air flow sensor, particularly a "hot wire sensor".
	
	Well, Scott, the beauty of the CIS injection system is it's simplicity!
	ie, no electronics other than some form of temp sensing for fuel
	enrichment upon cold start, etc. The system is for all intents and
	purposes is a purely mechanical system, and it works very well indeed.
	My experience with it is limited to '84 Quattro, and VW Scirocco,
	but the systems are all very similar.

	Coupling an inherently mechanical system to an electro-mechanical
	control system is indeed a heavy task! You would need some form of
	controller/computer to translate the output from the hot wire sensor,
	(a voltage or current), and then generate an output voltage,current
	and/or series of pulses to some form of actuator or motor to move the
	fuel control the proper distance to provide the correct amount of 
	fuel for a given load condition.... It gets pretty messy, and that is
	probably why Bosch didn't design their next fuel control system that
	way. 
	The next generation, correct me if I'm wrong, was the Jetronic types
	which used electronically pulsed injectors, a fully "computer-
	controlled" injection system. The latest version of this injection
	system on the 968 uses a hot wire sensor!

	So if what you really want is a hot wire sensor system, you' be better 
	off starting out with a later model injection system, retrofitted
	to your '76 911, not an impossible task for a shade tree mechanic.
	Then fit an after market hot wire kit available through an un-named
	performance supplier as advertised in your Panorama, and you're
	there!
	Or if your realy want the experience of developing your own system,
	then purchase an air flow sensor for a 968, and go to it!
	But I'd start with a late model 911 Carrera Fuel injection system.

	Further curiosity. What would it take to put a 968 air sensor on an
	'87 911 Carrera? Anybody got any Ideas re. the compatibility of the 
	Hot Wire Sensor output to the Motronic style Air Sensor?

	The Motronic Air Sensor, according to the Manual, has a voltage output
	of about .05 Volts at Zero Flow to about 5.6 Volts at full flapper
	deflection. (From memory). What output can we expect from a
	hot wire sensor? Anybody tried this?
	RUF provides a hot wire sensor on their version of the Carrera II
	Turbo!

John Tilden
Data I/O Corp.
tilden@data-io.com

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 15:05 CDT
From: emuroga@ihlpe.att.com
Subject: Re: Leaks in tail light.


I've found a possible cause for water in my 944 taillights:


Water runs down the hole where the latches for the rear hatch are.
There's this little gutter tray under the latch with a rubber hose 
running down to the bottom of the car. I theorize that water either 
overflows the tray or somehow drips around it. But anyways right below 
this area is the taillight assembly. I've only noticed water down 
there after driving in a very heavy rain or spraying water at the 
back of the car (seems the water gets past the hatch seal).



Eisuke Muroga
muroga@att.com

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 17:02:55 CDT
From: cork@cs.rice.edu (Corky Cartwright)
Subject: Lowering a 951

You can lower the front of a 951S about 1/2" without affecting
suspension geometry by installing 225/45-16 tires instead of 
225/50-16's.  I made this change to my 951S and it subjectively improved
the steering response and cornering behavior of the car.
I suspect most of the change is due to stiffer sidewalls and better
tires (Dunlop SP8000's instead of D40/M2's), but the lower ride height
may help slightly.  Before the change, I had trouble with my car
understeering at the track; it has the small (16mm) rear stabilizer bar.

If you want to get really radical you can try 225/40-16's.

- -- Corky Cartwright

Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 14:04:51 EDT
From: F.W.Kerfoot__Iii@att.com
Subject: Re: 944 clutch noise

>What does a worn 944 clutch sound like? I have an '88 944 with about
>51K miles, and now when I step on the clutch pedal, I hear this
>scrunchy whirring noise. The whirring continues as long as I have the
>clutch depressed. Could it be the hydraulics part of it?

Unfortunately (in terms of cost), your symptoms sound exactly like
those of a failed/failing throw-out bearing. Since the throw-out bearing
is concentric with the driveshaft, the repair process looks essentially
like that for the clutch itself. I can't see any way it could be the
hydraulics (or anything else you could fix from the outside).

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Porschephiles #466                         Fri Jul 30 23:02:12 CDT 1993
Date: Thu, 29 Jul 93 22:36:22 -0500
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Fuel and ignition questions on 76 911s

>    Driving along at steady state speeds
>    the car will, very seldomly, have the ignition cut out for only the 
>    briefest moment and then return.  

Interesting. I have a friend who has the same problem. Initially, I thought
it was the fact that he had a loose ground wire to his coil. But it wasn't
that easy... Theory now is a faulty Permatune unit, but we haven't 
gotten to test that yet.

>Now just a flight of technical fancy - 
>
>The major problem with the CIS fuel injection is the air flow sensor.  In that
>it is very subjectable to intake pulses that would cause the air flow sensor to
>bounce and disturb the fuel flow.  So what do you think the possibility of
>melding something like a hotwire sensor in its place and a control to accuate 
>the metering body.  

Well, now that you mention it.... I've had one of these in development 
since March or so. The theory is that since pulsed injection approximates
CIS at the limit of the cycle time, you win big time if you can recover
the relaxation time of the injector and still use a more sane air flow
metering scheme than stock CIS. Hence, flapper box or hot wire air flow
metering, fed into an electronically controlled fuel distributor.

Some comments: no, it's *NOT* easy. But, it's not been too expensive unless
you count my time at the rate my clients get billed for it.... If I'm
able to get it working, it should be a good alternative for *some* applications
but it may be too expensive (relative to Haltech, Electromotive, or 
CompTech -- about $6k for a 911, installed and running, for any of those
BTW...). Note that the Autothority hot wire kit for '84-90 Carreras 
is almost $2k and doesn't include any plumbing....

More as I know more. Rest assured, you'll hear about it...

Stan

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 1993 08:53:17 -0400
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: CIS flight of fancy

RE:  Replacing the CIS air flow sensor (flapper plate)

	Not as fanciful a flight as you might think.  Most of the work has already 
been done by Autothority.  Their air mass flow sensor works by sensing the pressure
of the air passing through the chamber, and provides air mass data to the DME.

Your Task would be:

1.  Adapt pressure sensor to CIS airbox, calibrate for diameter of CIS air flow
sensor housing.

2.  Convert pressure sensor output signal to input signal for stepper motor
	(ie. build signal processor)

3.  Provide power supply for pressure sensor, signal processor, stepper motor

4.  link stepper motor to plunger in CIS fuel head.

	Obviously, the trick here is step 2.  The other 3 steps will take about
10% of the time invested in such a project.  The nice thing is that once done,
the whole assembly should fit in the CIS airbox, and could be a bolt-on upgrade.
If sold for under $1000, there would be a market.  Accompanied with the
appropriate mods to the fuel head venturis, this should be good for about +10% HP.

Bob
rmohan@afit.af.mil

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 1993 09:25:55 -0400
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: RE: 76 911 fuel & ignition Q's

A couple of warnings/notices about mail currently circulating:

1.  A very common but very wrong fix for CIS idling problems is
disconnecting and plugging the decel valves.  Typically, the
decel valve does not fail, it just loses its calibration.  To
test it, remove it from the car.  Try to blow/breathe through
either of the large outlet tubes.  If you can, it's busted.
Now hook up your trusty brake bleeder, or other vacuum source,
to the small outlet tube at the top, and apply some vacuum.
Now you should breathe through the large tubes if its working
properly.  If it's working, it just needs adjustment.  This is
done by (no kidding) crushing it lengthwise.  Call your local
dealership, and ask him to dust off his tech bulletin archives.
I think it's about 1.5 mm.  Place a 19mm socket over the small
outlet tube to protect it.  Stick the whole thing in a vise 
lengthwise, and squeeze.  Measure the length before and after.

2.  FYI, Authority air mass flow sensors use a pressure sensor,
not a hot-wire sensor.

Date: Fri 30 Jul 93 09:47:04-EDT
From: DEHAASM@NAPC-1.NAVY.MIL
Subject: RE: Fuel and ignition questions


The subject of fuel injection systems has come up and I can attest from
personal experience that the Bosch CIS system is very reliable.
On my TR-6 (Sorry not a Porsche) race/road car I became fed up with
the dual carb set up and didn't want to go the Weber route.  So I designed
a fuel injection system for it.  Needless to say the car has turned into
a rolling laboratory.  If you plan on getting the car through emissions
you better know what you are getting yourself into.  If you are using
stock throttle bodies and injector locations you will probably be OK.
However, I decided to build my own throttle bodies and mess around
with injector locations.  The key word in any fuel delivery system is
MIXTURE.  If you are not getting the fuel to mix with the air you will
have rough idle problems poor performance and super high HC readings at
the tail pipe.
     If you are just modifying the control part of the FI system then
you are a little more out of the woods.  I decided to try and experiment
with a throttle position approach and discard a air mass/flow sensor.
This was to experiment on a new way to reduce the cost of an FI system
on non-catalytic cars.  The FI computer software uses "fuzzy logic"
(don't laugh its a serious control theory) based on throttle position,
ambient air temps, pressures, and engine RPM to set steady state fuel 
flow.  The neat thing is that it works!  I've logged about 3k mi this
summer on this system without even fouling a plug or burning a valve.
I was not able to put the engine on a dyno but I rented an engine
emission analyzer to fine tune the control logic.  It's a lot easier to
change fuel delivery schedules now with a lap top than it is to swap
needles or jets.  Any comments or questions....drop me a line.

Mark deHaas
dehaasm@napc-1.navy.mil

------------------------------

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 1993 09:47:00 -0400 (EDT)
From: RICHARD3300@delphi.com
Subject: Carbs, Venturis and Etc.

Steve:

Double check the graph in the book I've found it's very small and difficult to
work with unless you use a ruler to accurately lay in your variables. (I
found out the hard way!) As regards your desire for larger venturis the ans-
wer is totally dependent upon what you want the car to drive like.

Yes, the larger venturis will flow more air (constrained of course by the 
valves and porting on your heads) but at low RPM the vacuum signal is too
small to pull fuel into the carbs so you'll have to ramp up the idle RPM and,
at off idle the fuel will fallout of the fue/air stream leading to poor mid
range perf. At full throttle it will all smooth out.

Not having your engine in front of me I can speculate only about the following:

	Carb Synchronization
Part of your problems can b e the carbs are out of sync. If you balanced air
flow through them with the Unisyn as described in the book they are synced to 
pull equal amounts of air but they must open and close in sync or you'll run
one side of the engine ahead of the other. The book procedure is not good 
enough in the real world where things like heim joint drag and linkage fles
(oops - flex) come into play. There is a better way and if you're interested
let me know and I'll describe it for ya.

	Fuel PumP Pressure
Don't want to dwell on this but the overpressure caused by the electric pump
you've installed will cause continuous rich running and the engine will
never come on line and run correctly. Regardless of what you've been told
this is the single biggest problem you can have. I've had this problem on 
both air and H2o engines with webbers, dells, you name it. Before I got
smart on the Pantera I put on a Halon system to deal with carb fires that came
from the fuel pump overpressure. You live and occasionally learn! 

While you're at it check the tank outlet screen (depends on yor fuel tank), the
fuel line, fuel filter - any one of these can starve the engine at high RPM.
If the previous owner happened to use silicone sealers on any part of the fuel
system you've found a potential problem.

	Big Venturis
You mentioned that you would have to live with the venturis you currently had.
If I'm not mistaken you can replace the ones you have with a larger size. They
are real simple to exchange, a single nut/large dog point set screw assy loc
ates them in the carb body. They have to be replaced in the correct orientation
as shown in the book.

You might as well rebuild the carbs if you change venturis. The pump/accelera-
tor diaphram often goes south and the small passages in the well gum up. Again
be careful to get the float setting just right. You have to hold the upper
plate with the float sideways so that gravity doesn't compress the spring assy
in the needle valve.

Here's a final trick not recommended for daily drivers. Go up one or two sizes
on the idle jets and redadjust the idle. It won't idle as smoothly and the 
plugs will eventually load up but it will SCREAM off line and mid range. It
just won't stay in tune too long. Of course if you're only trying to out run
the Miata in a drag race that's all you need.

Richard

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 93 07:33:10 PDT
From: lhdsy1!HOVMB.HOU281.CHEVRON.COM!TSJJE2@uunet.UU.NET
Subject: NO SUBJECT

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC Customer IT Strategies Dept., Advisory Services Div.
'
SUBJECT:

FROM: Scott Jessen

Corky Cartwright wrote:

> You can lower the front of a 951S about 1/2" without affecting
> suspension geometry by installing 225/45-16 tires instead of
> 225/50-16's.  I made this change to my 951S and it subjectively improved
> the steering response and cornering behavior of the car.
> I suspect most of the change is due to stiffer sidewalls and better
> tires (Dunlop SP8000's instead of D40/M2's), but the lower ride height
> may help slightly.  Before the change, I had trouble with my car
> understeering at the track; it has the small (16mm) rear stabilizer bar.

> If you want to get really radical you can try 225/40-16's.

Although it is true that your lower sidewall will contribute to better
steering input reaction and reduced deflection under load, the compromise
is that as you go smaller overall diameter you reduce the length (area)
of your tire's contact patch.  This is why larger wheels are typically used
with short sidewall tires (one of the reasons, anyway).

If you are tracking the car, I'd suggest working with the suspension versus
reducing your tire footprint.

By the way, I had one die-hard competitor friend who maintained that the
correct suspension geometry was more a factor than lower center of gravity
in making quick laps.  He finally lowered his car (911), and his lap times
significantly came down as well.

Only if there are major rough sections in a track will I consider raising
the car.......

 -Scott

Date: 30 Jul 1993 08:44:49 -0800
From: "Graphics Group" <Graphics_Group@qmgate.arc.nasa.gov>
Subject: Re:  Re- Fuel and ignition q

 Re> Re: Fuel and ignition questions on 76 911s
>From: tilden@pascal.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)

>Scott et all,
>	The Motronic Air Sensor, according to the Manual, has a voltage output
>	of about .05 Volts at Zero Flow to about 5.6 Volts at full flapper
>	deflection. (From memory). What output can we expect from a
>	hot wire sensor? Anybody tried this?

    I recently borrowed the Bentley book on Bosch FI.  It's a good source for
all kinds of info about general theory & even a little specific info.  The
author
(Probst) says that the MAF (hot-wire) puts out a voltage that is inverse to
the flapper-type.  Although I thout he said that the Flapper puts out a lesser
voltage for a greater displacement...  Could be wrong....
    Myself, I have wondered what it would be like to put a MAF on a 914 1.8L,
turning the L-jetronic into LH-jetronic.  The easy answer is: "not simple".
But it would appear that air mass is really what you want to sense, since the
air:fuel ratio is based on masses, and besides, the flapper takes longer to re-
spond and _can_ (but hasen't yet on mine) be warped by backfires.

My $00.02.
- --Dave D.

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 93 08:58:09 PDT
From: pasha@hal.com (James Pasha)
Subject: Misc. Grumblings


Hi Guys,


So you want to play with fuel injection? Consider this, a better timed
system that has hot wire and the desired crank indexed sensing is made 
General Motors. The best one came on the GMC Scylone and can be mapped
to a 3.6 Porsche engine very easily. 

Here's the trick part, fabrication of the intake and tuning the runner
length. I'd say you'd need a 14 inch length on the intakes and a 35mm
bore on the tubes. the injectors should be place to spray on the back 
side of the intake valves, about 50mm away.

The stock mass air sensor and all of the computer sensors must be used,
this isn't a job for an amateur, but a pro could put a kit together.
Stan??? The computer would need a remap, but there are interfaces to
do PROM development. The basic unit will handle up to 5.0 liters of 
engine capacity.

About ten years ago I adapted CIS to a 914 2.0. I had to make a plenum 
and new runners, but it ran like stink. Much better that the timed 
stuff.

Give me high butterfly mechanical injection on the big motors. Expensive
but simple.

To the gentleman with the 928 blowing white smoke, if your car has two 
valve heads, it won't be a cheap repair for the head gaskets, but much
simpler. If it's a four valve, well, I hope you got it cheap. The motor
has to come out, a 15 hour job to R&R. ( I've done one, so don't give
me some quick and dirty, there isn't one for them).

If you want to get more airflow for your CIS, use the V-8 sensor plate
and the 3.0 Carrera (euro) fuel distributor. Factory Carrera cams work 
fine. Timed fuel injection is a mixed blessing as the comprimises 
included are for regulatory and longevity issues. The single product 
from LUCAS that I would consider are the engine management units used
in F1 and Indy car racing. No single hot wire, but they don't compromise
much. Going fast is expensive, I'd recommend using the best chip UNLESS
your profession is developing one of those systems. GM has about 24
engineers assigned to the Chevrolet engine management systems. That's
a lot of full time, focused talent doing this stuff. I agree with
Mark DeHaas on the level of design talent reqired.

To Pam at Collopy, Try Kahlers in Dublin for your service, They know 
what they're doing. (510) 829-2050

Jim Pasha
pasha@hal.com
Diablo Region PCA

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 93 12:49:46 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: Re:  Electronic ignition systems

> >	The Motronic Air Sensor, according to the Manual, has a voltage output
> >	of about .05 Volts at Zero Flow to about 5.6 Volts at full flapper
> >	deflection. (From memory). What output can we expect from a
> >	hot wire sensor? Anybody tried this?
> (Probst) says that the MAF (hot-wire) puts out a voltage that is inverse to
> the flapper-type.  Although I thought he said that the Flapper puts 
out a lesser
> voltage for a greater displacement...  Could be wrong....

Hi injection-talkers,

Hot-wire systems work on different principles than vane-type systems:

Vane systems use the volume of air entering the engine to mechanically 
move a potentiometer, giving a varying output voltage or current 
directly (depending on how you'd like to interpret things) related to 
the air volume.  This is simple but does not compensate for air density 
(altitude or other barometric changes).

So called hot-wire systems use a current flow to heat a wire which is 
tensioned in the incoming air flow.  Passing air cools the wire and 
changes its resistance, altering the current flow.  Electronics detect 
this and therefore know the *mass* of air (not volume) entering the 
engine.  This technique does compensate for barometric changes and is 
generally more accurate and less prone to mechanical failure.

Hot-wire sensors don't "put out" any voltage.  In fact, they do nothing 
if they aren't heated via sufficient current (like a fuse, sort of).  
The electronics supporting a hot-wire system are a little more 
complicated than mechanical potentiometer systems, but not by much.  
It's the remaining stuff that drives the injectors and controls the 
timing that's a big deal.

Simply changing the metering box from vane to hot-wire will gain a 
measurable but inconsequential reduction in intake flow resistance.  
It's a lot of work for practically no gain, and this is on an 
electronic vane system.  Adapting a K-Jetronic to behave this way would 
be a great academic exercise and would delight a few parts supply 
companies, but likely wouldn't startle you with new found power, IMHO!

		JBS

   (and leaving) and they wait just for us.

border
    there, the border wait is averaging about 2 1/2 hours.  The truck 
crossing a
    bit to the East, (sorry, can't remember the name of the town) is usually 
quicker.

are looking for a track vehicle or fast autocrosser, this is a good one.


About 924, 931, 944, 951 and 968 lowering, no matter what method you 
use, keep the lower arms parallel in respect to the ground and front
crossmember. Any lower will cause some `porpoising' across unevent
pavement in corners. If you lower the body in respect to the length of
the shock absorber, you must also shorten the shaft of the shock to
keep the shaft from ramming the bottom and destroying the adjustment
mechanism. Carrera Shock made me specially shortened ones for my 914/6
to improve suspension travel at a low ride height.

There is nothing funnier than one of these kids in a mini-truck that's 
lowered to leave muffler tracks in the pavement showing a pair of 
front shocks that have bent. I've seen afew out of 914s.

insist on playing dumb by driving in a less than opitimal manner in 
front of a Porsche. Where's the Red Baron when you need him the most???

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 93 14:58:05 CDT
From: cork@cs.rice.edu (Corky Cartwright)
Subject: Tire sizes and footprints 


>Although it is true that your lower sidewall will contribute to better
>steering input reaction and reduced deflection under load, the compromise
>is that as you go smaller overall diameter you reduce the length (area)
>of your tire's contact patch.  This is why larger wheels are typically used
>with short sidewall tires (one of the reasons, anyway).

>If you are tracking the car, I'd suggest working with the suspension versus
>reducing your tire footprint.

At some point, reducing tire diameter is clearly a bad idea, but
I'm not convinced that Porsche equips all of its cars with tires of
optimum size.  For example, in Solo II CS autocrossing, competitive 944's 
use smaller, lower profile tires than the original equipment.  The
change in gearing is helpful, but so is the wider (and shorter)
tire patch.  In many cases, I suspect that Porsche's choice of tires 
is dictated by factors such as body clearance, ride quality, ground 
clearance, gearing, and availability rather than optimum track or
autocross performance.  

In my case, before I switched to lower profile tires in front,
I had to put 10-12 psi more pressure in my front tires
than in my rear tires to balance the handling of my car at the track.  
With the increased pressure, I doubt that 225/50's have a longer footprint
than 225/45's.  

- -- Corky

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 1993 13:35:53 -0700 (PDT)
From: cherie@cirrus.com (Cherie Woodward)
Subject: RE:76 911 Ignition cut-out

I once had a car (not a splendiferous PORSCHE) that would 'turn off' at
fwy speeds. This would happen at any time, but it seemed only on the fwy.
I'd pull over and adjust the points, get in and start the car right up.

After much consternation, I found that when the distributor advanced,
a solder joint would ground against the body. Since this only happened
with the distributor advanced, it was a tricky problem to track down.

So, first check your point gap, as this is most often the culprit, then
look for anomolies such as a faulty coil wire or loose contact point
mount.

Cherie

Date: Fri, 30 Jul 93 14:37 CDT
From: rondl@ihlpe.att.com
Subject: Re: '67 911, now what?


I can relate to your quandry, having just completed a complete
metal restoration (body-wise) on a '66 912 which was stored for
10 years prior to my purchase.  It was a total rust bucket.

I dis-assembled the car and then used paint stripper to remove
all the undercoating/paint/etc.  I also did at least four full
days worth of sand blasting on rust areas.
Then, replaced the floor pans, inner/outer rockers, door jams,
hinge posts, trailing arm support sheet metal, and 25+ patches throughout
the car.  It took me a year to do it.

Depending on the sales/swap meets the sheet metal ran 
about $1000 or so.  Its the LABOR/TOOLS/MATERIALS that 
costs the big bucks.  In Illinois, I found a place that
will make custom sheet metal and its .1 the price of P-car metal.
I just bring in the hold hunk of metal and they make a new one.

Find a local who likes that kind of work - I found a guy who was
willing to do the welding/fitting through a local paint supply shop,
I asked for someone who was good and worked in his garage.
A german car shop will also probably have some names.

I did all the grunt work - stripping, grinding, disassembly, patch
forming, etc. and left the welding and final fitting to him.  This
saved major money.  Expect to pay $2000+ for someone to do it all.
It cost me $1400 for welding/fitting work, after I had already 
stripped and disassembled the car.

Redi-Strip will bead blast the outside of your car for about $500
which saves a huge amount of time.  If they "dip" the car, its
about $1500 and does a even better job, and saves even more time.
I would do this were I to do it again.

Bottom Line,  you have to look at this as a hobby and be willing
to learn a lot, spend a lot on tools, air compressor?, and parts.
If you cant wait 1+ years to just get the body back in shape,
assuming you work 40+ hours per week for a living, then look for a nice
used one.  I get depressed every show/swap meet I go to and
see $7k 912s and $10k 911s that aren't all that bad.  I
easily have $8k++ in mine, I paid $2.5k for it and the rest is
parts/labor - I still am assembling it, and it aint even painted yet.
I wont even mention the interior.....

At this point, I quit counting it all up.  I justify it by telling
myself its a hobby, I enjoy working on it, and where else could
I find a perfect example of a vintage sports car for $12k (my current
estimate of final investment).  I like my 912 a whole lot more
than if I had bought some goofy mazda or corvette (no offense to
mazdo and 'vette owners).  My $944 friend has out-spent me on
parts/labor in the last year, so hook up with someone who
spends more than you do (no offense to $944 owners), we use
the '$' as a joke when we talk/email about our cars.

Good luck, and dont get depressed.  I'm sure I am not the only
one out there who has more $$ in their restoration than the
car is worth.  You have to include the non-tangibles, and then
its worth while.

Ron
